        How to view it
            An extremely useful but deceptively difficult thing.
        Reducible
            All reasoning can be reduced to [deduction (broad)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=-n8BacOsj9yIbpLxsbz4df8M)
                Probabilistic/non-probabilistic dichotomy argument
                    P1. All non-probabilistic reasoning is a form of deduction. | P → Q (p)
                        P1. All non-probabilistic reasoning can be reduced to [standard deduction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ENg9uidRSS0t3eQBEFX4pGmm)
                        P2. Standard deduction is a subset of deduction (broad).
                    P2. All probabilistic reasoning [can be reduced to deduction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=shgVf32T8JinVWc0u-xf8ZtY) | R → Q (p)
                    P3. All reasoning must be probabilistic or non-probabilistic. | P v R (p)
                        The [negation](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Fn48qj62GIJ1QrISLDjPhqnc) of "probabilistic" is "non-probabilistic".
                        [Excluded Middle](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=50yUI8YjZZnsIqPMR80s9hep)
                    C. All reasoning must be a form of deduction. | Q (P1,P2,P3 [vE](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=vXHh-RchhugkRBUD155IgO4F))
                Simplicity argument
                    It's simpler to suppose that all reasoning derives from only a small subset of rules, which deduction seems to describe and allow for.
                Via logic argument
                    P1. All reasoning [can be reduced to logic](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=AbNaVbQdlvY2B3giwwf9Ej7N)
                    P2. Deduction (broad) is entirely describable with logic.
                    C. Therefore all reasoning can be reduced to deduction (broad). (P1,P2)
            All reasoning can be reduced to logic
                <Argument about how any reasoning not being reducible to logic would cause a contradiction given the recommended axioms.>
        Axioms
            Definition
                Foundational beliefs which lack epistemic justification or are justified [circularly](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ll7iV-XLg_ynG7p4yWT34KAu).
                    **Foundational:**   Plays a significant role in the relevant epistemology(s).
                **Usually:**   Justifies a ton of other beliefs (often indirectly).
                **Usually:**   Commonly held.
                **Examples:**   [Specific axioms](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=DUl4hPw0aFpUvSp0yEx5SgPT)
            Importance
                Knowing axioms substantially helps with reasoning
                    Understanding axioms allows one to shift to slower but **more reliable reasoning**.
                        Understanding axioms and how they build up to higher levels of reasoning allows us to use a lower level of reasoning when a higher one is brought into question.
                        Likelihood
                            Lower/higher level definition
                            Move all of this to definitions section.
                                Lower/higher level of abstraction from pure logic.
                                ^ **Low-level reasoning** is reasoning done close to the level of pure logic.
                                    **Example:**   Symbolic logic
                                    Create and link section
                                    ^ **Definition:**   Symbolic logic is a branch of formal logic that uses mathematical symbols, variables, and operators to represent logical expressions and relationships explicitly.
                                ^ **High-level reasoning** is reasoning that's highly abstracted/emergent from the level of pure logic.
                                    It's entirely reducible to pure logic.
                                    It has many short-cuts and functions that make it far easier to work with for everyday matters where precision is not super important.
                                    It tends to be far more ambiguous than low-level reasoning.
                                        <Consequences of misunderstandings>
                                ^ **Pure logic** is ...
                            This allows for more resolutions of disagreements.
                                Cases where people agree (at least technically) about the relevant axioms
                                    Understanding axioms and how they build up to higher levels of reasoning frequently allows for being able to systematically reason through a disagreement with someone when it would not otherwise be possible. So long as all the most impactful information can be shared.
                                        [Information exchange limit](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uEaZcmGBfWucfZDX4My1zNhq)
                                        Likelihood
                                            We can't exchange information with other people efficiently enough to reason through a disagreement, even when understanding and agreeing on axioms.
                                            Relevance
                                        The relevant axioms are generally not agreed upon.
                                        Likelihood
                                            **Example:**   [Cases where people consciously disagree (at least technically) about the relevant axioms](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=aRml1nvvK4ApEDb2GXoGMYIZ)
                                Cases where people consciously disagree (at least technically) about the relevant axioms
                                    Often when people consciously disagree about axioms, they still subconsciously disagree. So it is still theoretically possible to resolve the disagreement by showing them how they subconsciously rely on the same axioms.
                                        This is usually infeasible due to how common a high level of [close-mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=TBDKCqfm_SEQfc4szPkt4Aqr) is.
                                    Being able to use a lower level of reasoning can often be very helpful even in cases where people even subconsciously disagree on the axioms.
                                        Even if they don't agree on the specific axioms, they probably agree on most if not all of the relevant principles built not more then a few levels above those axioms, and those principles are part of how axioms build up to typical reasoning.
                                            [This applies even when the lower level(s) of reasoning are not axiomatic.](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ZMSRvi3uMATIOcUXniVzUp37)
                            This applies even when the lower level(s) of reasoning are above axioms.
                                Understanding axioms gives us a better intuition for how reasoning builds up in general, even at a high-level.
                                I'm a little skeptical of this, unsure.
                        This applies even when the supposed axioms in question are not the axioms one really accepts.
                        Likelihood
                            Even if these axioms are not accepted, the ones that are accepted will probably be de-facto accepted in such a way that they support all the main common reasoning principles in deduction and induction (and in turn virtually everything else).
                            Likelihood
                                We typically choose/modify our axioms with the intent of keeping our reasoning principles in-tact.
                                Likelihood
                                ^ Our fundamental reasoning principles are almost always essentially equivalent or reducible to the fundamental reasoning principles in deduction and induction.
                                Likelihood | Link to a "common sense" section or something.
                    Axioms allow us to better avoid overly [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                        Understanding axioms and how they build up to higher-level reasoning allows us to default to a deeper level of reasoning when there is sufficient evidence of our [heuristics](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=49rqRClTn-Vx2v-ZXXm90ukK) being insufficiently reliable for the relevant case(s).
                        Likelihood
                        ^ Unless we accept [circular reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ikMYp91GwlvLX0gZwMfCovX_), axiom(s) must underlie all reasoning.
                        Likelihood
                            [Gödel's incompleteness theorems](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=LUzSkKXZ4EfIFAKz5YFjBroE)
                            Likelihood
                            If axiom(s) must underlie all reasoning, then understanding them and how they build up to higher-level reasoning would necessarily give us a lower/deeper level to default to.
                            Relevance
                **Common:**   With the rare exceptionof those who accept [non-axiomatic circular reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0DXdYOjFnNHS8xtYxVsGa0Ro), everyone holds some axiom(s).
                    [Unless we accept circular reasoning, axiom(s) must underlie all reasoning.](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=-jvgPdcXr3P7QdiziXf6BMOQ)
                **Knowing axioms ≠ reasonable:**   Even if you understand axioms very well, it is still very possible to be very unreasonable.
                    For example via [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                    Likelihood
                        Eg. If one uses very simplistic heuristics to determine when they should stop using said heuristics, and the heuristics promote using heuristics in virtually all cases, it may be infeasible to get them to think at a lower level to properly check their reasoning.
                    For example via [close-minded close-mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=g6oCGh3-jkvcVnp1kIMGHAZ7)
                    Likelihood
                        If one is too overconfident, they may consider it a waste of time to check their reasoning at a lower level, resulting in it never (or very rarely) being checked, preventing them from improving even if they have a good understanding of axioms.
            Specific axioms
                The laws of thought
                    Definition
                        The set of three laws: identity, contradiction and excluded middle.
                        Synonym
                            Typically synonymous with the "laws of logic".
                    Importance
                        Laws that are generally regarded as underlying all reasoning.
                    Identity
                        Definition
                            All [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ewojo1Kk7SCQ_tTLnAMkJeio) are equivalent to themselves.
                        Importance
                            Common
                                Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.
                        [Intuition pull](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6Efrp6c3GsagWNAxFomdfD8A)
                            All reasoning relies on propositions being equal to themselves, otherwise they could be different from themselves at any point.
                            Anti-circularity specific
                                Any attempt to justify the law of identity must implicitly rely on its propositions being equal to themselves, thus dooming the attempts to be circular at best.
                    Contradiction
                        Definition
                            No [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ewojo1Kk7SCQ_tTLnAMkJeio) can be both true and untrue.
                        Importance
                            Common
                                Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.
                        [Intuition pull](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6Efrp6c3GsagWNAxFomdfD8A)
                            The principle of explosion
                                Definition
                                    The law according to which any statement can be proven from a contradiction.
                                    The implication is typically that accepting any contradiction is absurd.
                                    [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principle_of_explosion)
                                Importance
                                    This principle doesn't apply to [paraconsistent logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paraconsistent_logic)
                                        This guide is based on [classical logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_logic), not paraconsistent logic.
                            Anti-circularity specific
                                Any attempt to justify the law of contradiction must implicitly rely on its propositions not entailing any contradictions, thus dooming the attempts to be circular at best.
                    Excluded middle
                        Definition
                            All [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ewojo1Kk7SCQ_tTLnAMkJeio) must either be true or false.
                        Importance
                            Common
                                Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.
                        [Intuition pull](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6Efrp6c3GsagWNAxFomdfD8A)
                            Given the other laws of thought and modus ponens, it appears to be possible to derive the law of excluded middle and unnecessary to accept it as an axiom.
                            Link or move proof from elsewhere
                            However if we do not accept one of: [[identity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QNeZxWHbe7v-UYKBxTdg2LFx), [contradiction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=f0OtGrGk9iM-Ry2mQ04gpGKG), [modus ponens works](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=k3emDCQzI6x381obzDk2UWqL)], then we can either accept it as an axiom, or reject it and potentially face some issues.
                            ^ Without the law of excluded middle, we need a method to separate which [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ewojo1Kk7SCQ_tTLnAMkJeio) can be neither true nor false, and which can't.
                                If we don't have this method, then:
                                For any well-defined proposition, we could say "how do we know that this is true or false? It could be neither."
                                This would allow all well-defined propositions to escape the perview of reasoning, leaving us with no means to reason about anything.
                            However if we come up with a method, then it seems like we have other issues.
                                It seems unclear how to justify such a method without using itself (because of the aforementioned problem of any [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ewojo1Kk7SCQ_tTLnAMkJeio) possibly being neither true nor false, including the method), so it seems like we must use [circular reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ikMYp91GwlvLX0gZwMfCovX_) to justify it.
                                However circular reasoning tends to pose issues.
                                Read the circular reasoning (circularity) section for more details. Just click the "circular reasoning" link above to visit it.
                        Alternative
                            Negation as failure
                                Definition
                                    Synonym: NAF
                Modus ponens works
                Likely replace this
                    Definition
                        If [if x then y] and x, then y.
                    Importance
                        Common
                            Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.
                    [Intuition pull](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6Efrp6c3GsagWNAxFomdfD8A)
                        Without accepting that modus ponens works (or something similar) as an axiom, it seems we are unable to engage in logical inference.
                            If only the other axioms listed here are presupposed, then it seems like we are unable to justify modus ponens without using modus ponens.
                                Using modus ponens to justify modus ponens would involve [circular reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ikMYp91GwlvLX0gZwMfCovX_).
                                    If we accept a type of logic that allows for circular reasoning, it is [unclear](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Y3lxgSPd1iSGFrv4gcsKYHsK) why it should be allowed in this case but not in other cases that one would (ostensibly) not accept.
                    Isn't modus ponens an [inference rule](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=MtLuLLSMtth4OOhFXZZJyyKb) rather than an axiom?
                        Modus ponens is an inference rule, however the claim that it works is a proposition that can be treated largely like any other, including being considered an axiom.
                Principle of induction
                    Definition
                        The principle that a higher number of cases of association will increase the probability of a fresh association.
                        Clarify to what degree the probability increases and what exactly this means.
                        Examples
                        Provide non-event based examples if applicable.
                            We experience the sun rising many times, therefore we are more likely to experience the sun rising again then we would otherwise be.
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_thought#The_Problems_of_Philosophy_(1912))
                    Importance
                    Supports [probabilistic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=i_iQ7vvMYVnf1xdY5Ymr8--m)
                    [Intuition pull](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6Efrp6c3GsagWNAxFomdfD8A)
                        Anti-circularity specific
                            The problem of induction
                                Definition
                                    The problem that there is no non-circular way to justify inductive [inferences](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ua8jzU8x2DrWQ46H4Rz65bfj)
                                [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Problem_of_induction)
                    Alternative
                        Sometimes people presuppose the principle of uniformity and derive the principle of induction, rather than the other way around.
                Semi-reliability of mental faculties
                    Definition (specific)
                        Your own conscious reasoning mental faculties are semi-reliable.
                        Distinction
                            Different from the idea that everyone's conscious reasoning mental faculties are semi-reliable, this is just about your own conscious reasoning mental faculties.
                    Importance
                        **Common:**   Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.
                        **Reasoning specific:**   Non-reasoning mental faculties can still be less than semi-reliable.
                            **Example:**   [System 1 thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ya-MUAgoHP7_PEgC9ypSAe9I)
                            Likelihood
                        **Limited reliability:**   Reasoning mental faculties can still be unreliable to a substantial extent.
                            We can still often be wrong for reasons we never considered.
                            Likelihood
                    [Intuition pull](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6Efrp6c3GsagWNAxFomdfD8A)
                        Any attempt to justify the semi-reliability of our mental faculties must implicitly rely on our reasoning being semi-reliable, which implicitly relies on the semi-reliability of the mechanism(s) responsible for that reasoning, which are by definition our mental faculties. Thus dooming the attempts to be circular at best.
                        ^ Thus we would have to use [circular reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ikMYp91GwlvLX0gZwMfCovX_) to justify this, and circular reasoning tends to lead to issues.
                        See linked section for details.
                        Anti-circularity specific
                Cogito
                Needs a lot more work.
                    Definitions
                        There are multiple subtly different variations/interpretations of the cogito, but they're all roughly based on the idea of "I think, therefore I am".
                        Synonyms
                            Cogito, ergo sum
                            I think, therefore I am
                    Importance
                        Common
                            One of the most common beliefs considered to be at the foundation of one's epistemology.
                        Impactful
                            At least in [argument form](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=SzQXXOBPHpRDjzpcv7Lcl66B) (as it is often understood), [it appears to be misleading about how axioms work](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=gQgtUg9J0XHH3ajcYY8h49vq).
                    Full argument version
                        Definition
                            The axiom that the argument: "I think, therefore I am" is sound.
                            "I think" is a premise, "I am" is the conclusion.
                        Related
                            [Just-conclusion version of the cogito](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ZQ-Su6jH0Wl9HVLphQE3nZZQ)
                    Just-conclusion version
                        Definition
                            The axiom that the argument: "I think, therefore I am" has a true conclusion.
                            "I think" is a premise, "I am" is the conclusion.
                        Importance
                            Impactful
                                This seems to be misleading about how axioms work.
                                    The argument really appears to be based on premises that are not normally deep down accepted as axioms.
                        Possible implied premises
                            If something thinks, then it must exist.
                                Why?
                                ^ It is self-evident.
                                Create a definition for "self-evident" and link it here.
                                    As intuitive as it seems, it doesn't seem like it's truly self-evident.
                                    For example the statement below has the same structure:
                                    "If x does not exist, then x must exist."
                                    And yet would not generally be considered true for obvious reasons.
                                    So why is "if something thinks, then it must exist" true, when the above example is not?
                                    It seems like there must be __some__ kind of answer here, and that this answer would justify the "if thinks then exists" statement.
                                    If this is the case, it's difficult to see how it's **self**-evident, seeing as its evident-ness comes from something outside of itself.
                            [Modus ponens works](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=k3emDCQzI6x381obzDk2UWqL)
            Axiom systems
                Definition
                    Collections of axioms.
                How to select an axiom system
                    Typically, people select an axiom system based on the following principles:
                    Minimal axioms
                        No unnecessary axioms if it can be helped.
                        Why isn't this an axiom?
                            Definition
                                Why don't we treat this essentially like an axiom?
                                Eg. "If it can be justified, then it is not necessarily true".
                    Matches irreducible intuitions
                        Definition
                            The axiomatic system appears to match our most fundamental reasoning intuitions.
                            Fundamental reasoning intuitions (definition)
                                What we would refuse to stop believing regardless of the evidence presented to us.
                                At least unless that evidence convinced us for non-evidential reasons, such as causing an emotional reaction that irrationally changes our mind. This doesn't account for that.
                            Example
                        Why accept this?
                            It doesn't seem justified to trust our intuitions (even our most irreducible ones).
                                We should likely not trust our intuitive distrust of this principle.
                                ^ To be intuitively opposed to trusting our most irreducible intuitions is likely essentially equivalent to being intuitively opposed to a stronger intuition.
                                    P1. These intuition of distrust must (to the extent that it is justified by any sort of reasoning) be based on some kind of reasoning that is ultimately justified by our axioms.
                                    P2. Our axioms are in turn likely partially derived from our irreducible intuitions.
                            Essentially all reasoning necessarily relies on irreducible intuitions.
                            Justify
                        Why isn't this an axiom?
                            Definition
                                Why don't we treat this essentially like an axiom?
                                Eg. "What matches our irreducible intuitions must necessarily be true".
                            Our temptation to make this an axiom appears to come from our subconscious desire to go "my axioms must match my most irreducible intuitions, therefore axioms like the [law of contradiction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=f0OtGrGk9iM-Ry2mQ04gpGKG) must necessarily be true."
                            
                "Recommended" system
                Likely unfinished and somewhat wrong.
                    [Law of identity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QNeZxWHbe7v-UYKBxTdg2LFx)

                    Immediate derivations
            The causal source of accepting axioms
                Definition
                    The causal reason for us accepting our axioms.
                    Distinction
                Importance
                Coincidentally accurate axioms
                    Definition
                        The position that we coincidentally don't hold any wrong axioms.
                    Importance
                        Accuracy
                            In order to better survive and reproduce, evolution would have been best off selecting for the (at least deep down) acceptance of axioms that match reality.
                                This feels like circular reasoning.
                                Addressed
                                    It feels circular because of using axioms to investigate the causal source of axioms.
                                    To be a form of circular reasoning, it would have to be using axioms to justify axioms, or using axioms' causal source to investigate their causal source. Neither is being done here.
                                There could be axioms that were not relevant to our survival/reproduction.
                                Addressed
                                    Simpler/broader axioms would likely apply everywhere in the universe in substantial way(s).
                                        Occam's razor supports the idea that all axioms are simple/broad.
                Transcendental-esque access
                    Definition
                        Some sort of direct, implicit, perfect access to truth.
                        Not indirect/imperfect and probably not coincidental.
                    Importance
                        Common
                            Accepting something like this in terms of trusting strong enough intuitions is pretty common.
                        Impactful
                            
                        Accuracy
            Accepting axioms with uncertainty
                Definition
                    Accepting axioms in a manner that is uncertain.
                    Distinction
                        This is different from considering __our conception__ of what axioms we hold to be uncertain.
                        ^ It's possible to think that we could be wrong about what axioms form the foundation of our reasoning, while thinking that the axioms themselves cannot be wrong.
                Importance
                    **Common:**   For people to attempt to accept axioms in an uncertain way.
                    **Inconsistent**   It isn't possible for us to be consistent in believing that we hold axiom X while also believing that axiom X is uncertain.
                        P1. Uncertainty about an axiom requires the possibility of affecting some currently present supporting evidence of that axiom.
                            P1. Uncertainty requires the possibility of being wrong.
                                In order to be uncertain about an axiom, we must consider it possible for that axiom to be wrong.
                                **Example:**   If there's an 80% chance of being right, then there's necessarily a 20% chance of being wrong.
                                Link relevant induction section about probabilities adding up to 100%.
                            P2. The possibility of an axiom being wrong requires the possibility of affecting some currently present supporting evidence of that axiom.
                                P1. The possibility of an axiom being wrong requires the possibility of some evidence existing which indicates that the axiom is wrong.
                                    **Distinction:**   This evidence/indication does not necessarily have to be practically or even theoretically accessible.
                                    In order for a well-defined proposition to be wrong, there must be some logic that makes it wrong.
                                    Likelihood
                                P2. In order for some evidence to indicate that an axiom is wrong, the evidence must influence some information we previously had that was supporting the axiom.
                                    This is because counter-evidence always functions via deductive or inductive reasoning, which both always have this property.
                                    Likelihood
                                        Example
                        P2. It isn't possible to affect some currently present supporting evidence of an axiom.
                            Axioms are (by this guide's definition) necessarily unsupported.
                        Accepting axioms with uncertainty frequently results in confusion and mistakes, especially in important areas.
                        Relevance
                            **Example:**   When feeling cornered with logic about religion or lackthereof, people will often resort to challenging their own implicitly accepted axioms with "we can't be sure about that".
            The ways in which these can be axioms
                All of these can be assumed as applying in all cases or in at least most cases, or in at least most cases that have been observed, etc.
        Standard deduction
            Definitions
                **Deductive reasoning** is the process of drawing valid [inferences](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ua8jzU8x2DrWQ46H4Rz65bfj)
                    Example
                        The inference from the premises "all men are mortal" and "Socrates is a man" to the conclusion "Socrates is mortal" is deductively valid.
                Alternative definition
                    One approach defines deduction in terms of the intentions of the author: they have to intend for the premises to offer deductive support to the conclusion.
                        With the help of this modification, it is possible to distinguish [valid](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UUAfcY2vsb-7FyMTvOmfclqa) from invalid deductive reasoning: it is invalid if the author's belief about the deductive support is false, but even invalid deductive reasoning is a form of deductive reasoning.
            Importance
                Common
                    Most non-trivial reasoning sessions involve some standard deduction.
                    Likelihood
                **Systematic reasoning:**   Makes it much easier to use more systematic reasoning.
                    Better understanding the processes underlying deduction allows for more [systematic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7)
                    Likelihood
                        [It's easier](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=p9E_cUv003cdY4Ew8l0BE60q) to create very systematic deductive arguments than probabilistic ones.
                        Likelihood
                    It's easier to create very systematic deductive arguments than probabilistic ones.
                    Likelihood
                which allows for better decision-making during [big decisions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=3IMrmLH-UeufI_a8aVlL492y)
            Limitations
                It's very easy to error in a very impactful way when using deductive reasoning.
                    There are a lot of common and easy-to-make mistakes and fallacies surrounding standard deduction.
                    **Analogy:**   Just as it's easy for one small mistake in a mathematical solution to ruin the entire process.
                There are more specific limitations listed in other "limitations" items in this section.
            How to view it
                Standard deductive reasoning (when applicable and properly used, which like most reasoning is often difficult) is extremely strong and reliable, so where available, it tends to be preferable to using just inductive reasoning.
                However combining both inductive and standard deductive reasoning can be even better in many situations.
            Structure of deduction
                Definition
                    The standard deduction topics that have to do with **logical structure**.
                Importance
                    Understanding and following structured deductive reasoning helps reason in a more [systematic](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7) way.
                        Unstructured reasoning typically leads to lots of/major blunders and mistakes.
                            Examples
                Propositions
                Needs much more work.
                    Definitions
                        Propositions are typically characterized in terms of three interlocking roles:
                        Meanings of [declarative sentences](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NDUzShypnzTdPkkQ1BHxzg6y)
                            Definition
                                The ideas or semantic contents expressed by declarative sentences.
                                The information content of sentences and cross-linguistic sameness.
                                Distinction
                                    Propositions are not identical to individual sentences and do not belong to any particular language.
                        Contents or objects of propositional attitudes
                            Distinction
                                Propositions are not identical to individual beliefs or desires since the same proposition can underlie many individual mental states.
                            Examples
                                Such as when someone believes that the sky is blue.
                                If Leila believes that the train will be delayed, then she has a mental state, called a propositional attitude, directed at the proposition that the train will be delayed. 
                            Propositional attitudes
                                Types
                                    Beliefs
                                    Desires
                                    Hopes
                                    Fears
                                Linking psychological attitudes
                                    Propositional attitudes are typically expressed through that-clauses to link a psychological attitude to a proposition.
                                        "She believes that it will rain."
                                The contents of propositional attitudes are shareable.
                                    Different persons can have the same beliefs or fears.
                                    Both Leila and Diego fear that the train will be delayed.
                        Bearers of truth values
                            Each proposition is a function which takes a claim and returns either true or false.
                            Primary truth-bearers
                                Non-proposition truth-bearers are true or false in a derivative sense by being about true or false propositions.
                                    Declarative sentences
                                    Beliefs
                                Propositions are not the only entities that have truth values.
                                    Examples
                                        Declarative sentences
                                        Beliefs
                            Bearers of modal properties
                                A proposition can be possible, impossible, or necessary, depending on whether it is logically compatible with coherent scenarios, or in some sense conceivable or contradictory.
                                [Modal types](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=c1IO0xgn3hhWQrXgqmoHrfRB)
                            [True vs false](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XMeFJ4S0hJUAe_TnOV3e7lG_)
                        Synonyms
                            Statement
                                Sometimes used synonymously.
                                However, the word "statement" is ambiguous since it can also refer to [declarative sentences](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NDUzShypnzTdPkkQ1BHxzg6y) themselves rather than their meanings.
                            Judgement
                                The term proposition overlaps with the term judgment, with one difference being that judgments are more closely associated with mental processes that affirm or deny the truth of a content.
                        Sub-definitions
                            Non-mental/abstract entities
                            General mental entities
                        Distinction
                            Unlike sentences, propositions are not linguistic expressions.
                                The English sentence "Snow is white" and the German "Schnee ist weiß" denote the same proposition.
                        Formal definition
                            Formal propositions are often modeled as functions which map a possible world to a truth value.
                                The proposition that the sky is blue can be modeled as a function which would return the truth value T if given the actual world as input, but would return F if given some alternate world where the sky is green.
                            Alternative definitions
                                Structured propositions view
                        Further clarification
                            Propositions are the objects denoted by declarative sentences.
                                "The sky is blue" expresses the proposition that the sky is blue.
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proposition)
                    Types
                        True vs false
                            The truth value of a proposition depends on its accuracy / correspondence with reality.
                            True
                                A proposition which is in accordance with reality.
                            False
                                A proposition which is not in accordance with reality.
                        Compound propositions
                            Definition
                            [Conditional propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5ScB8HIv-Th7cwN1Rh_C7pZh)
                                Conditional propositions are compound propositions since they have components that are themselves propositions.
                            Conjunctive propositions
                                Conjunctive propositions claim that all their component statements are true, typically expressed as an "and" sentence, such as "the tree is green and the sky is blue".
                            Disjunctive propositions
                                Definition
                                    Propositions which assert that one of their component statements is true, typically expressed as an "or" sentence, as in "it is windy or it is rainy".
                                Inclusive disjunctive propositions
                                    At least one but possibly both component statements are true.
                                Exclusive disjunctive propositions
                                    Exactly one component statement is true and the other is false.
                        Analytic vs synthetic
                            The difference depends on why they are true.
                            Analytic
                                The truth of analytic propositions is determined only by the meanings of concepts, independent of the actual state of the world.
                                Example
                                    The proposition "all bachelors are unmarried" is analytically true because the concept "bachelor" already includes the meaning of "unmarried".
                            Synthetic
                                The truth of synthetic propositions, such as "snow is white", depends on the state of the world.
                        A priori vs a posteriori
                            The difference depends on the source of their knowledge.
                            A priori
                                Propositions which can be known through pure reasoning alone.
                                Example
                                    "2+2=4"
                            A posteriori
                                Propositions which describe empirical facts knowable through sensory experience.
                                Example
                                    "The sun is shining."
                        Modal types
                            Definition
                                Modal propositions express what is possible, necessary, or impossible.
                                Examples
                                    "It is possible that I will win the lottery."
                                    "It is impossible to travel faster than light".
                                Distinction
                                    Rather than asserting how the world is, they describe how it could or could not have been.
                            A proposition is necessarily true if it is impossible that it is false. 
                            Alethic modality
                                Concerns what is possible or necessary relative to the laws of nature, metaphysics, or logic.
                            Epistemic modality
                                Concerns what may or must be the case relative to someone's knowledge or evidence.
                                Example
                                    "The butler cannot be the killer."
                            Normative propositions express what ought to be the case, like "you should not drink and drive". They include permissions, requirements, and prohibitions. Moral propositions are normative propositions that assert moral principles or judgments, such as "you should keep promises". Normative propositions contrast with descriptive propositions, which express what is rather than what ought to be. The schools of cognitivism and non-cognitivism debate the existence of normative propositions. Non-cognitivism argues that normative sentences are neither true nor false and do not express propositions, for example, because they convey emotions rather than propositions. A gappy proposition, also called an incomplete or unfilled proposition, is a statement whose subject matter is not properly specified, which results in an incomplete meaning. This can happen when the proposition involves an empty name, which does not refer to any real entity, such as the name Pegasus. Given the difficulties in assigning truth values to gappy propositions, philosophers debate whether they qualify as propositions in the strict sense. Alternative proposals suggest that they are another type of meaning content. Temporal propositions, another type, are statements that refer to specific times, such as "the Berlin Wall fell in 1989". Propositions are also classified by the domain or field of inquiry to which they belong, such as mathematical, scientific, metaphysical, and theological propositions.
                                
                        Categorical vs conditional
                            Categorical propositions
                                Definition
                                    Categorical propositions assert how things are, independently of other statements or assumptions.
                            Conditional propositions
                                Definition
                                    Conditional propositions link two simpler propositions, typically expressed as an "if-then" sentence.
                                    Synonym
                                        Hypothetical proposition
                                They hold that if the if-statement is true, then the then-statement must be true.
                                    If it rains, then the ground gets wet.
                        Typically linguistic
                        Check if the other types belong here.
                            General vs singular
                                How to view it
                                    Usually a purely linguistic distinction.
                                General
                                    General propositions are about a general set of entities.
                                    Universal vs existential
                                        Universal
                                            Definition
                                                Propositions which assert that something is the case for all entities in a domain.
                                                Example
                                                    "All humans are mortal."
                                        Existential
                                            Definition
                                                Propositions which state that something is the case for at least one entity in a domain.
                                                Example
                                                    "Some humans are left-handed."
                                Singular
                                    Singular propositions are about one specific entity.
                                    Example
                                        "Socrates is wise."
                            Affirmative vs negative
                                How to view it
                                    Often a purely linguistic distinction.
                                        Usually, the same information can be expressed with and without negations, as in "he is not happy" and "he is sad".
                                Affirmative
                                    Affirmative propositions state that something is the case.
                                    Example
                                        The tree is green.
                                Negative
                                    Negative propositions deny that something is the case.
                                    Example
                                        The tree is not green.
                                If an affirmative proposition is true, then the corresponding negative proposition is false, and vice versa.
                        Misc
                            Fictional
                                Definition
                                    Propositions about non-existing entities.
                                    Example
                                        "Santa Claus has a beard."
                    Well-defined propositions
                    Reconcile with [Well-defined proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk)
                        Definition
                            Propositions that are defined in a perfectly unambiguous way.
                                Unambiguous
                                Infeasibility
                                    In reality this is typically infeasible to achieve, but we can get close enough by minimizing the probability of impactful ambiguities from arising.
                                        [Clear semantics](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=12AbgdKnf7gB2sw5V6F_Kh-6)
                Antecedent and consequent
                    Definitions
                        Antecedent
                            The first half of a [conditional proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5ScB8HIv-Th7cwN1Rh_C7pZh), whenever the if-clause precedes the then-clause.
                            Synonyms
                                Condition
                                    When the antecedent is used for a [IF-THEN (material conditional)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uzxNZgNX8Jlzb09GO2ZXwiQw)
                                Protasis
                                    Used synonymously in some contexts.
                        Consequent
                            the second half of a [conditional proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5ScB8HIv-Th7cwN1Rh_C7pZh). In the standard form of such a proposition, it is the part that follows "then".
                            Synonyms
                                Consequence
                    Related
                        [IF-THEN (material conditional)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uzxNZgNX8Jlzb09GO2ZXwiQw)
                            The only kind of [conditional proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5ScB8HIv-Th7cwN1Rh_C7pZh) where the sides cannot be swapped.
