                    Definitions
                        Premise
                        Conclusion
                Logical connectives
                    Basic connectives
                        NOT (negation)
                            Definition
                                Outputs the logical negation of the input.
                                Example
                                    I am human. -> I am not Human.
                                Symbols
                                    ¬
                                    -
                            Double negation
                                Definition
                                    "The tree is not not green."
                                A proposition with a double negation is logically equivalent to a proposition without either negation.
                            Understanding NOT in the context of contradictions.
                            What was this supposed to mean?
                        AND (conjunction)
                            Definition
                                Symbols
                                    ∧
                                    ^
                        OR (disjunction)
                            Definition
                                Symbols
                                Distinction
                                    More specific than [the colloquial definition of or](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=8JeXSZBuV_YRumG3FPV1y1id), in that it never means https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=n7OMAZ2eLgm2YIZTBh-L2db8
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boolean_algebra#Basic_operations)
                    Secondary connectives
                        IF-THEN (material conditional)
                            Definition
                                Synonym:   conditional.
                                [Antecedent](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=A1OY9ItQTQcgXLrjT6B9w-kv)
                                [Consequent](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Dr8LcqFWIIfqiyEAzLoy4d8P)
                                Symbols
                                    →
                                    ->
                                    =>
                                Distinction
                                    Different from [colloquial if-then](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=2TuOKskT9XSu0CoJqiPtqE29), in that there is no requirement for a causal or epistemic correlation between the antecedent and consequent.
                            Importance
                                Impactful
                                    Unintuitive
                                        [Non-colloquial](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=tMrZGIpiDGQKx4L5feB1o2hl)
                                    Very useful in logic in general
                                Common
                            Vacuous truth
                                Definition
                                    A true proposition that has the form "if P then Q" with a false antecedent.
                                        The antecedent is P in this case.
                                    https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vacuous_truth
                                Importance
                                    Impactful
                                        Unintuitive
                                            https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paradoxes_of_material_implication
                                    Common
                                The only way a vacuous truth can be false is if the antecedent is true and the consequent is false.
                                    But what if the consequent would be false if the antecedent was true, but the antecedent is false for now? It feels like the overall statement should be false, since colloquially "if P then Q" is false in this case.
                        IF-AND-ONLY-IF (material biconditional)
                            Definition
                                It can be understood as literally "if and only if", or "sufficient and necessary". But a more colloquial definition might simply treat it as synonymous with "equals". If the [antecedent](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=A1OY9ItQTQcgXLrjT6B9w-kv)'s truth value is equal to the [consequent](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Dr8LcqFWIIfqiyEAzLoy4d8P), then this connective 'returns' true (i.e. the whole statement is true). Otherwise, if the antecedent and consequent have different truth values, then it returns false.
                                **Why "if and only if"?**
                                    A nice intuitive way to think of this is to split a biconditional into "if" and "only if".
                                    "If" (in this context) just describes an [ordinary conditional](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uzxNZgNX8Jlzb09GO2ZXwiQw), so you can think of this part as describing the right side of the double-headed arrow ↔.
                                    "Only if" (in this context) means that the only way for the consequent to be true is if the antecedent is true. This implies that if the consequent is true, the antecedent must be true, so you can think of this part as also describing an [ordinary conditional](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uzxNZgNX8Jlzb09GO2ZXwiQw), but in the other direction.
                                    **Unimportant note:**   Technically it seems we could also derive "if" from "only if" as well, so the entire name only needs to be "only if", the "if and" is kinda redundant. But I think describing "if" and "only if" this way is still a nice way to intuit and memorize what "if and only if" means.
                                Synonym:   biconditional.
                                Symbols
                                    ↔
                                    <->
                                    <=>
                            Related
                                [Informal representation of (bi)-conditionals](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=2TuOKskT9XSu0CoJqiPtqE29)
                        XOR
                        https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boolean_algebra#Secondary_operations
                Validity and soundness
                    Validity
                        Definition
                            **Validity** (in this context) describes whether or not it is possible for an argument's conclusion to be false while its premises are true. If an [argument](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=M3cViLMa_VnUyP-7Xp01TfFR) is valid and has only true premises, that means that the conclusion *must* be true, it is logically proven.
                            Example
                                Horses and zebras 
                                    Argument
                                        Horses exist
                                        Therefore zebras exist
                                    Why it's invalid
                                        It's true that horses exist (the premise is sound).
                                        It's true that zebras exist (the conclusion is true).
                                        But it isn't logically impossible for horses to exist without zebras existing (the conclusion doesn't logically follow from the premise, so the argument is still invalid).
                        Importance
                            Very important for good reasoning
                                **Lacks deductive justification:**   An invalid argument cannot deductively justify its conclusion.
                                    **Often relevant:**   Deductive arguments [are common](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=t4q5dF-TXwNiSrdGj3N4glkf)
                                    Relevance
                                    When relevant, very relevant.
                                    Relevance
                                        Unlike in a probabilistic argument, in deduction, one tiny mistake about validity typically makes the difference between an argument that deductively proves the conclusion from the premises, and one that gives essentially no indication of the conclusion at all.
                                        Likelihood
                                    **Often irrelevant:**   Often people present reasoning in a misleading way that should not be taken literally.
                                    Relevance
                                        This is especially common in informal discussions.
                                        Likelihood
                                        Often people present reasoning in a deductive manner, when in reality they intend to give a probabilistic argument.
                                        Likelihood & Relevance
                                            A probabilistic argument does not need to be deductively valid to have a significant impact.
                                            Relevance
                                        Often people present premises that don't technically represent what they meant to say.
                                        Likelihood & Relevance
                                            When one can sufficiently [reliably and quickly] determine that this is happening, it is typically better not to focus on invalidity.
                                            Relevance
                                                In such cases, focusing on invalidity is likely to waste time.
                                                Likelihood
                                                    [It's very important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NYxdL99oO0wiJzLy4FF6TvXs) to save time.
                                                    Relevance
                                                ^ There are better options for figuring out what they meant to say, to address their actual reasoning.
                                                Likelihood
                                                    You can [ask for clarification](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Hf0OMZKK9gpCvfma6I4h5Nwk)
                                                    Likelihood
                                                    You can attempt to [anticipate their meaning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=GiYDKlicnNc1dQVnntzf_w58)
                                                    Likelihood
                                **Unavoidable uncertainty:**   We cannot ever know for certain that an argument is valid.
                                    [Limitations of mental faculties](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=C5Uyd9SYvh-0SPYyOkBCMDpA)
                                    Likelihood
                            **Invalid ≠ false:**   An invalid argument can still have a true conclusion.
                                Validity merely describes whether an argument's conclusion can be deductively derived from its premises (i.e. whether, given the premises, we can know* that the conclusion is true). It does not describe whether the conclusion is in fact true or false, since it does not tell us whether there are any other arguments that could work, or whether the conclusion could be unprovably true/false.
                                Likelihood
                                    * Technically we still [can't know for certain](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=p3smzzmZMepqF12QT_zfXcHm), but it should typically still give very good indication.
                                ^ [Validity definition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=WxCDZRz_H2-cmIaiApjKzBGz)
                                Likelihood
                            It's possible for a deductively invalid argument to work as a good inductive argument.
                                Examples
                                    Rising sun
                                        Argument
                                            The sun has risen every morning so far.
                                            Therefore the sun will likely rise in the next morning.
                                                * As long as there isn't sufficient evidence to the contrary.
                                        Explanation
                                            This argument is invalid, the premise does not prove the conclusion, but it works fine as an inductive argument.
                            If term(s) are used in multiple different senses but the logic requires that the terms describe the same thing, this is called [conflation](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=rkFEurnAxeGlEmMJ8RQUkGO2) and makes the logic invalid*.
                                This is because logic only applies to [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ewojo1Kk7SCQ_tTLnAMkJeio) and therefore only those can be valid.
                                Likelihood
                                * Technically it is possible for the logic to be valid if the two different usages are treated as distinct.
                        Related
                            [Standard form](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=WBGbs-oyLu2VHlBm5Vkh-KAb)
                                A form of argument that makes it relatively easy to check the validity of the argument.
                    Soundness
                        Definitions
                            **Soundness** (in this context) typically refers to whether or not an argument is both valid and containing no false premises. If it meets both of these conditions, then it is a sound argument.
                            Main alternative definition
                                Occasionally "soundness" will be defined to require *only* that the premises be true, and not that the argument is valid.
                                This definition is often more useful than the main one if it doesn't cause confusion.
                                    Because it's often useful to describe only the truthfulness of the premises, and it's easy to say "valid and sound" in place of using the more common definition.
                            Which definition to use
                                To avoid confusion, you can also use "valid and sound" in place of the former definition, and "sound premise(s)" in place of the latter definition.
                        Limitations
                            An argument with unsound premises can still have a true conclusion.
                                It just cannot deductively prove the true conclusion.
                            A valid argument can still have unsound premises.
                                If the premises are not solidly established, then the conclusion cannot be solidly established via the argument either. The logic being valid doesn't mean that the premises are true, it can only tell us the logical consequences of the premises.
                                The conclusion can still follow from the premise(s) while the premise(s) are false.
                                    Example
                                        Premise 1:   2 + 2 = 5
                                        Premise 2:   John and Mary both gave me two apples each.
                                        Conclusion:   I received 5 apples from John and Mary.
                                The conclusion cannot be established from mere validity, the premises also have to be sound.
                            We cannot ever know for certain that an argument is sound.
                                [Limitations of mental faculties](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=C5Uyd9SYvh-0SPYyOkBCMDpA)
                            Oftentimes what was technically stated is unsound but what was meant is sound.
                Formal deduction
                    Formal true and false
                        In and out of correspondence with reality, respectively.
                        **Synonyms:**  Truth simpliciter, absolute truth
                    Importance
                        A very important process to use for [formal reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0At0cZGMuQERz1v4oedfKRza).
                            [Importance of formal reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=MNUQZ7bu6j_LRJXAne92I7Ri)
                            Relevance
                    Rules
                        Importance
                            Crucial for performing [formal deduction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=zsOpjP92dDsI8MqzcQASsNlD).
                                Importance of performing formal deduction.
                                Relevance
                        Replacement rules
                            Definition
                                Rules that apply in both directions. Either side can be replaced with the other.
                                Distinction
                                    Some of these rules come in pairs, split with a "|". These pairs cannot be swapped. Only expressions on either side of a ↔ may be swapped.
                            Contraposition   (Contra)
                            P → Q   ↔   -Q → -P
                                **Synonym:**   Transposition
                            Double negation   (-E / DN / -- Elim)
                            --P  ↔  P
                                **Synonyms:**   double negation elimination, negation elimination
                                Double negation introduction
                                P   ∴   --P
                            Demorgan's laws   (DM / DeM)
                            -(P ^ Q) ↔ -P v -Q    |    -(P v Q) ↔ -P ^ -Q
                                Negation of conjunction 
                                -(P ^ Q)  ↔  -P v -Q
                                Negation of disjunction
                                -(P v Q)  ↔  -P ^ -Q
                            Distribution   (Dist)
                            (P v Q) ^ R   ↔   (P ^ R) v (Q ^ R)      |      (P ^ Q) v R    ↔   (P v R) ^ (Q v R)
                            Conjunction introduction   (^I)
                            P, Q  ↔  P ^ Q
                            Exportation   (Exp)
                            (P^Q) → R   ↔   P → (Q→R)
                                Reducible
                            Material equivalance   (Equiv)
                            (P ↔ Q)   ↔   [(P → Q) ^ (Q → P)]      |      (P ↔ Q)   ↔   (P ^ Q) v (-P ^ -Q)
                                [Logical connectives](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=4c53ztjrA-5-dTCfvBk6ryLz)
                            Material implication   (Impl)
                            P → Q   ↔   -P v Q      |     -(P → Q)   ↔   P ^ -Q 
                                **Reducible to:**   The definitions of [material conditional (→ )](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uzxNZgNX8Jlzb09GO2ZXwiQw), [disjunction (v)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=TKmTc0y15-zDJqP-9eeA6L5Z) and [conjunction (^)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=7L3dxYa84Fg0AT8HpDH4jekq).
                                See [this entry's note](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=w5dUValYHC3XOad1KGCjAcyQ) for more details on how this might be wrong and an alternative way to look at it.
                            Commutative   (Com)
                            P ^ Q   ↔   Q ^ P      |      P v Q   ↔   Q v P
                                **Reducible to:**   The definitions of [conjunction (^)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=7L3dxYa84Fg0AT8HpDH4jekq) and [disjunction (v)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=TKmTc0y15-zDJqP-9eeA6L5Z), respectively.
                                I think. But if not, then maybe the [principle of induction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=JGRs9pW59a9Y_OB1lx7HkVL2) or similar. Like we've done this operation without issue so many times, therefore likely reliable. Or it can be expressed in truth tables and truth tables have been shown to be reliable via induction, etc.
                            Associative   (Assoc)
                            (P ^ Q) ^ R  ↔  P ^ (Q ^ R)      |      (P v Q) v R   ↔   P v (Q v R)
                                **Reducible to:**   [Commutative](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5CIJnWDSNGCLYi8MFent2pho)
                        Inference rules
                        Add more from classically valid [...] section of https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Propositional_logic 
                            Definition
                            Modus tollens   (MT)
                            P→Q, -Q  ∴  -P
                                Reducible
                                    Maybe reducible to doing
                                    [Contraposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=C7HJTjN5oFYdYKKFdeGGg4Xv) to get -Q → -P
                                    and then [modus ponens](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=haL1Nh2Q-JDklRNUGUdjSDwV) to get -P
                            Disjunction introduction   (vI / Add)
                            P  ∴  P v Q
                            Constructive dilemma
                            P→Q, R→S, P v R  ∴  Q v S
                                Modus ponens applied to both possibilities of the OR.
                            Destructive dilemma
                            P→Q, R→S, -Q v -S  ∴  -P v -R
                                Modus tollens applied to both possibilities of the OR.
                                There should probably just be a rule for being able to account for both possibilities of the OR, and then a reducible section can be created here and for the constructive dilemma.
                            Disjunctive syllogism   (DS / MTP)
                            P v Q, -P  ∴  Q
                                **Synonym:**   modus tollendo ponens
                            Disjunction elimination   (vE)
                            P v R, P→Q, R→Q  ∴  Q
                                **Synonyms:**   or elimination, proof by cases, case analysis
                            Conjunction elimination   (^E / Simp)
                            P ^ Q  ∴  P
                            Hypothetical syllogism
                            P→Q, Q→R  ∴  P→R
                                Reducible
                                    Subproof?
                            Modus ponens   (→E / MP)
                            P→Q, P  ∴  Q
                                **Synonym:**   Conditional elimination
                            Absorption
                            P→Q  ∴  P→(P ^ Q)
                                **Reducible to:**   [Modus ponens](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=haL1Nh2Q-JDklRNUGUdjSDwV) and [conjunction introduction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=vpPbbla7pUAkVCE8iImkuKNN)
                                Need to show that **conditional proof** is reducible too or use an alternative method.
                                    1. P→Q     (P)
                                    Absorption premise
                                    2. | P     (Hyp)
                                    3. | Q     (2,3 modus ponens)
                                    4. | P ^ Q     (4 conjunction introduction)
                                    5. P→(P ^ Q)     (2-4 [CP](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=BEcqN1V3BBSW3Qpf_JVjoZQk))
                            Premise   (P / Supp)
                            P
                                **Synonyms:**   supposition
                            Assumption   (Hyp / Assump / ACP)
                            P
                                A temporary premise used to begin a subproof.
                                ACP as in "assumption for conditional proof".
                            Reiteration   (R)
                            P  ∴  P
                                **Reducible to:**   [Law of identity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QNeZxWHbe7v-UYKBxTdg2LFx)
                        Proof methods
                            Proof by contradiction
                                Definition
                                    The process of assuming that some proposition is false, then logically deducing that its falsehood leads to a contradiction, and thus deriving the assumption must be false, and thus that the proposition must be true.
                                Importance
                                    Well founded
                                        If an assumption leads to a contradiction, then we derive that the assumption must be false based on the [law of contradiction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=iRkaxeyoE5AgB17FzWOds2C7).
                                        Likelihood
                                            i.e. It's impossible for a contradiction to exist, therefore if some belief leads to a contradiction, it's impossible that the belief is true.
                                            Likelihood
                                        If an assumption [about a proposition being false] is false, then we derive that the proposition must be true via [double negation](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ifYR-YwY6DUytsHV25rMjYea).
                                        Likelihood
                            Conditional proof   (→I / CP)
                            Q is proven using [assumption](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qwicBgToMzZ4dsUMRSRdGN3I) P  ∴  P → Q
                                The process whereby a sub-proof is provided, and a conditional is proven from it.
                                **Synonym:**   Conditional introduction
                                Example
                                    1. | (P → Q) ∧ P                 (hyp)
                                    2. | P → Q                           (1 ^E)
                                    3. | P                                     (1 ^E)
                                    4. | Q                                    (2,3 MP)
                                    C. ((P → Q) ∧ P) → Q     (1-4 CP)
                            Proof by contrapositive
                            Indirect proof   (-I / IP)
                            (Q ^ -Q) is proven using assumption P  ∴  -P
                                **Synonym:**   Negation introduction
                                **Reducible to:**   [Law of contradiction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=f0OtGrGk9iM-Ry2mQ04gpGKG)
                        Meta
                            The colors for the grandchildren of [Rules](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=-BroXwfgopzetJHC1qX8IWK4) (aside from definition, importance, meta and related sections) are just used for easier navigation/memorization, and not intended to be interpreted in the usual way. Their meanings are roughly as follows:
                            **Red:**   Introduces/eliminates a negation (-).
                            **Orange:**   Introduces/eliminates either a disjunction (v) or conjunction (^).
                            **Yellow:**   Introduces/eliminates a disjunction (v).
                            **Green:**   Introduces/eliminates a conjunction (^).
                            **Blue:**   Introduces/eliminates a conditional (→) or biconditional (↔).
                            **Purple:**   Non-traditional rules or simple tautologies.
                            Within each color category, typically "introduction"-esque rules/equivalences/etc. will come first, then "elimination" ones.
                        Related
                            [Logical connectives](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=4c53ztjrA-5-dTCfvBk6ryLz)
                    Proofs
                        Definition
                            A deductive proof is a logical argument that establishes the truth of a conclusion based on the truth of one or more premises, using established rules of inference and previously accepted axioms or theorems.
                            Example
                                "There are no married bachelors".
                                ^ This statement can be deductively proven given the definitions of "married" and "bachelor". 
                        [Proof methods](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ElPBHwauxQxXIJg4H7SMO-9E)
                    Formal contradictions
                        Definition
                            When multiple [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ewojo1Kk7SCQ_tTLnAMkJeio) entail (directly or indirectly) that one proposition is both true and false.
                            Distinction
                                This is different from a colloquial definition of "contradiction" which only requires that two things be very unlikely to both be true.
                                This is different from a colloquial definition of "contradiction" which is synonymous with "paradox".
                        Importance
                            Recognizing them can be hard sometimes because of [vague wording](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0gOG4jQ-x49IDTjJn9kHifS6)
                            Recognizing them can often be hard because of how indirect they can be.
                                Examples
                                Sat solver
                                Link
                    Natural deduction
                        Definition
                            In logic and proof theory
                            ^ A kind of proof calculus in which logical reasoning is expressed by inference rules closely related to the "natural" way of reasoning.
                            Specifically: A [syntactic proof system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Propositional_calculus#Syntactic_proof_systems) which assumes inference rules as primitives.
                            Link Inference rules, create a definition for primitive and maybe section/definition for syntactic proof system.
                            Distinction
                                This contrasts with [Hilbert-style systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_system), which instead use axioms as much as possible to express the logical laws of deductive reasoning.
                        Undischarged assumptions
                            Definition
                                In natural deduction
                                ^ A premise or hypothesis in a proof that remains (at least at some relevant point(s)) active and unremoved.
                                If a proof contains undischarged assumptions, it establishes that the conclusion follows from those assumptions, rather than being a standalone truth derived solely from axioms.
                                Example
                            Importance
                                Common
                                **Very relevant to final conclusion**
                                    If assumptions are not discharged (removed) by inference rules like Implication Introduction or Reductio Ad Absurdum, the conclusion is only true relative to those assumptions, not universally.
                                    Likelihood
                            Related
                                Vacuous discharge
                                    Definition
                                        The process of removing an assumption even if it was not used in the parent derivation.
                                    Importance
                                        Impactful
                                            Permissible in classical logic. 
                        Deductive consequence (⊢)
                        I don't think this is specific to natural deduction, should probably be conbined with material implication or whatever?
                            Definition
                                
                                Synonyms
                                    Syntactic consequence (⊢)
                                    Material implication (->)
                                Distinction
                                    Semantic consequence (⊨)
                                    Create section and link
                            Inference bar
                                A line separating a conclusion from premises in a proof.
                                **Typically:**   Placed under premises and over a conclusion.
                                Synonyms
                                    Inference line
                                    [Therefore (∴)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=PrGbhUbVHDmySbRlLQFOymuL)
                            Therefore (∴)
                            Maybe
                            Comma (,)
                                In a deductive consequence
                                ^ A separation between premises.
                        Styles
                            Fitch-style
                                Definition
                                    A style which uses vertical lines instead of numbers to keep track of undischarged assumptions.
                                    It is a style characterized by:
                                    ^ Its linear presentation of the proof, instead of presentation as a tree.
                                    ^ Subordinate proofs, where assumptions could be opened within a subderivation and discharged later.
                            Suppes-Lemmon-style
                                Standard form
                                Standard form may be a subset, superset or alias to "Lemmon-style".
                                    Definition
                                        1. An argument in standard form puts the premises first, and the conclusion last.
                                        2. The premises and conclusion are labelled, so they can be referred to easily.
                                        3. An [inference bar](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=b_n_5bwCeuL3a3P8Dih3GYkW) is placed between the premises and the conclusion.
                                        "Therefore" should get its own section probably.
                                            The inference bar indicates that the conclusion is intended to follow from the premises above it.
                                            It's essentially synonymous with the "therefore" symbol (∴).
                                        Example
                                            Informal:
                                            ^ __There’s no need to stop using fossil fuels unless climate change is real. But climate change is fake. So there’s no need to stop using fossil fuels.__
                                            Standard form:
                                            ^ P1) There’s no need to stop using fossil fuels unless climate change is real.
                                            ^ P2) Climate change is fake
                                            ^ \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_
                                            ^ C) There’s no need to stop using fossil fuels.
                                            
                                            Note that the ‘So’ that indicates the conclusion has been left out of the standard form. 
                                                That’s because its role is to indicate where the conclusion is.
                                                It is not part of the content of the statement the arguer is trying to get you to believe. 
                                            Because there are multiple premises, we number them.
                                                It does not matter what order you put the premises in, in relation to one another.
                                            In standard form, the premises must occur above the conclusion they are intended to support.
                                    Importance
                                        **Easier to assess validity:**   Standard form makes it easier to assess the [validity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UUAfcY2vsb-7FyMTvOmfclqa) of arguments.
                                        Copy to formal deduction
                                            The standard form of an argument is the stripped-down version of the argument that has the pure reasoning involved, without any of the unnecessary rhetoric.
                                                This allows for the argument to be assessed on its merits as an argument alone.
                                        Slow
                                        Move to formal deduction
                                            **Slow creation:**   Creating standard form arguments tends to take longer.
                                                We tend to be much more accustomed to dealing with [informal arguments](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=8GzHVvJUrHROzdmXaF07m4cE) than formal ones.
                                                Likelihood
                                            **Slow translation:**   Translating informal arguments to standard arguments usually takes a bit.
                                                [We tend to be much more accustomed to informal arguments than formal ones](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0tkEVCykyWK_o8Looww3IbS1)
                                                Likelihood
                                        [Importance of informal arguments](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=PF8RPRZIZSNuaZyC6TP5UpKu)
                                        Move to formal deduction
                                            The main alternative to formal deduction.
                                            Relevance
                                    Translating informal to standard form
                                        The statements occurring in standard form are often rephrased so that they express their content in a straightforward way.
                                            In ordinary language, people sometimes use questions or commands to communicate statements. When we put the argument into standard form, we rephrase them as statements.
                                            Example
                                                Suppose someone says:
                                                __University education was free for our parents’ generation. Shouldn’t it be free for us?__
                                                This is an argument: the person is trying to persuade you of something, and they are giving a reason. The reason is “University education was free for our parents’ generation”. “Shouldn’t it free for us?” has the form of a question, but it’s being asked rhetorically. The person who gave the argument is not asking a sincere question: they are using the question to make a point. The statement the arguer intends is “University education should be free for us”. When we put the argument into standard form, each premise and conclusion is rephrased so that it is expressed as a statement.
                                                P1) University education was free for our parents’ generation.
                                                \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_
                                                C) University education should be free for us.
                                                Rhetorical questions are one common way of making statements in ordinary language. Also common is expressing statements as commands. So, someone might say:
                                                __University education was free for our parents’ generation. Make it free for us!__
                                                Once again, a correct standard form rendition of the argument will rephrase the command as a statement, resulting in the same formal argument shown above.
                                        Each statement should be complete in itself.
                                            Importance
                                                Each statement is expected to be assessable on its own, so that both the [Validity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UUAfcY2vsb-7FyMTvOmfclqa) and [Soundness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=3idCayhSSV8FPYu2R0U8ShgA) can be assessed in a systematic manner.
                                            Example
                                                Consider the following argument:
                                                __Barack Obama was a great president. He was always dignified and composed.__
                                                The sentence “He was always dignified and composed” is incomplete, because we don’t know, when considering the sentence by itself, who is referred to by “he”. To make the statement complete, all we need to do is replace the “he” with the person it stands for. This is clear from the context. So, in standard form:
                                                P1) Barack Obama was always dignified and composed.
                                                \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_
                                                C) Barack Obama was a great president.
                                        Omitting rhetoric
                                            Importance
                                                Common
                                                    It is often the case that putting an argument into standard form will require omitting a lot of what was said.
                                            Useful questions to ask during this process
                                                What is the person trying to persuade me of?
                                                What reasons do they give to support that?
                                            Example
                                                Informal:
                                                ^ __Vegans are a bunch of puppy-kissing tree-huggers. They seem to think everybody should be vegan just because they are. Everyone is entitled to their own opinion. Besides, they give lousy reasons for being vegan: just as many animals are hurt through harvesting of plants as die in animal farming. __
                                                The conclusion is not explicitly stated here, but the main reason is clear.
                                                Formal:
                                                ^ P1) Just as many animals are hurt through harvesting of plants as die in animal farming.
                                                \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_
                                                ^ C) It is not the case that everyone should be vegan.
                                                
                                                You may think something is missing from the above argument. You are right. In chapter 3 we will look at how to make such standard form reconstructions more complete. Before we do so, we need to look at one important aspect of what makes a good argument: [validity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UUAfcY2vsb-7FyMTvOmfclqa).
                                                Yoink more from https://oercollective.caul.edu.au/howtothinkcritically/front-matter/introduction/ to resolve this part.
                                    Source
                                        https://oercollective.caul.edu.au/howtothinkcritically/chapter/standard-form/

            Theorems
                Definition
                    A statement that can be proven with no [undischarged assumptions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=gQd6miuzU7YbR72h0S0y_wGb) (i.e., all assumptions are discharged).
                Importance
                    Common
                    Impactful
                The law of excluded middle
                    Definition
                        All [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk) must either be true or false.
                    Reducible
                        The definition of [well-defined proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk).
                        This feels wrong, because it feels like "well-defined proposition" should be defined more in terms of lacking ambiguity than having a definitive true/false state. The latter seems more like a necessary consequence than a definition to me. But given the definition I put together based on similar terms, this seems right, so I'll leave it for now.
                        Assuming the above doesn't work, there is also this natural deduction proof:
                        1. | -(P v -P) (assumption)
                        2. | | P (assumption)
                        3. | | (P v -P) (putting 2 in an 'or')
                        4. | | False (contradiction with 1)
                        5. | -P (proof by contradiction, 2-4)
                        6. | (P v -P) (putting 5 in an 'or')
                        7. | False (contradiction with 1)
                        8. --(P v -P)     (proof by contradiction, 1-7)
                        9. P v -P     (double negation, 8)
            Source
                [Logic 101 by William Spaniel](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JBjAyrX7wms&list=PLKI1h_nAkaQq5MDWlKXu0jeZmLDt-51on)
                    A good youtube series teaching formal deduction from the ground up.
            Related
                [Practical techniques](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yQVWivooam-CWEWkiSH8D5Gu)
                    Standard deduction plays a major role in most of informal reasoning.
        Probabilistic reasoning
            Definition
                A variety of methods of reasoning in which the conclusion of an argument is supported at best with some degree of probability*.
                    Probability: Almost always [credence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5Ms3v8Y2fFiAIyYnOmUc4oHN), but very occasionally [propensity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=vMm_PqTAjUdjIqlIjPZWSXDO) depending on the context.
                Distinct from [standard deduction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ENg9uidRSS0t3eQBEFX4pGmm)
                    In probabilistic reasoning, the conclusion does not [follow with certainty when the argument is valid and sound] like it does in deduction.
                        This doesn't mean that there isn't any uncertainty involved when using standard deduction.
                            [We cannot ever know for certain that an argument is valid](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=p3smzzmZMepqF12QT_zfXcHm)
                            [We cannot ever know for certain that an argument is sound](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=TPYM2ig0UCPesToWq2uW4E_R)
                        And the reasoning justifying the probabilistic inference rules can be reduced to standard deduction, so ultimately probabilistic reasoning is still reducible to deduction.
                            [Reducible](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yXZAWKV-nCFCPsJYuYLsJUX_)
                    Eg. [Mathematical induction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_induction)
                        Kind of deceptively named, since it's actually a form of standard deduction and not induction as defined [here](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=IySr180_V91UYqUgHnPhcp4n).
            Importance / Limitations
                Common
                    A massive portion of reasoning is probabilistic.
                Impactful
                    Probabilistic reasoning can never justify literal absolute certainty.
                    Typically if all the main easily accounted for factors are taken into account, probabilistic reasoning cannot justify a very high or low probability.
                Improvable
                    There's a lot of very impactful and typically improperly or inadequately taught aspects of probabilistic reasoning that are taught better here.
                    Will be*, once the guide is much more complete.
                Difficult
                    [Probabilistic biases](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CGI6yCYSAdy8jsILM1rD8Wsw)
            How to view it
                Probabilistic reasoning should typically be thought of as the weaker, less reliable but often more widely available sibling to [standard deductive reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ENg9uidRSS0t3eQBEFX4pGmm).
                    While standard deductive reasoning is by no means rarely applicable, there are a vast amount of important situations in which exclusively standard deduction cannot take you all the way to a useful conclusion, but alongside probabilistic reasoning, can.
                    [Inductive reasoning typically can't justify a very low/high probability for much](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=2zYOk8AVyLM5FCI87pKT-BdJ)
                [Probabilistic reasoning is reducible to deduction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yXZAWKV-nCFCPsJYuYLsJUX_), it just isn't regarded as a part of standard deduction.
            Reducible
                It's just a useful higher level of abstraction, so we don't have to deal with so many deductive steps all the time.
                All probabilistic reasoning is just the [deductive](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=-n8BacOsj9yIbpLxsbz4df8M) application of some axioms, with the uncertainty coming from the [principle of induction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=u6QOAA3CMaWyvXF8U03DcNth) (or similar) and the [semi-reliability of mental faculties](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=b_aWAORb1OU0XVl_fRpRpFsj)
                Known associations
                    Using the uncertain definition of "known".
                        [Know (probability)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=TnFde8i_liH0vcSg8sIc-HEA)
            Induction
            Add stuff from https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inductive_reasoning
                Definition
                    the process of inferring some general principle from a body of knowledge, where the principle does not necessarily follow from the body of knowledge.
                        The body of knowledge might give us very good reason to accept the principle, but never ensures it for certain.
                    Example: White/black swans
                        If all swans that a person has observed so far are white, they may infer a universal categorical proposition of the form "All swans are white". They have good reason to believe the conclusion from the premise because it is the best explanation for their observations, but the truth of the conclusion is not guaranteed. Indeed, it turns out that [some swans are black](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_swan).
                Importance / Limitations
                    Common
                    Impactful
                    Improvable
                        Most people tend to apply a lot of specific and selective forms of induction, but tend discount many broad and abstract forms without good reason.
                            [Bias towards the specific/"grounded"](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mks1gpdiHB01sMtJ2DSU-1Xn)
            Fundamentals
                Important definitions: [credence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=udaRfJbExQFcuWIonQ7Ij8VV) and [plausibility](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=LGQrLMSvzhsoMTLjitCCpiK4)
                Probabilistic axioms
                    Definition
                        Fundamental rules about probability.
                        Distinction
                            Not necessarily unsupported like some definitions of "axioms".
                    The probability of all events are non-negative real numbers
                    Assumption of unit measure
                        The probability [that at least one of the elementary events in the entire sample space will occur] is 1.
                    σ-additivity
                        https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sigma-additive_set_function
                Evidence
                    Definitions
                        Here defined as information that epistemically supports a proposition.
                            This includes all information that epistemically opposes propositions, since for any proposition there is a negation.
                                If X opposes Y, then X supports the proposition "Y is false".
                        Often defined more narrowly, like information that epistemically supports a proposition by a quite substantial amount.
                Principle of indifference
                    Definition
                        The principle that in the absence of any relevant evidence, agents should distribute their credence (or "degrees of belief") equally among all the possible outcomes under consideration.
                        Synonym
                            Principle of insufficient reason
                    Importance / limitations
                        Impactful
                            The principle of indifference tends to work well.
                            A uniform distribution typically requires fewer assumptions than other distributions.
                                [Occam's razor](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UULVW93uPNRrRBC6RL4mTOjT)
                                Relevance
                            Only works for cases where we have no information to favor any particular outcome.
                                Only works for cases where the possibility spaces of each of the considered possibilities are the same size.
                                    [Occam's razor](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UULVW93uPNRrRBC6RL4mTOjT)
                    Reducible
                    Ideally be more specific here.
                        A proof by contradiction using fundamental axioms of probability.
                        If we are to assign a greater probability than 1/possibilities without any evidence, it would seem to result in a contradiction with said axioms.
                    Advanced usage
                        Breaking down [more complex cases where this principle cannot be applied] into multiple cases, in which some can have the principle applied.
                50% default
                Intermediate
                    Definition
                        The concept that every [well-defined proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk) should be assigned 50% credence in the absence of any (other) information.
                    Importance
                        Can be very useful for systematically deriving probabilities, as it encourages accounting for every relevant factor explicitly.
                        If not all substantial intuitive factors are explicitly accounted for, the result of starting from the beginning may be worse than if we hadn't bothered.
                            It would typically still be more accurate, even in these cases.
                            Impact
                                Our intuition is very unreliable.
                                Link
                            It's usually not too difficult to account for all substantial intuitive factors.
                            Likelihood
                                Our minds are typically (almost definitionally) quite ready to offer intuitive factors when we look for them.
                    Reducible
                    This isn't quite complete, but probably close enough for a while.
                        **Premise 1:**   50% of [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk) are true, and 50% are false.
                            **Premise 1:**   50% of [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk) are the inverse of the other 50% of [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk).
                                All [well-defined propositions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk) have exactly one inverse.
                                [Premise 2: A well-defined proposition must either be true or false (i.e. the law of excluded middle)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=WDTpuNbZvVZczhTDp2Hel6uT)
                            **Premise 2:**   A [well-defined proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk) must either be true or false. (i.e. https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=V99dOKyaHrazTWcoUt-ly_eC)
                            **Premise 3:**   The inverse of a true [well-defined proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk) is a false [well-defined proposition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=oa2Z9ESPYZwu46VM4MvmxUAk) and vise versa. ([definition of inverse](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=j3H0k5NrgqqJ2__9SgtP8ngy))
                        **Premise 2:**   [Principle of indifference](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=nwdnsuvq_ylA7Zc7YVfSoowC)
                        **Conclusion:**   Well-defined propositions have (without accounting for any other information) a 50% chance of being true and a 50% chance of being false.
                    Objections
                        What about cases where it's quite obvious that something has <50% likelihood, even when we're running blind?
                            **Example:**   we have no information about [Russell's teapot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russell's_teapot), but we all obviously believe that there is no such teapot.
                                It seems like when considering the likelihood of Russell's teapot, our assignment of a low probability is based on evidence.
                                For example it seems like the main reason is abductive. It would simply be much simpler to suppose that no such teapot existed, because supposing that it is there seems to require a greater number of assumptions.
                                Link to relevant abduction section(s) maybe?
                                Russell's Teapot is typically used to support the concept of a burden of proof, which is often a misleading heuristic, see <section to be linked> for more info on why.
                The law of complement
                    Definition
                        p(a) = 1 - p(not a)
                    Importance
                        [Calculating the inverse [is frequently useful] [...]](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=85SVn5HckMK8xnoDd9V9eo0P)
                Zipf distribution of evidence weight
                    Definition
                        The distribution of weight for different pieces of evidence for a given hypothesis tends to follow [Zipf's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf%27s_law).
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            The authors of this guide have consistently observed this.
                            Likelihood
                            [Zipf's law]([Zipf's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zipf%27s_law)) supports this.
                            Likelihood
                            [Possible to resolve virtually any disagreement with reasonable people](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=r1Q6C5LxLgbQ2nrTGTq2hC-G) thanks in part to this.
                            Relevance
                                It drastically limits the median amount of possible relevant irriducible disagreements that have to be explored to resolve the initial disagreement.
                                Likelihood | Maybe move elsewhere in "zipf distribution of evidence weight" importance and link here? | Link definition to "irreducible disagreement".
            Abduction
                Definition
                    Abduction is a form of logical inference that seeks the simplest and most likely conclusion from a set of observations.
                    Look into whether "simplest" is defined specifically or broadly.
                    Compared to other forms of probabilistic reasoning
                        While induction draws general conclusions that apply to many situations, abductive conclusions are confined to the particular observations in question.
                        Link induction
                    Synonyms
                        Abductive reasoning
                        Abductive inference
                        Retroduction
                    [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abductive_reasoning)
                    Examples
                        Two friends frequently go jogging together. They have a major argument, and therefore a falling out. However, the next week, you see them jogging together again, and assume they made up.
                            Source: https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/Reasoning
                        Observing a billiard game and seeing the 'eight' ball moving towards us, and abducing that the cue struck the ball.
                            
                            Source: https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/Reasoning
                How to view it
                    An often helpful form of probabilistic reasoning.
                Explanation / hypothesis
                Probably incorporate "explanation" terminology in other sections where reasonable?
                    Definition
                        A claim (or set of claims) intended to explain/account for some data.
                    Comparing explanations
                    Related
                        [Falsification](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=bL5yJhZQHw5Ii8SveWCStaDT)
                Prior probability
                    Definition
                        The credence that should be given to a hypothesis before considering the main evidence.
                            Main evidence: The main evidence that would typically* be considered.
                                * Except when the evidence that would typically be considered is [prior evidence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=xGudJgQHlJyk5nLoeSUyyHmN).
                        **Context:**  Otherwise known as simply "prior" in the right kind of probabilistic context.
                    Importance
                        **Impactful:**  Prior probabilies are typically one of the most impactful unknowns.
                            It's [important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=cXfd8G1zK1UZ_HGp2aSVkGmK) to account for unknowns.
                            Relevance
                        **Improvable:**  Obtaining the prior probability of a claim just involves looking at the ratio of scenarios under which it could be true vs couldn't be true, which is usually pretty easy to estimate.
                    Typical prior evidence
                        Usually priors use [occam's razor](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UULVW93uPNRrRBC6RL4mTOjT) and nothing else.

                    All prior evidence
                        [Occam's razor](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UULVW93uPNRrRBC6RL4mTOjT)
                Occam's Razor
                    Definitions
                        The problem-solving principle that recommends searching for explanations constructed with the smallest possible set of assumptions.
                        Synonyms
                            The principle of parsimony.
                            The law of parsimony.
                        Other relevant definitions (for other terms)
                        Move to definitions and link here
                            Broadness/simplicity and specificity/complexity
                            Probably move to definitions but retain a copy of the definitions here.
                                How few and many (respectively) logically irreducible suppositions have to be made.
                                Logically irreducible supposition
                                    A supposition that is unable to be logically reduced any further.
                                    Logically irreducible
                                        "Logically irreducible" is here defined as something like "cannot be reduced to multiple smaller elements of formal logic".
                                        This is because without this "floor" on complexity, it's very unclear how to properly count the number of assumptions. And this floor is grounded in the nature of the recommended [axioms](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HTSBFQMP49xI2F7tHDD5P1pd).
                                        Relevance
                                    Supposition
                                        "Supposition" is here defined as "proposition that is not epistemically justified by anything aside from simplicity."
                                    Note that this is a different concept from metaphysical irreducibility.
                                Usually the terminology used is "complexity" and "simplicity", but the definitions used in the context of Occam's razor translate roughly to measures of specificity/broadness.
                            Possibility
                                In this context: events or series' of events that are subjectively possible.
                                    "subjectively possible" being defined similarly to how "subjective probability" is defined.
                                        [Credence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5Ms3v8Y2fFiAIyYnOmUc4oHN)
                            Possibility space
                                [Possibility](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=IRjvgIigwzwP7Ki7mU3MS4m1)
                        Distinction
                            Different from "The simplest answer is the correct one.", since this is not always the case.
                        Examples
                            [Russel's Teapot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russell%27s_teapot)
                            Horse vs zebra
                                You hear hoofbeats outside, is it a horse or a zebra? Unless you live in the African savannah, it's very unlikely to be a zebra. We'd need more assumptions to get there - a zebra was imported to a local zoo, it escaped captivity, and now it's running amok. Whereas a horse would typically require fewer assumptions. That doesn't mean that it __cannot__ be a zebra, it just means that the prior probability for the horse hypothesis appears to be higher.
                            [Left out pickles](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=P5gRs0BLZT5qdBSotopaDGaw)
                        For more info, visit here: [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occam's_razor)
                    Importance
                        Common
                            Even when it's not directly mentioned by name, you'll see it frequently pop up in terms like "what, you think things just __happened__ to end up that way?"
                        Impactful
                            Reliable
                                [Reducible](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=sfhS9SmyddKtN_s1h63EzJNw) to solid principles that can be applied systematically.
                                Likelihood
                                Feels unreliable
                                Likelihood
                                    This is likely in part caused by the [bias towards the specific/"grounded"](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mks1gpdiHB01sMtJ2DSU-1Xn)
                                    Relevance
                                        Occam's razor typically doesn't feel specific/solid/grounded, it feels abstract and vague.
                                        Likelihood
                                        [Occam's razor can be specific/grounded](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=sfhS9SmyddKtN_s1h63EzJNw), it just isn't typically used in a proper systematic manner.
                                        Relevance
                                            This guide teaches how to use it in a proper systematic manner.
                                            Relevance
                            Useful if reliable
                                Generally powerful
                                    Specificity can often provide a very strong case against hypotheses that seem otherwise plausible.
                                    Relevance
                                    ^ When you add properties to a hypothesis, the probabilities of the properties initially get multiplied together, quickly resulting in a much lower prior probability.
                                    Likelihood
                                Rejecting the absurd
                                    Occam's razor is one of the most common tools we use to reject absurd hypotheses.
                                    Needs to be justified
                                    [Examples](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=GEc18a_OfPkwulsQ-VCBlSVl)
                                In the absence of other evidence
                                    Occam's razor provides us with epistemic justification to place a lower probability on even things that lack any evidence for/against it other than complexity.
                    Reducible
                    Make this much more clear.
                        [50% default](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=va42B5s8gXVrfWzHBQVBMPKV)
                            Via the principle of indifference, we can conclude that logically irreducible assumptions should by default be considered ~50% likely. Thus the more logically irreducible assumptions are involved in a hypothesis, the more unlikely it tends to be.
                        [Induction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OJSQEWGwiRo2syWQyU9FbjoE)
                            Occam's razor tends to work well, so via induction we can say that it will likely continue to work well.
                        "Complexity" translates to possibility space size
                            Definitions
                                [Possibility](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=IRjvgIigwzwP7Ki7mU3MS4m1)
                                [Possiblity space](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CWsm9Z1X5Oi2O7vGouwzf91b)
                            The set of possibilities where a given specific possibility occurs is typically smaller than the set of possibilities where a given broader possibility occurs.
                                Because specificity/broadness tends to refer to the amount of components required for something to be the case, i.e. the number of set divisions specified.
                            Example
                                If there's a ball in a box and that ball could be any of the 7 colors of the rainbow and you have pretty much no reason to think that it's more likely to be any particular color, then you should place an equal probability on all colors, meaning they all have a 1/7 chance of being the color of the ball.
                                The hypothesis "the ball is not red" is less specific than the hypothesis "the ball is red". The latter is more specific, so the set of possibilities where the ball is red is smaller (1) than the set of possibilities where the ball is not red (6).

                        Only applies to hypotheses that explain observations equally/similarly well
                            Definition
                                Occam's Razor does not apply when one theory explains observations better.
                                Example
                                    Quantum mechanics is more complicated than Newtonian mechanics, but it explains certain observations better, so Occam's razor doesn't apply.
                                        It's not quite as obvious as one might expect that QM is more complex than Newtonian physics.
                                            Most of the complexity of QM that we're familiar with is emergent from much simpler rules.
                                            Likelihood
                                            Much of QM is made much less comprehensible by the fact that it involves a kind of probabilistic process that's very unfamiliar to us.
                                            Likelihood
                                        https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=rcyeuef9zWsNYMnUImsDLk7a
                            Importance / Limitations
                                Impactful
                                    Occam's Razor should often apply at least somewhat in cases where relative explanatory power has more impact on the posterior probability than relative prior probability.
                                    Link explanatory power and prior probability
                                        The prior probability often still has enough of an impact on the posterior that it's worth taking into account.
                                        Likelihood
                                        ^ Even a difference of 1 assumption  means one side has at least twice the relative prior probability as it had before.
                                        Likelihood | Not sure about this
                                    It's very rare that we reason about two hypotheses for more than a few moments and justifiably find that they explain the relevant observations exactly equally well.

                Bayesian inference
                ~~Adept
                    Definition
                        Bayesian inference is a specific method for calculating a probability of a hypothesis given prior evidence and new information.
                        The hypothesis is sometimes referred to as an unobserved variable.
                        I think
                        **Inverse probability** is an old term for the probability distribution of an unobserved variable. 
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            Very generalizable
                                Bayesian inference can be generalized to many different kinds of problems, many of which involve unintuitive correct answers and intuitive incorrect answers, so it is a powerful way to overcome our intuitive limitations when it comes to probabilities.
                                Split
                        Improvable
                            We make it easy to use
                                We provide calculators and teach how to use them.
                    Calculators
                        You can use [this Bayesian calculator](https://www.desmos.com/calculator/k7ggs7wm2z) to more intuitively grasp how it works and more easily perform Bayesian reasoning.
                        There are likely better calculators out there so if anyone finds them, please let me know.
                            The way to use it is:
                            1. Assign the prior probability (the probability of a given hypothesis before accounting for whatever new information you want to account for) by dragging the black dot near the bottom to the appropriate place (eg. 0.1 = 10%).
                            2. Adjust the hypothesis' probability given the new info (without accounting for the prior) by dragging the yellow dot on the left to the appropriate place.
                            3. Adjust the probability of the hypothesis being false given the new information (without accounting for the prior) by dragging the blue dot on the right to the appropriate place.
                            After this, the bar at the bottom will tell you how probable the outcome is. 
                            You can drag the black circle to that spot and repeat the process if you have another piece of new info that you would like to calculate on top of all the other info as well.
                    Informal usage
                    Source
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bayesian_inference)

                            Another definition of "probability" that can be used in bayesian inference.
