            General tips
            Fix this, move some into inductive biases
                **Calculating the inverse:**   Frequently when trying to find the probability of an event, it is most easy to calculate the probability of the event *not* occurring and then deriving the probability of it occurring using [the law of complement](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=YyCP6IBRSC05LETZJBFlZhDM).
                "Inverse" might cause some confusion here, due to [inverse probability](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=8OQGdiSyGCTa9RInC0du0vng).
                Multiple events of a certain probability cannot simply have their probabilities added together unless the events are disjoint (mutually exclusive).
                Make sections for disjoint and conjoint(?) events, and how to calculate probabilities as a result. And create a section for the inductive bias involved in trying to add probabilities together when they are not mutually exclusive.
                    Otherwise, rolling a 6 sided dice 6 times would guarantee a 100% chance of rolling a 6 once.
                **Compounding uncertainty:**   In almost all cases, all else being equal, the more steps that are involved in any kind of reasoning, the more uncertainty carries into the conclusion.
                Likely link this in many places.
                    This is because all propositions (including most inference rules) aside from [axioms](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HTSBFQMP49xI2F7tHDD5P1pd) rely on:
                    Make a section for inference rules, probably under [Axioms](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HTSBFQMP49xI2F7tHDD5P1pd).
                    ^ The [semi-reliability of mental faculties](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=b_aWAORb1OU0XVl_fRpRpFsj) (which, due it to being "**semi**-reliability", always has some uncertainty).
                        Example:
                        Move or link this example in [Deduction (broad)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=-n8BacOsj9yIbpLxsbz4df8M).
                        When making [deductive](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=-n8BacOsj9yIbpLxsbz4df8M) arguments, we often like to say "so long as the premises are true and the argument is valid, the conclusion is certain!"
                        ^ In itself, this is true. However the implication is often something more like "so long as we have a high confidence in the premises and a high confidence in the validity of the argument, we can know for certain that the conclusion is true!", which is obviously quite questionable.
                            It is very possible (and common) for people to accidentally create invalid arguments or rely on premises that really seem true at first glance, but on closer inspection are plausibly false. Deduction provides "certainty" only in an idealistic sense, or often a "high probability" sense. Practically it only provides a stronger inductive argument (eg. "you suppose that your mental faculties are semi-reliable and you can't see a problem with this deductive argument, so you would agree that it's probably true, right?").
                    ^ And/or the [principle of induction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=u6QOAA3CMaWyvXF8U03DcNth) (or similar alternatives). Which also has some uncertainty, due to the fact that we (ostensibly) do not make infinite observations.
                Common probabilistic errors
                Maybe just move the stuff in here into the parent category.
                    Definition
                        Common probabilistic errors or areas where probabilistic errors often arise.
                    [False cause](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0W3UbrW6nEPQtBlFnsKqSbXT)
                    [Lacking evidence ≠ false](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=PoAcoW2P9Q9L6hiLGzVECWql)
                        The failure to consistently account for this.
                Prioritize more likely hypotheses
                    Definition
                        The strategy of prioritizing hypotheses that initially appear to be more likely.
                        Examples
                            Left out pickles
                                A man goes into his kitchen and sees a jar of pickles left out on the counter. He knows it wasn't him, which left two possibilities that their mind initially came up with:
                                1. A burglar broke in, stole several other items, and __also__ ate a pickle. He left the jar out to taunt the owner.
                                2. The man's wife had a pickle and then forgot to put away the jar.
                                The man could have totally checked their locks, made sure their valuables were still in the right place, etc. Instead he just yelled "Hey, did you leave this pickle jar out?" and got the simpler answer right away. Starting with the simpler solution (fewer assumptions than my burglar story) got them to the right answer efficiently.
                    Importance
                        Common
                        Impactful
                            In general, this strategy saves time.
                                Once the correct hypothesis is found, it typically means that no more analysis is necessary. Thus prioritizing the more likely hypotheses typically results in more quickly finishing the analysis.
                                Likelihood
                    First prioritize simplicity
                        Definition
                            When trying to explain an observation, you should go with the [simplest](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OZPNRE7XVJaT-1kbq4eiWIsC) explanation first.
                            Example
                                Forgetting Avengers
                                    You notice that the name of an old film wasn't you remembered it being. Maybe you notice a poster for the first Avengers movie and see it's called "Avengers Assemble". That can't be right, you think, you're sure it was just called The Avengers.
                                    Two explanations occur to you:
                                    1. you misremembered it
                                    2. you come from another universe where it was called The Avengers and you somehow jumped dimensions
                                    The second one requires more assumptions, namely the assumptions inherent in other universes existing and the possibility of travelling between them. The first assumption doesn't require any new assumptions on top of how you already understand reality, so you go with that one.
                                    But then you gather new evidence--another poster where it was called The Avengers. So what now--your first theory now doesn't work, so what do you do? Immediately adopt the second theory?
                                    No, because someone suggests a different theory. The film was released under different titles in different regions, and you saw a poster made for the UK. This isn't as simple as that first theory, but it's still simpler than the multiverse theory, so you change to that theory. And in this case that is the actual answer.
                                    So, it doesn't mean "the simplest explanation is always true", just that it's usually an easier process of arriving at the truth if you start at the simplest answer and work up
                                    The important part is that if you have two theories that explain observations similarly well then you should assume the simpler is true.
                                    **Source:**   [A Reddit comment](https://www.reddit.com/r/explainlikeimfive/comments/vyzqqs/comment/ig5b20x/?utm_source=share&utm_medium=mweb3x&utm_name=mweb3xcss&utm_term=2&utm_content=share_button)
                        Importance / Limitations
                            Impactful
                                Complexity = difficulty to investigate
                                Plausibly important enough to become its own section or something, and then become linked here?
                                    The more different variables or assumptions have to add up to get to a solution (i.e. the more complex the hypothesis), the more difficult it is to investigate.
                                The more complex the hypothesis, the less likely it is to occur in general.
                                Decently accurate
                                    Usually, there is [an obvious enough difference in simplicity] to make it quick and easy enough to estimate prior probabilities well enough to make them worth thinking of first.
                                    Very unsure about this, because you can switch back and forth between considerating simplicity and other factors at first, and the effect of starting on one vs the other would be so small in that case, that other factors likely play a bigger role in which should be started with.
                                        Still isn't always the case.
                                        Relevance
                        How to view it      (crucial to move stuff to importance / organize)
                        Likely move some of this to Occam's razor how to view it section and link it here. | Organize this better | Move a bunch of this to importance.
                            Simplicity and other factors both typically offer evidence.
                            Neither are inherently stronger than the other.
                                In some cases simplicity is stronger, in some cases it isn't.
                                Likelihood | Link examples for both kinds of cases.
                            Getting the posterior is just a matter of combining these probabilities, which can ultimately be represented by numbers.
                            The combination method should not care which number comes first. So it shouldn't intrinsically matter whether the number comes from the simplicity or other evidence.
                            So prioritizing one kind of evidence can only be justified when it's typically stronger, typically easier to get a relatively decent estimate for quickly, or something similar. 
                            And even then, it's questionable whether it's better to more quickly get into other considerations, or whether one should try to estimate both simplicity and other factors, and/or then more deeply consider both simplicity and other factors for each hypothesis.
                            Considerations for simplicity should definitely not end at prioritization of which hypotheses to consider in what order, because it's often unclear when a hypothesis should be discarded (and we are often irrationally reluctant to discard a hypothesis), and there are often unobvious considerations to have about simplicity that have a substantial impact on the prior probability (and thus also the posterior probability).

            Complex probabilistic reasoning
            ~~Advanced
                Definition (specific)
                    Probabilistic reasoning about topics with many complex interactions and factors.
                Importance
                    Common
                Emergent simplicity
                    Definition
                        The supposed phenomenon whereby for a simple question about a complex topic, the importance of the relevant components is rarely uniform, resulting in a decent guess being derivable from a much smaller set of  the factors/interactions than might be expected.
                    Importance
            Probabilistic biases
            Probably move to cognitive biases and link here? Also update backlinks if so.
                Statistical bias
                    Definition
                        A bias that occurs when one's sample is unrepresentative of a corresponding population one is intending to make an inference about.
                        Example
                            **Scenario:** An urn contains seventy white balls and thirty red ones (the population). You pick a number of balls out (a sample), and try to infer the number of white balls vs red balls (using induction).
                            **Bias:** In this case, the white balls happen to be heavier, meaning that your sample (the balls that you happen pick when using the process of reaching into the urn to fetch them) is likely to be biased (unrepresentative of the population).
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            When your method of learning about the world is biased, learning more may not help. Acquiring more data can even consistently __worsen__ a biased prediction. 
            Falsification
                Related
                    [Explanation/hypothesis](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=lelHmq5_RA_nj89mdbd500nr)

            Misc
                Anecdotal evidence
                    Definition
                        Evidence based on descriptions and reports of individual, personal experiences, or observations, collected in a non-systematic manner.
                            Distinction (systematic)
                            ^ "systematic" is not used in the "systematic reasoning" sense, but a broader sense.
                            Link systematic reasoning
                        Synonym
                            Anecdata
                        Examples
                            Personal experiences
                            Self-reported claims
                            Eyewitness accounts of others
                        Source
                            [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anecdotal_evidence)
                    Importance
                        Often pretty unreliable
                        ^ Often non-representative
                        Likelihood
                            Often justified with only a small number of anecdote(s).
                            Likelihood
                            Often [cherry-picked](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=VCYeE_G2-lkwQ6_S-w6A9idu)
                            Likelihood
                                Psychologists have found that due to cognitive bias, people are more likely to remember notable or unusual examples.
                                Likelihood
                                    Thus, anecdotal evidence is often not representative of a typical experience even when accurate.
                                    Relevance
                                    **Source:**   Gibson, Rhonda; Zillman, Dolf (1994). "Exaggerated Versus Representative Exemplification in News Reports: Perception of Issues and Personal Consequences". Communication Research. 21 (5): 603–624. doi:10.1177/009365094021005003. S2CID 145050644.
                                Often cherry-picked from an individual's experiences.
                                Likelihood
                                Often cherry-picked from a search through many individual anecdotes.
                                Likelihood
                        Common
                    Related
                        [Anecdotal fallacy](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=BlL_bkib3oL-kwQR393H6fJS)
                            An argument which relies on anecdotal evidence that does not provide enough support to justify the argument are called "anecdotal fallacies".
                        [Link to / combine with] testimony section
                        [Induction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OJSQEWGwiRo2syWQyU9FbjoE)
                            Anecdotal evidence is almost always used in some kind of inductive argument.
                Analogical reasoning
                    Definition
                        Reasoning involving a comparison of two systems in relation to their similarity. It starts from information about one system and infers information about another system based on the resemblance between the two systems.
                        Make this more colloquial.
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logical_reasoning#Analogical)
                    Importance / Limitations
                        Common
                            People often use analogical reasoning.
                        Impactful
                            Analogical reasoning tends to be relatively weak
                                The differences between the target idea and the allegedly analogous idea often hold important details that change whether or not the analogy works.
                                Analogies are often used when there is a disagreement or potential disagreement. Propositions where there is relatively high (potential) disagreement tend to have more reasons for believing that particular details make them true/false.
                                Split
                                [Weak analogy](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=aIaqFNRqVPOJak6jkLM6mLod)
                            [Analogical reasoning tends to be relatively weak](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=sTV1i15MBelZwM1OZV54lcFB)
                            [People often overestimate analogical reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Qa9fASkuaXd072rMnTTM9TH9)
                        Difficult / Improvable
                            People often overestimate analogical reasoning
                                [Analogical reasoning tends to be relatively weak](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=sTV1i15MBelZwM1OZV54lcFB)
                                [Weak analogy](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=aIaqFNRqVPOJak6jkLM6mLod)
                Convergent futures
                    Definition
                        Future events which many, most or all possible timelines between now and then will lead to.
                            Possible timelines defined in terms of subjective possibility: things that are possible according to our best guesses.
                        Example
                            Ice cube melting
                                It is incredibly difficult or near impossible to predict where exactly all the atoms of water will go during the ice cube's melting, but it is often easy to predict that the ice will completely melt despite that.
                    Instrumental convergence
                Novel testable predictions
                    Definition
                    Importance
                Avoiding double-counting
                    Definition
                        Avoiding counting evidence's impact twice.
                    Importance
                        Difficulty
                            Double-counting is an easy mistake to make, due to how it can often be hard to tell when we're using two long lines of evidence that are incompatible with each other but both support a conclusion we're trying to defend.
                Related
                    [Practical techniques](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yQVWivooam-CWEWkiSH8D5Gu)
                        Probabilistic reasoning plays a major role in a lot of practical techniques.
        Psychology
            Definition
                Study of mental functions and behaviours.
            Importance
                Impactful
                    Many of the topics here are incredibly important for good reasoning.
                    [Induction biases](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CGI6yCYSAdy8jsILM1rD8Wsw)
                Difficult
                    It's very easy to reason badly about psychology.
                        It's very easy to fall into [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37) when learning about psychology, which usually makes our reasoning about psychology significantly worse.

                        So it's important to learn about a lot of things.

                        There are substantial [common misconceptions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=8pU9ttpcR1n3re0U17Q4JkZ2) about learning.
                        Likelihood
                        There's a lot of knowledge in this section to help.
                        Relevance
                How to learn from this guide
                    Importance
                        [Common misconceptions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=8pU9ttpcR1n3re0U17Q4JkZ2)
                    [How to practice this guide](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=zyl1TWJiM71PFuHXhvjpKQxD)
                Feedback
                    Definition
                    Importance
                        We typically need accurate, consistent feedback in other to form the right [heuristics](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=49rqRClTn-Vx2v-ZXXm90ukK) and avoid forming the wrong ones.
                    Reasoning feedback
                        Definition (specific)
                            Here: The feedback you get for how well you performed at reasoning and how you could improve.
                        Importance
                            Typically practicing reasoning on soft science topics does not help us reason much better because we either get no feedback or unclear/misleading feedback (such as our emotions, how right/wrong we feel). So we have no good system to reinforce good habits and prune bad ones.
                            However if we treat what others say seriously, even when we really feel like it's wrong, there is a good way to use what they say as effective feedback. However we must be incredibly careful with this, because it's very easy to either take what they say too seriously or not seriously enough (usually the latter, at least in practice).
                            That is one of the reasons we place such importance on steelmanning (in the way it will be described). Without this, it's very difficult to get good feedback. Unfortunately this can become a self-perpetuating cycle, where you can't get good feedback to improve how to get good feedback. As such, we will develop a system for practicing and getting clear feedback without needing these techniques.
                Psychology of learning
                    [Practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=7uTPFcFISeOZWgi6CC8b3Q50)
                        Incredibly important for learning in general.
                            [Skills require practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=79KS_JjMDcI8g9agrwdEtRao)
                    [Learning from others](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5Qlio6RfNJC5UcTvCa9o-7SX)
                Fitting things together
                    Definition
                        The process of mentally categorizing/connecting information.
                    Importance
                        Common
                            People fit different topics together all the time, so it's important to learn how to do it well.
                        Impactful
                            [Importance of generalizable concepts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HyAnyIPx3VpR9Vs61Kj6CslU)
                            Helpful for [clear communication](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Yxl_WsBK8P0HiEmkAD6MWYWr)
                                By having more mental connections (if they are largely accurate), we can often get a more precise grasp of what exactly things mean.
                    Primarily using broadly applicable concepts
                        Importance
                            Helps with accurately understanding things
                                Understanding how things work in a very general way helps avoid [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                            Helps with quickly learning things
                                By consistently understanding topics using concepts that apply to many different things, you'll have more very-generally-applicable-knowledge.

                    How to practice this guide
                        Definition
                            How to practice the material in this guide.
                        Reasoning practice problems
                            Definition
                            Importance
                                Testing
                                Practicing
                            Limitations
                                There aren't practice problems for many important forms/facets of reasoning.
                                    Cause: Many important forms/facets of reasoning are difficult to design practice problems for.
                                    Cause: People can use [reasoning feedback](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=W7VkGKlZfqI3D-chGqzjjpwn) to improve their reasoning further.
                            Problem types
                                Recognizing assumptions
                                    Definition
                                        Tests involving checking if a supposed assumption is actually an assumption of a given statement.
                                            Intentionally vague, I'm not sure what interpretation of "assumption" would be best to use for the test.
                                            Obviously should be clarified if/when the test is made.
                                    Implementation note
                                        We'll have to be very careful when making this test, as it would likely often be very easy to mistakenly assume that something is an assumption of something else when it isn't.
                                            We might be best off using very abstract or toyish scenarios, or just writing assumptions which are definitely necessary for the statement.
                                    Types
                                        Premises
                                            Definition
                                                Checking if something is necessarily a premise of something else.
                                            I haven't thought about this much.
                                        <99% credence
                                Recognizing (in)validity
                                    Definition
                                        Tests involving checking if conclusions necessarily follow from sets of premises (the sets of premises may take the form of natural language statements).
                                    Importance
                                        Practice sections on deduction seem like they could be quite beneficial for thinking of exceptions and nuances, which would be beneficial for criticalness and indirectly open mindedness.
                                    Specific problems
                                        Ferb's wager
                                Making good inferences
                                    Definition
                                        Problems involving addressing degrees of likelihood/certainly for a conclusion given a statement, assuming that the statement is definitely true.
                                        Distinction
                                            This doesn't necessarily mean assuming that our interpretation of the statement is definitely true.
                                        Example
                                            [5-step system](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=KKPP9u9YS7VgWMdzSR8SYgDE)
                                    Types
                                        5-step system
                                            Natural language interpretation
                                                True
                                                Probably true
                                                Not enough information
                                                Probably false
                                                False
                                            A credence interpretation
                                                c ≥ 19/20 (true)
                                                3/5 < c < 19/20 (probably true)
                                                2/5 ≤ c ≤ 3/5 (unsure)
                                                1/20 < c < 2/5 (probably false) 
                                                c ≤ 1/20 (false)
                                                Implementation note
                                                    5/95% might be better as 1/99% or even 0/100%, especially if we tell the test taker to select answers as if they're assuming that their interpretations are definitely (100% credence) correct.
                                    [Common inductive errors](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=m9PFkYuw2EMpsMzGXrogqtdj)
                                        We should abuse these in the problems.
                                Judging arguments
                                    Definition
                                        Judging how weak/strong arguments are.
                                            This definition is vague and should be improved for the test if nothing else.
                                Judging interpretations
                                    Definition
                                        A test comprised of statements and interpretations of sections of those statements, where one must answer whether the interpretations correctly interpret that section of the statement (at least when taking into account the context of the other parts of the statement).
                                Translating formal to informal
                                    Implementation note
                                        To make an automatic system to test for this, we can use multiple choice questions.
                                Translating informal to formal
                            Implementation types
                                Definition
                                    Ways in which problems can be implemented.
                                Types used
                                    Definition
                                        Which types are used during the test.
                                    1-type
                                        Only one type can appear, so you can practice just that type.
                                    All-type
                                        Any type can appear, so you have to pay attention to all the important details that you can.
                                Memory (optional)
                                    Definition
                                        Problems are presented and you have to remember important details about them and answer later.
                                    The way to view this
                                        Plausibly important for getting people to focus on important details about statements (especially like validity/invalidity).
                                    Pause-based
                                        Definition
                                            X seconds to read the statement, Y seconds waiting, then Z seconds to read the proposed conclusion/interpretation/etc. of the statement and answer.
                                        Times
                                            60-5-60
                                    Juggling
                                        Definition
                                            Back and forth between reading and answering, and you have to remember important details about the problems.
                                            Example
                                                A¹,?²,B¹,A²,C¹,B²,D¹,C²
                                Emotions
                                    Definition
                                        Implementations that try to activate irrational emotions during the test.
                                    Importance
                                        Avoiding overly [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                                    Controversial topics
                                        Disclaimer
                                            This test uses controversial and disturbing topics to try to illicit irrational, emotional intuitions, instincts and reactions, to make you rely on previously (and likely unreasonably) memorized patterns, rather than general reasoning.
                                            Your goal is to try to resist these things as much as possible and reason using the same kind of general reasoning you would normally apply to less controversial topics.
                                    Using [persuasion](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=S-S1oq4cZbPSPeH4eYJKd9K7) tactics
                                    Abusing [Hot biases](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=4b2Z6i8P8doUYRe6rHWYtv5I)

                                    User contributions
                                        Definition
                                            Users contribute the problems.
                                        Process
                                            User gets info.
                                                User is given a template and instructions on roughly how to fill it out.
                                            User gives problem.
                                                They fill it out and send it to server.
                                            Auto moderation?
                                                Check for correct values in correct places.
                                                    Correct length.
                                                    Correct type.
                                                Check for inappropriate language.
                                            User moderation.
                                                Make sure scorer isn't author.
                                                Check if user is trustworthy by giving random already-scored problem(s).
                                                    Probably give 2 already-sufficiently-scored problems (1 that isn't good and 1 that is) and some not-yet-sufficiently-scored problems to get feedback on them.
                                                        Give these in a random order.
                                        Motivation
                                            None
                                            To take test
                                                Proportional
                                                    1-per-n problems
                                                        Definition
                                                            Give one problem to receive n problems.
                                                    N problems free, very easy to get 10%, easy to get 25%, medium to get 50%, hard to get 75%, very hard to get 90%, impossible to get 100%.
                                                        Database has 200.
                                                            10 problems free.
                                                            Rate 4?/Create 1, +10 problems
                                                            Rate 8?/Create 2, +30 problems
                                                            Rate 16?/Create 4, +50 problems
                                                            Rate 32?/Create 8, +50 problems
                                                            Rate 64?/Create 16, +32 problems
                                                            Rate 128?/Create 32, +16 problems
                                                            Rate 256?/Create 64, +8 problems
                                    Rated problems
                                        The problems could be rated like in lichess, that could potentially be very cool and useful for several reasons.
                                            Maybe allows us to test people.
                                            Allows people to get problems of a more consistent difficulty.
                                Testing methodology
                                Combine with implementation methods
                                    Consistency > speed
                                        Maybe test should tell takers to aim for 95% accuracy, and heavily penalize for <90%?
                                        Consistency should be considered significantly more important than speed, especially when considering open mindedness.
                                    Auxiliary modifiers
                                        Interest
                                            People could be asked before or after the test how motivated they are/were.
                                        Stamina /ability
                                            People could be asked what their stamina / ability is/was like before/after the test.
                                        There could be an option to show the results with and without these modifiers maybe.
                                Having people write why they disagree with us if their answer is different maybe?
                                Using [vague wording](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0gOG4jQ-x49IDTjJn9kHifS6)
                            Ideas
                                Testing for [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37) instead of open mindedness directly.
                                    Easier because testing open-mindedness directly requires checking for changed beliefs, which is very cheatable and hard to do properly.
                            Related
                                [Practice problems](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=X1LikFP17mEZqub9nd_2Uq2E)

                            [Proactive practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=9SVyks7moIibu2HXE-cK1qWi)

                                If you don't get feedback, it's going to be very difficult to properly learn from this guide.
                    Skills require practice
                        Definition
                            Complicated precise behaviors typically require habits to perform consistently.
                        Importance
                            People typically don't properly internalize what they read about reasoning and implement it into their epistemic [habits](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mwz5Ve2BffIKD_YiMRN4SJOU), because they don't practice it enough to form those habits.
                            Merely knowing how to reason well doesn't do much until you form the [habits](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mwz5Ve2BffIKD_YiMRN4SJOU) necessary to consistently apply that knowledge.
                            [Common misconceptions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=8pU9ttpcR1n3re0U17Q4JkZ2)
                            Mastery
                                In virtually all areas* we can properly test, it seems that our [subconscious](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=r2u3zb8iZbtZCj5aZBVmU0ON) typically takes a lot of https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=9SVyks7moIibu2HXE-cK1qWi to properly master topics.
                                    * minus those that are (near-)trivially simple.
                        Reasoning
                            You can learn all the theory you want about how to play the piano, but if you don't practice it, you won't be able to perform all the precise movements, even for simpler songs (unless you've practiced being able to do so).
                            Likewise this applies for mental activities that require lots of complicated precise actions.
                            You can learn all the info you want about how to play chess well, but if you don't practice it, you won't be able to act it out consistently.

                        How to view it
                            Important info about ideas is scattered
                                Unfortunately, there is currently often no one place to look for information regarding a single topic of debate that represents all sides of the issue and presents enough depth and research for the information provided.
                        Wikipedia
                            https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia
                            How to view it
                            Limitations
                                Typically somewhat unstructured (compared to the ideal).
                                Typically has a lot of fluff (compared to the ideal).
                                    Talking about the history/major figures a lot, in places where it's unlikely you would need the info.
                                Some important topics are often buried in the pages of related but different topics.
                                Usually lacking a lot of depth, particularly for controversial issues.
                                Typically using the broadest common definitions, which can often be confusing.
                            Benefits
                                Usually a relatively decent and quick way to grasp the rough outlines of ideas.
                        Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy
                            https://plato.stanford.edu/
                            How to view it
                            Limitations
                                Typically somewhat unstructured (compared to the ideal).
                                Many important topics do not have dedicated pages, which at best makes it difficult to find them and at worst neglects to cover them.
                                Usually lacking some depth, particularly for controversial issues.
                                Typically has a lot of fluff (compared to the ideal).
                                    Talking about the history/major figures a lot, in places where it's unlikely you would need the info.
                            Benefits
                                Usually relatively thorough.
                                Usually pretty clear if you're semi-familiar with the relevant concepts.

                    Research methodology
                        Primary sources
                            Definition
                                Immediate, first-hand accounts of a topic, from people who had a direct connection with it.
                                Example
                                Give an example based on reasoning/experiment rather than direct experience, to clarify that potential ambiguity.

                Proactive learning
                    [Post-failure review](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=vt5kpj9CiWd1H217AZA8HD94)
                Post-failure review
                    Definition
                        Reviewing the causes and trying to come up with solutions to prevent future repeats, after a failure.
                    Importance
                        Generally an incredibly underrated technique to apply to many problems that are often deemed (consciously or subconsciously) unsolvable.
                    Limitations
                        Freak accidents
                        ~~Intermediate
                            Definition (specific)
                                Freak accidents that are very unlikely to ever happen again.
                                Distinction
                                    This is different from freak accidents that are likely to happen again. Eg. A very unlikely event that causes a plane crash should likely be accounted for even if it's very unlikely, because there are so many planes flying constantly that the chances of a repeat are usually decently high.
                            Nuance
                                While the specific accident might be so unlikely, it may be part of a category of possible failures which has a small set of solutions required to prevent most of the possible failures. In this case, while the specific failure may not be worth focusing on for its own sake, it may be worth focusing on to better understand the category and how to prevent similar failures from occurring.
                            These are sometimes so unlikely to happen again that it's probably not worth trying to prevent repeats.
                        Unimpactful failures
                            Definition (specific) 
                                Failures that are so unimpactful compared to the cost to solve them, that they aren't worth solving for their own sake.
                            Nuance
                                While the specific accident might be so unimpactful, it may be part of a category of possible failures which has a small set of solutions required to prevent most of the possible failures. In this case, while the specific failure may not be worth focusing on for its own sake, it may be worth focusing on to better understand the category and how to prevent similar failures from occurring.
                            These are sometimes so unimpactful that it's probably not worth trying to prevent repeats.
                    Finding the causes 
                        Impact
                            Definition (specific) 
                                Causes that have the greatest impact on the potential for similar future failures.
                            Limitation
                                [Cost](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Cb8A2O3Dkr5lF2XUj99-rQWa)
                                    High impact causes may not be worth considering if the cost is too high.
                        Cost
                            Definition
                                Causes that have a low cost to prevent.
                            Limitation
                                [Impact](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=hmvcXgFtyABw9AdmcfZUXrBO)
                                    Uncostly-to-prevent causes may not be worth considering if they have low impact.
                            Direct
                                Definition
                                    The upfront obvious cost(s).
                                    Example
                                        The financial cost.
                            Indirect
                                Risks
                                Other negative consequences
                    Finding solution(s)
                        Solution(s) to the specific problem
                        Solution(s) to similar problem(s)
                        [Contingencies](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HGsYQS9G0jsA4vN6p0eGkk4G)
                Measuring learning
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            Measuring learning allows you to generate [feedback](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=nJ3ltWAInP1X3K5j7Yf8aM99)
                    Explaining what you're learning
                        Importance
                            Impactful
                            ^ A good way to measure how well you've learned something (and to better internalize it) is to try to explain it.
                            ^ ^ One's ability to explain something tends to be highly correlated with their intuitive understanding of the topic.
                            ^ ^ ^ A good intuitive understanding tends to be preceded by understanding that something in terms of more familiar things. And more familiar things tend to be understood by more people.
                        Related
                            [Intuitive understanding](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Lyj37DqUcGeYIrDH-6UuXTxI)

                    Common misconceptions
                        Definition
                            Common misconceptions about learning.
                        We are sponges
                            Definition
                                The feeling/idea that we are information sponges who merely need to hear things (often only once) in order to fully incorporate them into our behavior.
                            Importance
                                Even when we are consciously aware that this is wrong, we often still feel as though it is right.
                                    Example
                                        When we listen/read a ton more material than we can possibly internalize at once, because it makes us feel like we're making more progress than we would otherwise feel like we're making.
                            Related
                                [Skills require practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=79KS_JjMDcI8g9agrwdEtRao)
                        Little practice = mastery
                            Definition
                                The feeling/idea that we only need to practice a little bit in order to become masters.
                            Importance
                            Common causes
                                The feeling/belief that [proactive practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=9SVyks7moIibu2HXE-cK1qWi) can speed up mastery by more than it can.
                                The feeling/belief that [we are sponges](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mh31JVikXkHTrnsNloomB98p) (at least largely).
                            Related
                                [Skills require practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=79KS_JjMDcI8g9agrwdEtRao)
                        Mastery is unattainable
                            Definition
                                The feeling/idea that we could never be really fantastic at something(s).
                            Importance
                                Usually we underestimate how much we can improve with the right approach.
                            Nuance
                                Sometimes this is (at least practically) true.
                            Related
                                [Skills require practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=79KS_JjMDcI8g9agrwdEtRao)
            Habits
                Definition
                    Routines of [behavior](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Behavior) that are repeated regularly and tend to occur [subconsciously](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=r2u3zb8iZbtZCj5aZBVmU0ON)
                    Synonym
                        Wont (formal term)
                Importance
                    Impactful
                        Learning is not enough
                            Definition
                                Learning about a good/bad practice is typically not enough to adopt/avoid it.
                            broken link (replace with something relevant here)
                    Common
                        We're almost always operating on habits
                            There's little we can do to change this.
                    [Importance of practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=y9rQ9gCcN0WYM7R_0bgRUsHv)
                Limitations
                    [Practice](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=7uTPFcFISeOZWgi6CC8b3Q50) required
                    [Feedback](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=nJ3ltWAInP1X3K5j7Yf8aM99) required
                [Changing habits](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=737-9b9UIs5aAHrA10DDEWlC)
                Bad habits
                    Definition
                        Habits that you would rather not have.
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            The vast majority of our bigger mistakes are typically partially caused by bad habits.
                        Common
                            Bad habits influence a massive amount of behavior for virtually everyone.
                    Finding bad habits
                        Find source of bad behaviors
                        Related
                            [Self-awareness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=02ErcrGfQIBieY8kw6Fke-Nc)
                    Removing bad habits
                    Possibly somewhat combine/replace with changing habits section
                        Importance / Limitations
                            Impactful
                                [Requires finding them](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XpT2iNVGc7je2c3fLuyQcKV8)
                                Requires training
                        How to view it
                            Something we should virtually always be trying to do.
                        0. Prepare to remove the bad habit
                            These are optional practices in no particular order:
                            Find a habit to replace it
                                Importance: This often makes it feel more natural and easier, since you can focus on doing the alternative thing(s) rather than the thing you would have otherwise done.
                            Change motivators
                                Definition
                                    Motivator (in this context): Factor that motivates us to perform an act [we have a bad habit or want to have a good habit] to perform.
                                Bad motivator changes
                                    Definition
                                        Good/bad (in this context): Things we think would lead to better or worse habits, respectively.
                                            Distinction: Different from ethically/axiologically good/bad.
                                                It's possible for it to be best (especially temporarily) to keep a bad habit or remove a good habit for various reasons.
                                    Remove bad motivators
                                    Don't add bad motivators
                                        Example: You identify a bad habit to eat unhealthy food, and to make it easier to avoid, you stop buying unhealthy food.
                                Good motivator changes
                                    Definition
                                    Limitation
                                        Typically [reduces intrinsic motivation](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=SxW_3R0188AszzqLS04sbpt3) for the good behavior
                                    Add good motivators
                                        Example: Get someone to hold you accountable.
                                            Importance: Can often be very effective.
                                            Limitation: Such people often pressure us in a way that can cause bigger issues.
                                            Limitation: Such people often assume that certain things will help you, when they won't. This can cause issues.
                                    Don't remove good motivators
                        1. Learn to recognize when you're doing it
                            1.1 Learn to recognize when you're in situations where you will likely do it.
                            1.2 Learn to ask yourself questions to check if you are doing it.
                        2. When you realize that you're doing it, avoid following the bad habit
                            2.1 And instead do what you want to learn to form a different habit to do, if there is one.
                        3. Regularly practice the habits that you want to replace the bad ones with
                            3.1 Find good replacement habits.
                            3.2 Form a plan to make yourself practice the good replacement habits well and consistently.
                                3.2.1 Principles for practicing habits well.
                                3.2.2 Principles for practicing habits consistently.
                        **Source:**   https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Undoing_(psychology)
                    The habits of improving habits

                            Developing habits which help with improving your habits.
                        Importance
                            This is crucial for self-improvement in general.
