                        Habits are a crucial part of learning, and always arise as a sort of learned behavior.
            Intellectual humility
                Definition
                    General ability to avoid being overconfident.
                Importance
                    Impactful
                        Extremely important for avoiding close-minded tendencies.
                        ^ Close-minded tendencies are one of the largest contributors to irrationality ever.
                        Support
                    [The importance of learning about close-mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=3JYkrvEjUHYENVJaDqFSq3Cd)
                Open-mindedness (broad)
                    Definition (broad)
                        Skill to change your mind when it is most reasonable to.
                        Distinction
                            Distinct from one's [agreeableness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=zlCs-xFdA9UKoVTDSukgddfI)
                                Although this is typically correlated.
                            Distinct from the degree to which one is willing/able to tolerate others or other viewpoints.
                                Although this is still plausibly correlated.
                    Importance / limitations
                        Common
                            Close mindedness is very common.
                                If it weren't, people wouldn't disagree so much on topics with a lot of ["Soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=VqyTyvLNbJ6WtfifAe0egZtQ)
                                Examples of close mindedness being very common
                        Impactful
                            For those who use the right strategies, it's typically much more impactful than criticalness because of [critical piggybacking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=2J5emCtKut8DRA6avr2C4r87)
                            For those who use the wrong strategies, it's very bad.
                                Examples of lacking epistemic humility being extremely bad for reasoning
                                Move this to broader section about how people tend to be irrational.
                                    [Three out of three Verizon employees believe that $0.002 = ¢0.002](https://youtu.be/nUpZg-Ua5ao?si=ysSxbuts4S6LLFTD)
                            The usefulness of open-mindedness is very dependent on knowledge and criticalness.
                                Criticalness
                                    With insufficient criticalness (and/or insufficient [Open-mindedness (broad)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=TBDKCqfm_SEQfc4szPkt4Aqr)), it's going to be very difficult to properly filter the information you recieve and determine what makes the most sense.
                                Knowledge
                                    If you don't have the important info in the first place, the ability to change your mind in accordance with it won't do much good.
                        Improvable
                            Open mindedness is determined by mental [habits](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mwz5Ve2BffIKD_YiMRN4SJOU), and mental habits can almost always be changed.
                        Difficult
                            [Difficulty of measuring](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=rA2h7QNAIULbrA_CxFB34k2K)
                    How to view it
                        Reasoning abilities are mostly determined by open mindedness, so it is one of the most important things to get right.
                            ["Soft" reasoning makes up a large portion of important reasoning.](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=9tq0nu1KebS2pKlxYbgP9dvS)
                    Causes of close-mindedness
                        Emotions from open/close mindedness
                            Confidence
                                High confidence
                                    It almost always feels cool and satisfying to be confident, so long as it doesn't feel misguided.
                                    It feels like you're the strong, powerful, capable one who firmly knows the truth.
                                Low confidence
                                    Less respect
                                        Being less confident typically makes you appear weaker, less reliable, less cool and less attractive.
                                    Less trust
                                        People trust confidence way too much, so having less confidence means way less trust, even when the opposite makes the most sense.
                                        Link to cognitive bias on this in future.
                            Being "right"/"wrong"
                                Being "right"
                                    It feels cool and satisfying to feel right and like you have the truth.
                                    You're the capable one who's correct, who's aligned with reality, who knows best.
                                Being "wrong"
                                    Embarrassment
                                        Often, accepting that you were wrong means embarrassment, especially if it was because of a pretty obvious error. 
                                    Lack of recognition
                                        Unfortunately people don't typically appreciate or compliment people for recognizing their own errors, despite how important and rare it is to do.
                        Close-minded close-mindedness
                            Definition
                                Being close-minded about how close-minded they themselves are or could be.
                            Importance
                                Common
                                Impactful
                                    It makes it much more difficult to become more open-minded.
                                        If one can't recognize their own close mindedness (or the plausibility of it), they have much less reason and motivation to proactively improve.
                                    At the very least, it is important to seriously consider the possibility that you are slightly close-minded in the particular area you are discussing in the vast majority of cases.
                                        Even if there's only a 0.1% chance that you're somewhat close-minded about the topic, that's still enough to justify taking it seriously because the cost of taking it seriously is so low and the reward of fixing it is potentially very high.
                            How to view it
                                One of the most important areas to be open-minded about is the possibility of your own close-mindedness.
                            Defensiveness about cm cm
                                Definition
                                    Getting defensive about the idea that we could be close-minded.
                                Importance
                                    Common
                                        Often when someone points out our close-mindedness, they do so in a way that is or seems accusatory, causing us to feel defensive.
                                    Impactful
                                        When defensive about it, it's often very hard to properly reflect on the possibility that we could be close-minded on the topic when others point it out, despite how important it is.
                                Countermeasures
                                    Taking a [break](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=T8lQobqEPaFFypMihwloQHLZ) before considering it.
                                    [Analyzing your own discussions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=O32isZwT45SUSXPvCW0M_zr5)
                        Related
                            [Unsystematic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7)
                                [Unsystematic reasoning makes it much harder to realize when we're wrong](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=G6re_Z7HFY4TY1H6os1Spa16)
                            Lacking [self-awareness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=02ErcrGfQIBieY8kw6Fke-Nc)
                            [Heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                    Strategies for open-mindedness
                        [Acknowledging mistakes](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=u9DK4UQhAdQKZIwwKFHicH00)
                            [Intellectual humility](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=gh0FW_-AcyLbpnuFvEWm45Ke)
                        [Other-shoe review](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=d7AlWLW-6EAuja2PutdZEj64)
                    Measuring [open-mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=C2NJmspsNQQbItGTwTU9d299)
                        Importance
                            Impactful
                                Knowing yourself
                                Creating reasonable people
                                ~~Advanced
                                    It's very difficult to improve open mindedness, so finding open minded people to start with is very useful for creating very reasonable people.
                            Difficult
                                It's typically very difficult to accurately determine how open-minded people (including yourself) are.
                        How to view
                            Something that typically shouldn't be attempted on your own.
                Epistemic self-confidence
                Maybe change to intellectual self-confidence?
                    Definition
                        Confidence in your reasoning abilities, judgement, etc.
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            Generally you can avoid most of the detriments of low [criticalness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CDQTVmFBdUrFvIU4T3JfvdWZ) by having a relatively low epistemic confidence.
                    Epistemic self-overconfidence
                    A lot of links to this are actually supposed to be links to short-term close-mindedness. Create a temporary overconfidence section and use the dynalist feature to view links to this item in order to fix that.
                        Definition (overconfidence for short)
                            (A propensity towards) overconfidence in one's own reasoning abilities.
                                Overconfidence: an irrationally high credence.
                            "Overconfidence" is in this section used as a shorthand for this specific kind of overconfidence.
                        Importance
                            **Common:**   A massive portion of people are overconfident in this way about many topics.
                                Often even when they appear on the surface to have a lot of epistemic humility, they have just formed surface-level agreeable habits.
                            **Irrationality:**   Overconfidence makes one __far__ less [rational](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=rZcLNKNmVyYYwMjNA5z_NhQs).
                                Overconfidence usually makes one far more [close-minded](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=TBDKCqfm_SEQfc4szPkt4Aqr).
                                Likelihood
                                    Overconfidence usually greatly reduces the effort that one would put into considering how they could be wrong.
                                    Likelihood
                                        This is typically an intuitive response.
                                        Likelihood + Relevance | Has more than you'd think.
                                            We hardly ever consciously decide how much effort to spend on checking whether we're wrong about something.
                                            Likelihood
                                            Therefore our reduced effort doesn't only apply to the depth/breadth of counterarguments we're willing to consider, but to the effort we put into considering __each counterargument individually__.
                                            Relevance
                                                That it's an intuitive response means that it isn't only a mathematical division of effort, but also an emotional inclination to dismiss the possibility of being wrong entirely.
                                                Likelihood
                                                    The emotional inclination to [dismiss the possibility] heavily compels us to get the consideration over with as quickly as possible. 
                                                    Relevance
                                                    ^ Because in this situation we generally subconsciously accept that we're definitely right, and so feel that checking for the possibility that we're wrong is a waste of time.
                                                    Likelihood
                                                        https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ebci0135HVGMAuvAYUa_w1LW
                                                        Likelihood
                                                    Related:   [Confirmatory thought](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OrIjqF9wWhAuAxUvnk078FOy)
                                                This causes the accuracy of the checks to plummet more than proportional to the effort reduction.
                                                Relevance
                                                    Our reduced effort tends to result in us trying to do only the bare minimum to satisfy the scrutiny that other people [are putting/will put] our position under.
                                                    Likelihood
                                                        [Confirmatory thought](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OrIjqF9wWhAuAxUvnk078FOy)
                                                        Relevance
                                                        <Link to how irrational people are about soft reasoning or similar>
                                                        Relevance
                                                            Therefore only very flawed reasoning is typically "necessary".
                                                            Relevance
                                                            ^ And so we tend to have evolved and subconsciously developed strategies of using [rationalizations](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NaE-8bpB1ZDuRzcl_T3nNErK) to defend our positions.
                                                            Relevance
                                                                [Confirmation bias](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=_Dn0UzSi_JF-Rno7Uvb0luL-)
                                                                Likelihood
                                                                And so we're likely to rely on rationalizations while checking to see if our reasoning is flawed.
                                                                Relevance
                                                                ^ And so our check is less likely to be successful in the event that our reasoning is flawed.
                                                                Relevance
                                                    The main point of checking [to see if we're wrong] is to make sure that [system 1 thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ya-MUAgoHP7_PEgC9ypSAe9I) (intuition) isn't leading us astray.
                                                    Relevance
                                                        So relying so heavily on system 1 during the check makes it even less likely for us to find the flaw(s), if there are some.
                                                        Relevance
                                                        ^ It means that we will perform practically no system 2 reasoning on the matter.
                                                        Likelihood
                                                            [Combining system 1 and 2 is more accurate than only 1](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ta-FclRlNYMDkSM7zGdWY9ZM)
                                                            Relevance
                                        When one considers themselves so likely to be correct, they tend not to spend nearly as much effort checking to make sure.
                                        Likelihood
                                            Given such a high likelihood of being correct, spending more than a tiny amount of effort checking for the contrary seems like a waste of time.
                                            Likelihood
                                    [Importance of open mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=3JYkrvEjUHYENVJaDqFSq3Cd)
                                    Relevance
                                [Irrationality is very costly](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=06mMgDCwf1zEQOy-EBOIangh)
                                Relevance
                            Learning about distinguishing between overconfidence and confidence is impactful.
                                People typically can't distinguish between overconfidence and confidence very well.
                                Likelihood
                                It would likely make it much easier to see when you are being overconfident.
                                Relevance
                                    With sufficient [self-awareness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=02ErcrGfQIBieY8kw6Fke-Nc), it can help you recognize the signs in yourself.
                                    Likelihood
                                    Becoming less overconfident is very important.
                                    Relevance
                                        [Overconfidence makes one far less rational](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=YKxHmgAjiY1nQQCjSvq_lIZZ)
                                        Likelihood
                                        ^ [Importance of rationality](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=iDTLYnvT3mQLwAaXj65fNdY3)
                                        Relevance
                                This section can help you learn about it.
                                Relevance
                                    [How overconfidence seems like positive confidence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ityU4yMPEryFxasgaEMZk6tu)
                                    Likelihood
                                    [How epistemic humility seems like a bad lack of confidence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=fbZ_0EpA2GNrjG5Uu_td05-r)
                                    Likelihood
                            Overconfidence tends to increase how much one acts on their ideas.
                                It makes people feel more motivated.
                                Likelihood
                                This helps put their ideas to the test.
                                Relevance
                                There are usually much less costly ways to achieve a similar increase in how much one will act on their ideas.
                                Relevance
                                    [Overconfidence makes one __far__ less rational](https://dynalist.io/d/axyUc8EpOb4NEwUA5hpTKBU1#z=eOF242H0lJpbc2jU8tObt2jd)
                                    Relevance | Make sure this link still works after the move.
                                The efficiency reduction from irrationality resulting from overconfidence is very high.
                                Relevance
                                    So the value of the effort is far less, even if the amount of effort is more.
                                    Relevance
                                Overconfidence can lead to rationalized procrastination.
                                Relevance
                                    So the degree of increased action would likely be less than it otherwise would be.
                                    Relevance
                            Overconfidence has a mixed effect on charisma.
                            Link charisma, and link persuasion to and from charisma.
                                Among people who recognize it as overconfidence, it will generally be seen as very cringey.
                                Relevance
                                    There are some exceptions in the form of people who think it's cute, inspiring, helpful, etc.
                                    Relevance
                                    ^ These people generally fail to see how much of a negative effect overconfidence has on reasoning.
                                    Relevance
                                        So the people who like you most will be people who fail to properly understand one of the most crucial aspects of reasoning.
                                        Relevance
                                        ^ So the people who like you most are likely to be more irrational and overconfident themselves.
                                        Relevance
                                Among people who mistake it for competence, it's generally very charismatic.
                                Relevance
                                    https://dynalist.io/d/axyUc8EpOb4NEwUA5hpTKBU1#z=CZmSIG1xt_tN4899_oU3eVuS
                                    Relevance
                            Overconfidence increases how much counterarguing you'll get from other people.
                                This could help find flaws in your reasoning.
                                Relevance
                                    Generally, overconfidence makes it much harder to recognize flaws in your reasoning.
                                    Likelihood
                                        [Overconfidence makes one far more close-minded.](https://dynalist.io/d/axyUc8EpOb4NEwUA5hpTKBU1#z=HSLkskvXW7D6a6r_R18S7OE7)
                                        Likelihood
                                        Even when the flaws are pointed out explicitly.
                                        Relevance
                                    There are easy and effective alternative methods to achieve this.
                                    Relevance
                                        Eg. Acting overconfident when you actually aren't.
                                        Likelihood
                                        Eg. Surrounding yourself with critical people.
                                        Likelihood | Link criticalness and surrounding yourself with reasonable people
                            **Difficult:**   It's very difficult to avoid overconfidence.
                        The ways in which overconfidence seems like positive confidence
                        Maybe reorganize this
                            It often feels like better reasoning
                                It often feels like one must have good evidence/reasoning to be so confident.
                            It often feels like higher capability
                                It often feels like one must have good evidence/reasoning to be so confident, and therefore that they are very capable.
                            Related
                                
                        The ways in which epistemic humility seems like a bad kind of lack of confidence
                            Lack of intelligence
                                Indication that you didn't calculate properly.
                                (Especially if you had far too much confidence before changing your mind.)
                                ^ This often comes from people thinking that it's much easier to reason in general than it really is.
                                    Everyone is very often wrong all the time.
                                    Move and link here, and connect with [people are bad at "soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qarhXHvwcgUN1n5fnt5sFXck)
                            Lack of backbone
                                It often feels like changing your mind is a sign of weakness/gullibility, even when it isn't.
                        Related
                            [System 1 thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ya-MUAgoHP7_PEgC9ypSAe9I)
                                Usually one of the primary causes of overconfidence is over-reliance on system 1 reasoning.
                            [Heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                                Usually one of the primary causes of overconfidence is overly heavy heuristic usage.
                            [Pride](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=GdWZXDTEkI9DW_5ydfmnRxce)
                            https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubris
                The knife's edge of humility
                Link/combine with emotional/epistemic self-confidence.
                    Definition
                        The issues that epistemic humility can cause.
                    Doubting yourself
                        Doubting yourself epistemically is crucial for good reasoning.
                        Doubting yourself emotionally often leads to insecurity, anxiety, self-hate, and lots of other unhealthy things.
                    Believing in yourself
                        Epistemically believing in yourself tends to lead to frequently assuming you're right and failing to consistently give the possibility that you're wrong adequate consideration.
                        Emotionally believing in yourself is extremely useful for pushing past hard obstacles and bouncing back from bad feelings.
                    The correlation between epistemic and emotional habits
                        Long-term, the habits we form for how to emotionally view things tends to heavily skew how we epistemically view things in a similar way. 
                            Examples
                                If one is emotionally optimistic then they'll likely be epistemically optimistic.
                Related
                    [Measuring epistemic humility](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=IBGJau5KaVc-QdSibLKAw_4g)
                    [Goals of reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yDRmT592xGeslYb-hvYp2ujH)
            Cognitive biases
                Definition
                    Systematic pattern of deviation from rational judgement.
                    Distinction
                        When calling people biased, we are often using "biased" in a different way:
                        Unfair
                            One does not have to be "unfair" in any traditional sense to have some kind of systematic pattern of deviation from rational judgement.
                            ^ Eg. We have evolved to simplify our sensory data in a way that is more efficient to us given our cognitive limitations, resulting in us being consistently trickable with [optical illusions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_illusion).
                        Preferenced
                            One can have an emotional preference for something (that is specific to themselves) without any irrational judgement involved.
                        __Particularly__ biased relative to what we're accustomed to.
                            One can be just as biased (or even far less) than what we are used to, while still having __some__ deviation from rational judgement.
                            ^ [Optical illusions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=9Q0DI4-UOJ2wDg2RWheNrLTF)
                Importance / Limitations
                    Common
                        Cognitive biases are incredibly common.
                        Support
                    Impactful
                        **Cause big reasoning obstacles:**   Cognitive biases of various sorts are involved in all the biggest obstacles to good reasoning.
                        **Stacking effects:**   The effects of multiple cognitive biases can stack on each other, creating exponentially more irrational judgements.
                        **Can't substitute systematic reasoning:**   Learning to avoid specific cognitive biases can't be a complete substitute for [systematic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7)
                            If it's treated as a complete substitute, there is a significant risk of substantial [Uncriticalness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HFlajn-7ApUXftkQMGXZHVWq) due to still relying on [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                    Improvable
                        **Bias variety indicates solution variety:**   People vary in how strongly they exhibit different biases, so there should be a host of ways to influence how biased we are.
                        **Learning helps avoid:**   Learning about specific cognitive biases helps develop methods for recognizing and combating many specific cognitive biases.
                        **Bias blind spot:**   Due to the [bias blind spot](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qYKn9LjoeqFhKAwabwW925I7), studying biases can in fact make you __more__ vulnerable to [overconfidence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=BXkvTggpu62KYL_Vo7PXrNnB) and [confirmation bias](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=_Dn0UzSi_JF-Rno7Uvb0luL-).
                            Because it can help you see the influence of cognitive biases all around you—without seeing the influence in yourself.
                                https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0Twe0jxOkPS7EBmXHOxaQnBm
                            Richard F. West, Russell J. Meserve, and Keith E. Stanovich, “Cognitive Sophistication Does Not Attenuate the Bias Blind Spot,” __Journal of Personality and Social Psychology__ 103, no. 3 (2012): 506.
                Common cause
                    Cognitive biases arise when the corners cut with [heuristics](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=49rqRClTn-Vx2v-ZXXm90ukK) result in a relatively consistent and discrete mistake. 
                Bias blind spot
                    Definition
                        A special blind spot towards our own biases, which applies even when we can correctly identify the biases of others.
                    Importance
                        Common
                            In a survey of 76 people waiting in airports, individuals rated themselves much less susceptible to cognitive biases on average than a typical person in the airport. In particular, people think of themselves as unusually unbiased when the bias is socially undesirable or has difficult-to-notice consequences.
                                Emily Pronin, Daniel Y. Lin, and Lee Ross, “The Bias Blind Spot: Perceptions of Bias in Self versus Others,” __Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin__ 28, no. 3 (2002): 369–381.
                            Other studies find that people with personal ties to an issue see those ties as enhancing their insight and objectivity; but when they see __other people__ exhibiting the same ties, they infer that those people are overly attached and biased.
                        Impactful
                            The bias blind spot, unlike many biases, is __especially severe__ among people who are __especially intelligent, thoughtful, and open-minded__.
                                Not to be confused with people who think they’re unusually intelligent, thoughtful, etc. because of the illusory [superiority bias](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=cjbQO8yswEZPcz1DSN_OENAs).
                                Richard F. West, Russell J. Meserve, and Keith E. Stanovich, “Cognitive Sophistication Does Not Attenuate the Bias Blind Spot,” __Journal of Personality and Social Psychology__ 103, no. 3 (2012): 506.
                            Studying biases can make you more overconfident and susceptible to confirmation bias.
                            [Learning about biases can make us more overconfident](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=tnS7Qx3CE7MMeNj2iCv-gShl)
                                Therefore learning how this works, how to recognize it, etc. is incredibly important.
                                Relevance
                        Difficult
                            [Learning about biases can make us more overconfident](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=tnS7Qx3CE7MMeNj2iCv-gShl)
                                Bias blind spot would ostensibly apply to itself, so we must be very careful with how we go about learning to avoid it.
                    Causes
                        We’re especially loath to think of our views as inaccurate compared to the views of others.
                        We fail to detect any “biased-feeling thoughts” when we [introspect](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=aqX299sRiWRL4uS29eOaKCGW), and so draw the conclusion that we must just be more objective than everyone else.
                            Joyce Ehrlinger, Thomas Gilovich, and Lee Ross, “Peering Into the Bias Blind Spot: People’s Assessments of Bias in Themselves and Others,” __Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin__ 31, no. 5 (2005): 680–692.
                        https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=02ErcrGfQIBieY8kw6Fke-Nc
                    Source
                        https://www.readthesequences.com/Biases-An-Introduction
                Base rate neglect
                    Definition
                        The fallacy where we ground our judgements in how intuitively "normal" a combinatino of attributes is, neglecting how common each attribute is in the population at large.
                        Example
                            Is it more likely that Steve is a shy librarian, or that he’s a shy salesperson? Most people answer this kind of question by thinking about whether “shy” matches their stereotypes of those professions. They fail to take into consideration how much more common salespeople are than librarians—seventy-five times as common, in the United States.
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_rate_fallacy)
                    Importance
                        Improvable
                            There are effective [countermeasures](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=beXBS7l0arptnCenIIQfz8aa)
                    Reducible
                        [Representativeness heuristic](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HFS-yoqzJcX2UK10bI3AB6p4)
                    Countermeasures
                        Thinking of probabilities as frequencies of objects or events.
                            [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HZGCoVF3YvM](This video) shows a visual technique that can help with this.
                Confirmation bias
                    Definition
                        Giving more weight to evidence for confirming what we already believe (in itself).
                        Distinction
                            The "in itself" is very important here. without that, we might be tempted to object: "We ought to give more weight to evidence that confirms what we believe, as it fits in better with our model of reality in that case, doesn't it?"
                            ^ Confirmation bias occurs when it is [the **mere fact** that it confirms are beliefs] that makes us give it more weight, and not any indirect factors such as the abductive weight provided by [it fitting into our model of reality well].
                        [Example and source](https://youtu.be/SoCqftOYHX4)
                    Importance
                        Common
                            [Wason selection task](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wason_selection_task)
                                A very small number of people (~4-10%) are able to complete the task successfully, when it is presented ordinarily.
                                    **"ordinarily":**  (i.e. Without prior knowledge about its difficulty, and with abstract elements.)
                                [We tend to be able to perform well at the task when it is presented in a social context.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wason_selection_task#Policing_social_rules)
                                    Whether this is the result of instinctual or learned processes, either way it seems obviously dependent on [heuristics](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=49rqRClTn-Vx2v-ZXXm90ukK) which are not ordinarily active in more abstract variations.
                        Impactful
                            It easily tricks people into denying important logical consequences of new information.
                        Improvable
                            We can effectively practice systematic methods of reasoning that account for and largely avoid most forms of biased thinking.
                            Needs support.
                            We can learn much more consistent means of accounting for [past reasoning that may have been forgotten].
                        Difficult
                            Merely knowing about a bias is rarely enough to protect you from it.
                                In a study of __bias blindness__, experimental subjects predicted that if they learned a painting was the work of a famous artist, they’d have a harder time neutrally assessing the quality of the painting. And, indeed, subjects who were told a painting’s author and were asked to evaluate its quality exhibited the very bias they had predicted, relative to a control group. When asked __afterward__, however, the very same subjects claimed that their assessments of the paintings had been objective and unaffected by the bias—in all groups!
                                ^ Katherine Hansen et al., “People Claim Objectivity After Knowingly Using Biased Strategies,” __Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin__ 40, no. 6 (2014): 691–699.
                                ^ Similarly, Pronin writes of gender bias blindness. 
                                    In one study, participants considered a male and a female candidate for a police-chief job and then assessed whether being “streetwise” or “formally educated” was more important for the job. The result was that participants favored whichever background they were told the male candidate possessed (e.g., if told he was “streetwise,” they viewed that as more important). Participants were completely blind to this gender bias; indeed, the more objective they believed they had been, the more bias they actually showed.
                                    ^ Eric Luis Uhlmann and Geoffrey L. Cohen, “‘I think it, therefore it’s true’: Effects of Self-perceived Objectivity on Hiring Discrimination,” __Organizational Behavior and Human Decision Processes__ 104, no. 2 (2007): 207–223. 
                                Source for this section
                                    https://www.readthesequences.com/Biases-An-Introduction#footnote3
                    Reducible
                    Indicators
                        Definition
                            Indications that you may be under the influence of cognitive bias(es).
                        Someone is claiming that you are being close-minded or failing to take into account an obvious fact.
                        Rejecting a proposition that you likely do not fully understand.
                    Suggested countermeasures
                        Systematic reasoning
                            Learning (and practicing) to [reason systematically](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7) (at least for important decisions).
                            This minimizes the room for all biases (confirmation bias included) to creep in.
                        [Reduce over-reliance on intuition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OUAhqDVNYRgxVu2oBtqGswCr)
                        Only visit this now if you're heavily focusing on learning countermeasures.
                        Recognition
                            Practice recognizing when confirmation bias is likely occurring.
                            We are typically very bad (and overconfident) at recognizing when a bias is affecting us.
                                So this method should likely not be relied on, at least on its own.
                                [Bias blind spot](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qYKn9LjoeqFhKAwabwW925I7)
                        Considering alternatives
                            Practice considering alternative possibilities that feel wrong in situations where you lack very solid conscious reasoning to justify your feelings.
                        Disproving
                            Create the habit of (for non-trivial problems) considering whether it would be easier to solve the problem via trying to disprove what you're ultimately trying to prove.
                            Software debugging
                                To think of your ideas and beliefs as software you're actively trying to find problems with, rather than things to be defended.
                                If they should be defended given the information you'll have, you should naturally realize this without needing emotionally assume that they are true.
                                It's easy to get into habits that make you __feel__ like you're doing this, without actually properly doing it.
                                    So there should be some feedback mechanism to practice this, if you intend to use this method.
                        ["Blind" techniques](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CLmXQEO4-lPT5O56n2jbZsSC
                        Only visit this now if you're heavily focusing on learning countermeasures.
                        Related
                    Potential evolutionary explanations
                        It tended to be more efficient for multiple people with competing perspectives to focus on reasoning for their own perspective rather than for multiple perspectives, and then to use that reasoning to competitively try to influence the overall tribe. In the absence of biases specific to the topic in question, the crowd would tend to overall come to an agreement on the better justified position this way, with the "researchers" often only needing to do enough work to represent the best reasons they could give for their own perspective.
                        The past importance of making rapid decisions in uncertain situations.
                        Confirmation bias implicitly accounts for (in some sense) past reasoning that may have been forgotten, since we ostensibly arrived at our initial belief through reasoning that we may or may not remember.
                        We evolved to __act__ reasonable more than __be__ reasonable.
                        Create/link
                    Myside bias
                    Synonym of confirmation bias? I recall it being different
                        Definition
                            A bias toward searching and interpreting evidence in a manner that tends to favor the hypothesis we want to be true.
                Groupthink
                PC ~Beginner, link in framing/etc., deal with duplicate in "environment" or sociology category.
                    Definition
                        The bias to let the social dynamics of a group situation override the most reasonable choice.
                        Synonym
                            Informational social influence
                        [Example and source](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=n1A9vrsw6Hw)
                    Importance
                        It's common
                            It's often used in advertising.
                        It can lead us to deny important logical consequences of the available information in favor of the socially accepted answer.
                            This is a cognitive bias that plagues many people
                                Dissent can be uncomfortable and dangerous to one's social standing, and so often the most confident or first voice will determine group decisions.
                                
                        We can learn much more consistent means of accounting for the reasoning that others have performed, such as gaining an in-depth understanding of why they hold different opinions.
                    Reducible
                        Partially reducible to [anchoring/framing effects](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=gqfxRHs-DUYtXdVA2pFuNEji).
                            Social norms are a heavy anchor/frame.
                                [Overton Window](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uN0SA7RJNS9CHXfwu6uBCV9g)
                    Indicators
                        Your belief initially differs from the relevant social group and the subject of the beliefs is heavily reliant on ["soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=VqyTyvLNbJ6WtfifAe0egZtQ).
                        Fix link
                        The reasoning that other(s) provide(s) seems flawed.
                    In-group favoritism
                    PC Link stuff from importance in in-group vs out-group or something.
                        Definition
                            The propensity for people, under certain conditions, to prefer and have affinity for one's in-group over the out-group, or anyone viewed as outside the in-group.
                        Importance
                            Common and impactful
                                How we perceive the actions of others is frequemtly and substantially affected by in-group favoritism.
                                    People may perceive the same action very differently depending on whether the action was executed by a member of the same group or a member of a different group.
                                    People tend to evaluate actions of their own group or team members much more favorably than those of outgroup members.
                                    This phenomenon was demonstrated in an empirical study conducted by Molenberghs and colleagues in 2013.
                                        In the study, participants were arbitrarily divided into two teams where they watched videos of individuals of competing teams and individuals from their own team perform hand actions. Participants were then asked to judge the speed of the hand movements.
                                        On average participants judged members of their own teams to be faster, although the hand movements were the exact same speed across the board.
                                    Similarly, Hastorf and Cantril conducted a pioneering study in 1954, where students of both Princeton and Dartmouth viewed a contentious football game between their two teams.
                                        Although they had watched the same motion picture of the game, their versions of what transpired were so starkly different it appeared as though they had watched two totally different games.
                            Common
                                This can be expressed in one's evaluation of others, linking, allocation of resources, and many other ways.
                            Improvable
                                Elements of favoritism are flexible in that they can be erased by changes in social categorization.
                                    This change is known as [recategorization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recategorization).
                                    Probably make a separate section for this and link or something.
                        Potential evolutionary explanation
                            In-group favoritism was likely selected for the advantages of [coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coalition) affiliation.
                        Related
                            [Information bubbles](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Mb-QFAcGf_sctWRPSrJ6Ubpp)
                    Suggested countermeasures
                    In roughly descending order of effectiveness.
                        Learning (and practicing) to reason in a systematic way (at least for important decisions)
                            This minimizes the room for all biases (confirmation bias included) to creep in.
                        [Reduce over-reliance on vague intuition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OUAhqDVNYRgxVu2oBtqGswCr)
                        Learn about (and practice recognizing) cognitive biases and other common flaws in reasoning to be able to better recognize when it seems that others are using flawed reasoning to arrive at their conclusions.
                            
                            Other flaws (fallacies?)
                        [Facilitate group critical thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=eFQRxyWWj9LJQipxpfkDcZbr)
                        Getting better at measuring how much ["soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=VqyTyvLNbJ6WtfifAe0egZtQ) is involved in a topic.
                        Practice recognizing when groupthink is likely occurring.
                            Limitations
                                This likely won't be very effective because it's difficult to practice this in a reliable way.
                            
                        Common links
                            
                    Potential evolutionary explanations
                        The importance of social cohesion for our ancestors.
                        The tendency for flaws in reasoning to sort of "even out" for most hypotheses, making the majority's opinion more reliable usually.
                    Related
                        [Overton Window](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uN0SA7RJNS9CHXfwu6uBCV9g)
                        [Isolating yourself from unreasonable people](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=pqLJHM1DMjTBOlRsyi6vQORW)
                            Reasonable people are much less likely to apply many of the kinds of social pressures that encourage groupthink.
                            Relevance
                                Therefore complete isolation would likely not be necessary to be able to greatly reduce groupthink via isolating from unreasonble people.
                                Relevance
                Anchoring/framing effects
                    Definition
                        The bias to consider values in the context of other presented values.
                        Example
                            Today only, you can get a new Toyota for 20% off the sticker price! So act now and drive your new car away today!
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            Especially impactful when it biases us to consider an overly-limited range of potential values.
                        Common
                            Incredibly commonly abused by marketers and salespeople.
                        Improvable
                            We can learn to consider unpresented values to compare something with.
                                Many of the most common and important unpresented values are fundamentally pretty easy to account for.
                                    Example: [prior probability](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=p27oF333OJQDubxwMG2_gtog)
                    Failure to (sufficiently) account for unknowns
                        Definition
                            **Unknowns:**  Unknown factors relevant to the claim(s) you're considering.
                        [Importance](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NQqQgRCBkQi_8NvWlOhzlMob)
                        Causes
                            [Reasoning short-cuts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QbiCWrDMPM38d8BpMPryvG49)
                                Typically we simply do not have the time to account for all the biggest potential unknowns. We instead heavily rely on unreliable reasoning short-cuts and engage in [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37), which tends not to be the best way to consistently account for the biggest potential unknowns.
                            [Unsystematic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7)
                                We typically lack the time to investigate arguments in a systematic fashion, which would typically involve (or at least help with) accounting for all the biggest potential unknowns.
                            
                                Often when considering other peoples' claims (particularly those from those we are close with), we will be less [critical](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HFlajn-7ApUXftkQMGXZHVWq) and more agreeable.
                        Solutions
                            Many specific strategies to account for unknowns are in [accounting for unknowns](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=cXfd8G1zK1UZ_HGp2aSVkGmK)
                            [Systematic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7)
                        Related
                            [Accounting for unknowns](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=cXfd8G1zK1UZ_HGp2aSVkGmK)
                    Less-is-better effect
                        Definition
                            The cognitive bias to favor a lesser option when it is presented separately, but to prefer the better option when both are presented together.
                            Examples
                                A person giving a $45 scarf (from scarves ranging from $5-$50) as a gift was perceived to be more generous than one giving a $55 coat (from coats ranging from $50-$500);
                                An overfilled ice cream serving with 7 oz of ice cream was valued more than an underfilled serving with 8 oz of ice cream;
                                A dinnerware set with 24 intact pieces was judged more favourably than one with 31 intact pieces (including the same 24) plus a few broken ones.
                            [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Less-is-better_effect)
                    The availability heuristic
                        Definition
                            The bias to assume that the likelihood of something is more heavily correlated with how readily it comes to mind (its “salience”) than it actually is, or with more confidence than is justified.
                        Bias towards how well we can initially imagine something
                            Causes
                                When we can better initially imagine something, it typically feels more real, which makes it feel more likely to be true.
                            Related
                                [Bias towards the specific/"grounded"](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mks1gpdiHB01sMtJ2DSU-1Xn)
                        Bias towards the stated
                            Related
                                [False dilemma](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HgqsuPlUw6Vw0iQlDlryh4oB)
                        Causes
                            Often an over-reliance on our [system 1 thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ya-MUAgoHP7_PEgC9ypSAe9I) to provide good and relevant evidence, when it merely provides what we've formed the strongest relevant associations about.
                            PC copy/move to subconscious and link here
                        Related
                            [Heuristics](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=49rqRClTn-Vx2v-ZXXm90ukK)
                    Related
                        [Assuming that others feel the same as us](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uOOadpgv2pwwmCpjInvAhEFN)
                            Likely often caused by an anchoring/framing effect.
                        [False dilemma](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HgqsuPlUw6Vw0iQlDlryh4oB)
                Wishful thinking
                    Definition
                        Bias towards emotionally appealing people/ideas/etc. and bias against emotionally unappealing people/ideas/etc.
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            
                        [Importance of "defending sub/un-conscious"](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Po037OookU67GxF_ILSnIc90)
                    Forms
                        Defending sub/un-conscious
                            Definition
                                Bias towards believing (and especially defending) what we unconsciously or subconsciously did.
                            Importance
                                Common
                                    Virtually everyone does it.
                                    ^ 
                                Impactful
                                    
                            How to abuse this
                                Make one feel like the belief you want them to have is their own idea (i.e. embed it in their un/sub-conscious). Once they feel this way, they will be much more likely to adopt/defend it themselves, possibly even if it goes against their previous belief(s).
                        Projecting desires
                        This seems very similar to the parent.
                            Definition
                                The bias to project our own desires onto reality and fool ourselves into thinking that reality conforms to how we would like it to be.
                    Potential evolutionary explanation
                        The conscious as our "PR team"
                            Humanity was (and still is) an irrational species, in a way that gradient decent promoted.
                                A more rational species would require very specific moral/axiological axioms in order to consistently act in advantageous ways, and due to the way that our brains evolved, there's no easy way to evolve such axioms, only emotional instincts towards simplistic patterns roughly representing those axioms.
                            In order to motivate humans towards certain ideal actions (eg. mating more capable people), they developed metrics for judging features like capability.
                            However since humans were so irrational, their judgement was highly susceptible to trickery.
                            So natural selection supported the evolution of a mechanism that convinces people to view us in a better light than they otherwise would.
                            Our bias to defend our actions, even when they were our un/sub-conscious actions and there's no justifiable defense, is plausibly a result of this.
                    Related
                        
                        
                Bias towards the specific/"grounded"
                ~~Amateur
                    Definition
                        We feel like things presented in a more specific/"grounded" way are more likely to be accurate.
                        i.e.
                            We are biased against vague/"ungrounded" things.
                        Examples
                            One presents us with a realistic hypothetical and all the necessary reasoning to support it, but we still treat it like it is "just a hypothetical/conceptual thing" and consider it substantially less likely to be truthful.
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            [Theoretical reasoning importance](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ES3dSwo0NpH94kCkD6KVMiQi)
                        Common
                    Reducible
                        [Heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                            We often develop the heuristic that more vague/abstract evidence is less reliable.
                            ^ Partially because they are more prone to being interpreted in a biased manner.
                                And this is generally accurate, vague/abstract things allow for more possibilities, thus allowing us to fill those gaps with our biases.
                                However there are major exceptions to this heuristic, such that relying on it to give more than a minor indication of how to treat vague/abstract evidence is sure to lead to major mistakes.
                        [Bias towards how well we can initially imagine something](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=hnFpbjCcECkbgFcHNQupthnG)
                            When we have an explicit example (especially when it is tied to an emotion that compels us to accept the fact), we can typically imagine it more clearly.
                            Relevance
                            Our imagination often has an easier time with more explicit/detailed descriptions.
                        Over-reliance on [System 1 thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ya-MUAgoHP7_PEgC9ypSAe9I)
                            The bias towards the specific/"grounded" would not be very influential, except that we tend to trust our [system 1 thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ya-MUAgoHP7_PEgC9ypSAe9I) (especially our initial intuition) far too much.
                            Relevance
                    How to abuse this
                        [Characterization](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=-RfRZ7650bJF8cl3dEY8_zE_)
                    Related
                        [Theoretical reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=a__A4WdOE_LwKq1rrTZjz2Rm)
                Attribution errors
                Fix terms of sections under this and refine
                    Definitions
                    Fix
                        1. The underemphasis of situational and environmental factors and overemphasis on dispositional or personality factors for the behavior of other(s).
                        2. The overemphasis of dispositional or personality factors for the behavior of one's self.
                        i.e.
                            An error where one tends to overattribute the behaviors of others to their personality (e.g., he is late because he's selfish) and underattribute them to the situation or context (e.g., he is late because he got stuck in traffic).
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                        Common?
                            It is (at least according to my Wikipedia skim) seemingly substantially contested how common these errors are.
                    Failures
                        Other-personality overemphasis
                        Self-environment overemphasis
                    Successes
                        Other-environment overemphasis
                        Self-personality overemphasis
                Sunk cost fallacy
                    Definition
                    Improve this definition a bit.
                        Feeling committed to things you’ve spent resources on in the past, when you should be cutting your losses and moving on.
                Duration neglect
                    Definition
                        A bias that occurs when we evaluate an experience without regard for how long it lasts.
                    Countermeasures
                        Depicting duration graphically.
                            Michael J. Liersch and Craig R. M. McKenzie, “Duration Neglect by Numbers and Its Elimination by Graphs,” __Organizational Behavior and Human Decision Processes__ 108, no. 2 (2009): 303–314.
                        Directing more attention to duration.

                Mere exposure effect
                ~~Adept
                    Definition
                        The phenomenon whereby individuals develop a preference for things merely due to repeated exposure.

                Distinction bias
                ~~Intermediate
                    Definition
                        The tendency to view two options as more distinctive when evaluating them simultaneously than when evaluating them separately.
                        Examples
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distinction_bias)
                    Importance
                        Common
                    Countermeasures
                        Independent evaluation
                            Definition
                                An evaluation which avoids comparing two options directly and instead considers each option individually and makes an overall assessment of each one on its own, and then compares the assessments.
                            Limitation
                                Can easily involve us accidentally imagining differences that don't actually exist.
                                    For example by thinking of a new way to utilize a common factor present in both options only while considering the second option. We may fail to account for how this factor would also improve the experience of the first option, if we do not keep the first option in mind.
                        [Systematic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7)
                            By being systematic, we can largely avoid relying on heuristics from which distinction bias emerges.
                Gambler's fallacy
                ~Intermediate
                    Definition
                        The belief that, if an event (whose occurrences are independent and identically distributed) has occurred less frequently than expected, it is more likely to happen again in the future (or vice versa).
                        Synonyms
                            Monte Carlo fallacy
                            Fallacy of the maturity of chances
                        Distinctions
                            Events whose probabilities are explicitly not independent.
                                The gambler's fallacy does not apply when the probability of different events is not independent. 
                                In such cases, the probability of future events can change based on the outcome of past events, such as the statistical permutation of events.
                                Example
                                    When cards are drawn from a deck without replacement. If an ace is drawn from a deck and not reinserted, the next card drawn is less likely to be an ace and more likely to be of another rank. The probability of drawing another ace, assuming that it was the first card drawn and that there are no jokers, has decreased from ⁠4/52 (7.69%) to 3/51⁠ (5.88%), while the probability for each other rank has increased from 4/52 (7.69%) to 4/51⁠ (7.84%).
                            Events whose probabilities are not independent due to hidden factors.
                                For example, if a coin is flipped 21 times, the probability of 21 heads with a fair coin is 1 in 2,097,152. Since this probability is so small, if it happens, it may well be that the coin is somehow biased towards landing on heads, or that it is being controlled by hidden magnets, or similar. In this case, the smart bet is "heads" because Bayesian inference from the empirical evidence — 21 heads in a row — suggests that the coin is likely to be biased toward heads. Bayesian inference can be used to show that when the long-run proportion of different outcomes is unknown but exchangeable (meaning that the random process from which the outcomes are generated may be biased but is equally likely to be biased in any direction) and that previous observations demonstrate the likely direction of the bias, the outcome which has occurred the most in the observed data is the most likely to occur again.
                            Events whose probabilities change
                                If external factors are allowed to change the probability of the events, the gambler's fallacy may not hold. For example, a change in the game rules might favour one player over the other, improving their win percentage. Similarly, an inexperienced player's success may decrease after opposing teams learn about and play against their weaknesses. This is another example of bias.
                        Example
                            The belief that the next dice roll is more likely to be six than is usually the case because there have recently been fewer than the expected number of sixes.
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gambler%27s_fallacy)
                        Pull more from this starting with origins
                    Importance
                        Common
                            It is a major contributing factor to the amount of people who gamble.
                    Limitation
                        In most illustrations of the gambler's fallacy and the reverse gambler's fallacy, the trial (e.g. flipping a coin) is assumed to be fair. In practice, this assumption may not hold.
                    Reverse position
                        After a consistent tendency towards tails, a gambler may also decide that tails has become a more likely outcome. This is a rational and Bayesian conclusion, bearing in mind the possibility that the coin may not be fair; it is not a fallacy. Believing the odds to favor tails, the gambler sees no reason to change to heads. However, it is a fallacy that a sequence of trials carries a memory of past results which tend to favor or disfavor future outcomes.
                        Inverse gambler's fallacy
                            Examples
                                A situation where a gambler entering a room and seeing a person rolling a double six on a pair of dice may erroneously conclude that the person must have been rolling the dice for quite a while, as they would be unlikely to get a double six on their first attempt.
                                A game of roulette at the Monte Carlo Casino on August 18, 1913, when the ball fell in black 26 times in a row. This was an extremely unlikely occurrence: for any given sequence of 26 spins, the probability of either red or black occurring 26 times in a row on a single zero roulette wheel is around 1 in 68.4 million, assuming the mechanism is unbiased. Gamblers lost millions of francs betting against black, reasoning incorrectly that the streak was causing an imbalance in the randomness of the wheel, and that it had to be followed by a long streak of red.
                    Retrospective gambler's fallacy
                        Definition
                            A similar bias for inferences about unknown past events based upon known subsequent events.
                            Examples
                                To observe multiple successive "heads" on a coin toss and conclude from this that the previously unknown flip was "tails".
                    Causes
                        [Hasty generalization](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ecu_HuUAoK7nIZBDmynRINzl)
                        [Representativeness heuristic](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HFS-yoqzJcX2UK10bI3AB6p4)
                Low-ball technique
                ~Advanced
                    Definition
                        A two-stage compliance technique in which the influencer secures agreement with a request but then increases the size of that request by revealing hidden costs.
                    Importance
                        It's a common technique
                        It can allow people to manipulate you or people you are about.
                        You can learn to be harder to manipulate this way.
                        It can be used to manipulate other people for your benefit.
                    Indicators
                    Suggested countermeasures
                    In roughly descending order of importance.
                    Potential evolutionary explanation
                Foot-in-the-door technique
                ~Expert
                    Definition
                        A two-stage compliance technique in which the influencer sets the stage for the real request by first getting the person to comply with a much smaller request.
                        [Example and source](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1x19z5Jb_pg)
                    Importance
                        It can allow people to manipulate you.
                            This is a technique that works on a lot of people.
                        You can learn to be harder to manipulate this way.
                            Learning patterns to watch out for and calculating important decisions consciously.
                            Adept
                        It can be used to manipulate other people for your benefit.
                            
                    Indicators
                        A small request is quickly followed up by a much larger one.
                    Suggested countermeasures
                    In roughly descending order of importance.
                        Learning (and practicing) to reason in a systematic way (at least for important decisions)
                            This minimizes the room for all biases (confirmation bias included) to creep in.
                            
                        
                        Practice recognizing when this technique is likely being used.
                    Potential evolutionary explanation
                Minor biases
                ~Advanced
                    Ending positivity
                        Definition
                            The bias to be positive about how an event went at the end of the event.
                [Hot/cold biases](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=e6Z-xzc6uDwhxxlrgZhYFwN7)
