            System 1 thinking
                Definitions
                    Subconscious
                    Synonyms
                        Intuition
                        Vibes
                        Emotions (in some cases)
                        System 1 thinking
                    Distinction
                        Not conscious reasoning.
                            Can still be rational or have rational components.
                    [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subconscious)
                Importance / Limitations
                    Impactful
                        It tends to heavily contribute to bad reasoning
                            Peoples' ["soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=VqyTyvLNbJ6WtfifAe0egZtQ) habits tend to rely too heavily on system 1 thinking.
                            Likelihood & relevance
                                Peoples' common ["Soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=VqyTyvLNbJ6WtfifAe0egZtQ) habits tend to focus on vibes over the reasoning itself.
                                Likelihood
                                Peoples' common ["Soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=VqyTyvLNbJ6WtfifAe0egZtQ) habits often apply even to formal reasoning, albeit to a lesser extent.
                                Likelihood
                                [Importance of "soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=jPysip7brKTtf9PYUPBWVJ1I)
                                Relevance
                            Relying on vibes/intuition too much tends to make us much worse at reasoning.
                            Likelihood & relevance
                                Frequently ignoring relevant reasoning is bound to lead to a whole host of fallacies, but one of the most straightforward and common ones is [Strawmanning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=j7YUoP-36fiXLnITfI6f1Tl5)
                                Add non-strawmanning stuff here, very important!
                                    If you focus on vibes over content, you're going to strawman people because focusing on vibes over content entails a lack of focusing on the content of what they're actually saying.
                                The subconscious begins with in-built fundamental processes that are very epistemically unideal.
                                    Overcoming these is not easy.
                                    [Our brains evolved to reason badly](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mkjCEhDKSSE1q2KpLj9SH4Oj)
                                A plausibly correct intuition is typically a plausibly incorrect one as well, and the cost of checking is typically pretty small and good for building up better reasoning habits for more important decisions.
                            During many important decisions, we often become impulsive, in significant part due to trusting intuition too much.
                            Likelihood & relevance
                                If we did not trust intuition so much, we would usually internalize that our emotions not very trustworthy in those areas, and thus usually not be as impulsive.
                                Likelihood
                        Common
                            Reasoning habits often rely on vibes/intuition a lot.
                            Likelihood
                                https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qSM0l5Zj-kIBKAbPujHYYidX
                                Likelihood
                                https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=zG_I7j1EH7y2eLtxTBpqml6a
                                Likelihood
                        [Our intuition can be trained to reason pretty well.](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=LNNPBQDfQgBSVNFinWhv4mbG)
                    Improvable
                        A combination of system 2 and system 1 reasoning is almost always __far__ more accurate than just system 1 reasoning.
                            People almost always have a set of habits that relies much more on system 1 and much less on system 2 than would be ideal.
                            Relevance
                        Our intuition can be trained to reason pretty well
                            Teaching your intuition to reason well is typically much harder than it seems.
                            Relevance
                                By default it is subject to a massive host of [cognitive biases](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=a4amRVifCY8jNCgNpW6j6zB9)
                                Likelihood
                                The subconscious cannot "think" in a general sense*, so it will usually handle situations it doesn't have any specific training for badly.
                                Likelihood
                                    * This may be technically untrue.
                                    ^ Our subconscious/unconscious may be able to learn to think in a very consistent and general sense if we trained it in an extremely specific way. However this is likely not realistic, as it would be incredibly easy for the subconscious/unconscious to learn the wrong things even if you were incredibly epistemically knowledgeable. Because the in-built evolutionary instincts are very broad, epistemically unideal and difficult to overwrite or sidestep.
                How to view it
                    Fast if-then tree
                        The subconscious comes from a part of the brain that's far faster than other parts. It recognizes patterns (typically simple ones), and immediately spits out the associated result. In other words, it is essentially a network of [heuristics](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=49rqRClTn-Vx2v-ZXXm90ukK) that is applied immediately, without inherent questioning.
                        With adequate training, it can basically act as a big if-then tree that runs super fast and pretty consistently. That means if you do the same process a lot, in the absence of other factors, your subconscious can often learn how to do more and more of that process until your conscious isn't needed at all for even a pretty high level of performance. This is so effective that the vast majority of our behavior is governed by subconscious processes.
                    Minor indication
                        In general: a minor indicator that should not be blindly trusted for remotely important decisions, but should likely be investigated.
                    [A helpful video on intuition](https://youtu.be/1f6N2UrCK6o)
                        Note that this is mostly focused on the benefits of intuition, so it may give a positively skewed impression of it.
                Causes of over-reliance on system 1
                    Rationalization
                        Definition
                            The process of constructing seemingly logical, socially acceptable excuses to justify behaviors, decisions, or feelings that are actually driven by unrecognized, irrational or unacceptable motives.
                            **Often:**   Unknowingly.
                            Distinction (from good reasoning)
                                While good reasoning typically applies logic to find a conclusion, rationalization starts with a predetermined conclusion (usually an emotional impulse) and works backward to invent justifications in an irrational manner.
                                [Assuming that other(s) are wrong](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=525oYS_TiO4rZW4aUGvWXESU)
                        Importance
                            Common
                            Impactful
                        Instinctual rationalization
                            We tend to have very strong instincts to consciously defend our intuitive/subconscious reasoning/decisions to the death, even when we consciously believe otherwise.
                            Create/link to section(s) on why this is the case.
                        Causes
                            It acts as a cognitive mechanism to protect self-esteem and alleviate guilt.
                                Helps the ego defend against anxiety and discomfort. It allows individuals to protect their self-image after making a poor decision or experiencing a setback
                        Anti-rationalization strategies
                            Learning to recognize rationalization
                                [Learning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XwNwxOcZRgH_hY46vX9t5UTb)
                                [Self-awareness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=02ErcrGfQIBieY8kw6Fke-Nc)
                                [Bias blind spot](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qYKn9LjoeqFhKAwabwW925I7)
                            "If this reason were enough, what else would have to be true?"
                                Definition
                                    The process of checking a suspected rationalization by asking "if this were true, what else would have to be true?"
                                    Example
                                        "I can't respect a girl if she goes home with me on the first date."
                                            The rationalization: "It's risky, given how little she knows about him."
                                            What else would have to be true: "Let's say a friend of yours goes out at night in a dangerous part of town. That's risky, would you then lose respect for them?"
                            "If the emotional aspect wasn't there, would I think differently?"
                                Definition
                                    The process of checking a suspected rationalization by asking "If the emotional aspect weren't present, would I think differently?"
                                    Example
                                        Asking someone out
                                            You're thinking of asking an attractive stranger out. But you're nervous and afraid of being rejected, and then think to yourself "I don't have time for dating anyway, I should just focus on my schoolwork."
                                            Considering if the emotion wasn't present: "If I knew for sure that they're interested in me too, would I still think it isn't worth approaching them because I don't have time for it?"
                                    Distinction
                                        This doesn't mean that the emotional aspect should never be given any weight.
                                            Typically, there's a reason (even if a bad one) why our emotions are leaning in some direction.
                                            The reason may be good, even if we can't immediately think of why.
                                                Eg. In the [asking someone out example](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QVjtZjfd0iDCadnYEoVrK6Pc), rejection may make you feel significantly worse for a few days or even weeks in a way that isn't worth it.
                                                    Perhaps the coming days/weeks are quite important to feel good in, or perhaps you're sufficiently confident you can find a partner of similar quality with less costly strategies later on.
                                            However it should still likely typically be scrutinized, to avoid getting into the habit of relying so heavily on [system 1 thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ya-MUAgoHP7_PEgC9ypSAe9I) as to trust reasons that are likely rationalizations.
                                                https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Bl3wKaMf5c2daJkgAQEcPkOp
                                Importance
                                    Helpful for investigating the actual weight of the emotion(s) on your decision-making, when compared with the reason(s) your brain conjures up.
                                        If the weight of the emotion is far greater than the reason, the reason is very likely a rationalization.
                                        Relevance
                                    Typically reduces the degree to which we would be compelled by our emotions towards a certain kind of reasoning, by removing the emotional element.
                            Checking for emotional convenience
                                Definition
                                    Checking to see if the reason is emotionally convenient, especially if it protects us from some emotional threat.
                                    [Example: Asking someone out](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=-XDUDPbKjlM3FEDEJLIa7GG0)
                                        "I don't have time for dating anyway, I should just focus on my schoolwork." is an emotionally convenient excuse in this scenario, it protects you from the possibility of being rejected.
                                Importance
                                    Emotional convenience is correlated with likelihood of a reason being a rationalization.
                                    ^ Rationalization tends to occur as a result of our emotions driving us towards a conclusion.
                                    Likelihood
                            "If this reason were not true, would I think differently?"
                                Definition
                                    The process of checking a suspected rationalization by asking "If this were not true, would I think differently?"
                                    [Example: Asking someone out](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QVjtZjfd0iDCadnYEoVrK6Pc)
                                        Same example, except instead of checking what you would think if the emotional aspect wasn't there, you check what you would think if the reason wasn't present.
                                        Specifically: "If I did have time and didn't have to worry about schoolwork, would I still think it isn't worth asking them out?"
                                Importance / Limitations
                                    Impactful
                                        It's typically better to [check for the lack of the emotional aspect](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Py1-2VklYhuNpH_hl4f0YxM5) instead.
                                            [Typically reduces emotional influence more](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ghExX9EFB-aIOp3J0HBSzhzZ)
                                        It's easy for another rationalization to slip in with this strategy.
                                            "but I have this other reason!"
                                                Our brain can come up with a practically infinite amount of rationalizations if it subconsciously feels necessary to. So being able to come up with more reasons that appear (on the surface) to support your position is not in itself very good reason to trust the position.
                                            "I'm sure it would be different!"
                                                Instinctually self-assuring yourself with the thought/proclaimation that it would be different (and thus, you can feel like you aren't just rationalizing), without actually properly thinking through whether it would be different or not.
                            Source
                                [How to spot a rationalizaton (4 min video)](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=tfp34-ruvGE)
                                    Heavy inspiration, practically plagerizing.
                        Related
                            [Confirmatory thought](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OrIjqF9wWhAuAxUvnk078FOy)
                            [Explaining system 1](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Srymy9g8BcuTG-UVoOZfLZwY)
                    Trusting emotions
                        Importance / Limitations
                            Impactful
                                Our emotions are unreliable
                                    Definition
                                        We can almost never trust our initial emotions to pick a reliable confidence value for claims in areas we are inexperienced in, or topics involving a lot of ["soft" reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=VqyTyvLNbJ6WtfifAe0egZtQ).
                                    Link to section(s) about subconscious/intuition being unreliable.
                        Instinctual trust
                            Definition
                                Our tendency to automatically react and place higher/lower confidence on things that emotionally feel more/less trustworthy.
                            Limitations
                                Impactful
                                    Hard to resist because of intensity
                                        We often feel like certain ideas are obviously certain, absurd, unacceptable, etc. when really we have nowhere near enough evidence to justify that feeling.
                                    Hard to resist because of some common habits
                                        Emotional incentives
                                            There are significant emotional incentives to form such habits.
                                            
                                            [Being "right"](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=SEY-nxGd5c0jjzwT_16t4yWc)
                                                When one blindly trusts in their emotions in areas where they feel like they can't solidly be shown to be wrong, they can enjoy the feeling of being "right" and evade the feeling of being "wrong".
                                                    A countermeasure to this is to become decent enough at automatically recognizing when you're shown to be wrong, even when it wouldn't feel solid to the vast majority of people.
                                                    This is __much__ harder than it seems and it's incredibly easy to think you're much better at this than you really are.
                                                This incentive likely often effects trust in emotions subconsciously.
                                                    It seems likely that the short-term feelings of discomfort with proper truth seeking and comfort in the following of emotions would (over time) subconsciously influence many people to start trusting their emotions more.
                                        Instructed
                                            We're often told to, and thus implicitly guided to form habits around doing it.
                                            "Don't think about it"
                                            "You'll know what to do"
                                            "Trust your gut"
                                            "Follow your heart"
                        Related
                            https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NaE-8bpB1ZDuRzcl_T3nNErK
                                Often we trust emotions because we feel like we can adequately rationalize them.

                        Step 1: Recognition
                            Definition
                                Learning to recognize when you're making important decisions based on vibes/subconscious reasoning without adequately consciously checking that reasoning.
                            Importance
                                Impact
                                Difficulty
                                    Learning to recognize it consistently is very hard.
                                        If It was easy, lots more people would do it and there would be far less disagreement on subjects requiring good general reasoning.

                                Definition
                                    Learning roughly what over-reliance on subconscious/intuition looks like.
                                Importance
                                    Most people don't have a great handle on even roughly what it looks like.

                                                    It often overestimates the value of many things.
                                                        Immediate ability to guess how you feel.
                                                            You can usually (and probably usually should) communicate how you feel to them, so they can understand you in individual cases even without being able to guess how you feel. 
                                                            You can usually communicate how you feel to them enough over the course of a while for them to learn the patterns required to guess how you feel, especially if you explicitly ask them to and explain why it's important. So them not being able to guess how you feel short-term isn't great evidence that they can't guess it long-term.

                How it works
                    Analogy
                        We are made up of two kinds of cognition. A rider (controlled processes, conscious reasoning) and an elephant (automatic processes, including emotion and intuition).
                        Automatic processes run the human mind, just as they have been running animal minds for 500 million years, so they’re very good at what they do (instinctual, quick decision-making based on survival and reproduction), like software that has been improved through thousands of product cycles.
                        The rider evolved to do several useful things. It can see further into the future (because we can examine alternative scenarios in our heads) and therefore it can override the elephant's propensity to aim for short-term/intuitive benefits, to make better decisions when better but unintuitive ones are available.
                            The rider can guide the elephant to learn new skills and master new technologies, when the elephant would by itself rarely have the discipline for that.
                        The rider acts as the spokesman for the elephant, even though the rider doesn’t necessarily know what the elephant is thinking. The rider is skilled at fabricating post hoc explanations for whatever the elephant has just done, and it's good at finding reasons to justify whatever the elephant wants to do next. This was incredibly socially advantageous, it allowed us to defend lots of our socially unacceptable actions and mistakes much more effectively, by essentially turning us into fantastic unconscious liars.
                        This analogy becomes a little misleading in that we might be tempted to think of the rider and the elephant as having different desires, but this is not exactly correct in the intuitive sense.
                            Typical emotions and desires are all [controlled by the elephant](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yP6X4DaMABkdUQV3tpI7NRpZ), our conscious reasoning does not have emotional desires of its own.
                            However it can have its own distinct __goals__ separate from the "goals" of the elephant, albeit likely ultimately based on the desires of the elephant.
                            Link to psychological hedonism.
                            The "goals" of the elephant are really just collections of behaviors that (typically) were generally evolutionarily beneficial in the past.
                        Source
                            Taken from "The Righteous Mind" by Jonathan Haidt.
                    The subconscious/intuition works by learning and using patterns and associations.
                        We especially tend to associate things with positive or negative emotions and use these associations to decide what to do.
                            <broken link>
                    Our feelings are largely controlled by our subconscious/intuition.
                    The subconscious seems to learn to mimic what you do, albeit in a simplistic and flawed way.
                        <broken link>
                    <broken link>

                    How to practice this
                        Finding useful patterns
                            Useful patterns are often in useful subjects and involving at least one useful idea.
                            Useful patterns are often very general, involving many things you know about. It's often not so important to find links that are immediately important as it is to constantly build up the web of links to recognize large patterns.
                        Frequently look for similar/connected things/concepts and try to find plausibly useful patterns.
                Explaining system 1 reasoning
                    Definition
                        Trying to explain/justify your system 1 intuition.
                    Importance / Limitations
                        Common
                        Impactful
                            Difficulty explaining indicates bad reasoning
                                If you feel like you can't explain the reasoning behind system 1 thinking even after a substantial amount of trying, then that's a strong sign that the intuition is unjustified, and __barely__ a sign that you're only unable to articulate your thoughts well.
                                    If it was just an articulation problem, you should generally still be able to justify the intuition well, albeit in an inefficient, inarticulate manner.
                                    ^ Providing sufficient justification for something almost never requires good articulation.
                                        If your intuition is well-founded, you should be able to identify the reasoning why.
                                        ^ Because in the time it takes to adequately train your intuition about the topic, you should be able to learn the vast majority of the reasoning that your intuition relies on.
                    Related
                        [Rationalization](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NaE-8bpB1ZDuRzcl_T3nNErK)
                            The most common cause of trying to explain system 1 thinking.

                Causes
                    Emotions occur in steps that involve a rough, simplified appraisal of whether something that just happened advanced or hindered your goals or evolutionary goals.
                    Small flashes of positive or negative feeling that prepare us to approach or avoid something. Every emotion (such as happiness or disgust) includes an affective reaction, but most of our affective reactions are too fleeting to be called emotions.
                    Affective reactions are so tightly integrated with perception that we find ourselves liking or disliking something the instant we notice it, sometimes even before we know what it is.
                Social Emotions
                altin didnt read yet
                    Definition
                        Emotions that depend upon the thoughts, feelings or actions of other people, "as experienced, recalled, anticipated, or imagined at first hand".
                        Examples
                            <broken link>
                            <broken link>
                            <broken link>
                            <broken link>
                            <broken link>
                            <broken link>
                            Admiration/coolness?
                            [Elevation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elevation_(emotion))
                        Distinction
                            Basic emotions such as happiness and sadness don't depend upon the thoughts, feelings or actions of other people.
                        Synonym
                            Social emotions are sometimes called moral emotions, because they are often considered to play an important role in morality and moral decision making.
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_emotions)
                    Development
                        The development of social emotions is tightly linked with the development of social cognition, the ability to imagine other people's mental states, which generally develops in adolescence.
                        Children as young as 2 to 3 years of age can express emotions resembling guilt and remorse.
                        Five-year-old children are able to imagine situations in which basic emotions would be felt.
                        Seven year old children can describe situations in which social emotions might be experienced.
                        Studies using fMRI have found that different brain regions are involved in different age groups when performing social-cognitive and social-emotional tasks. While brain areas such as medial prefrontal cortex (MPFC), superior temporal sulcus (STS), temporal poles (TP) and precuneus bordering with posterior cingulate cortex are activated in both adults and adolescents when they reason about intentionality of others, the medial PFC is more activated in adolescents and the right STS more in adults. Similar age effects were found with younger participants, such that, when participants perform tasks that involve theory of mind, increase in age is correlated with an increase in activation in the dorsal part of the MPFC and a decrease in the activity in the ventral part of the MPFC were observed. Studies that compare adults with adolescents in their processings of basic and social emotions also suggest developmental shifts in brain areas being involved. Comparing with adolescents, the left temporal pole has a stronger activity in adults when they read stories that elicit social emotions. The temporal poles are thought to store abstract social knowledge. This suggests that adult might use social semantic knowledge more often when thinking about social-emotional situations than adolescents.
                    Self-conscious emotions
                        Definition
                            A variety of <broken link> that relate to our sense of self and/or our consciousness of others' reactions to us.
                            Examples
                            Distinction
                                They have five distinct features that differentiate them from other emotions:
                                ^ Require self-awareness and self representation
                                ^ Emerge later than basic emotions
                                ^ Facilitate attainment of complex social goals
                                ^ Do not have distinct universally recognized facial expressions
                                ^ Cognitively complex
                        Importance
                            Impactful
                                Social benefits
                                    Social harmony
                                        Self-conscious emotions are seen to promote social harmony in different ways. The first is its ability to reinforce social norms. It does this in a very similar way to that of [operant conditioning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operant_Conditioning). Performing well in situations while keeping to social norms can elicit pride. This feels good so therefore encourages the behaviour to be repeated. Equally performing in a situation while not sticking to the social norms can leave individuals feeling [embarrassed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embarrassment). This feels bad and is generally avoided in the future. An example of this is a study (Brown, 1970) where participants were shown to choose avoiding feelings of embarrassment over financial gains.
                                        <broken link>
                                    Social healing
                                        Self-conscious emotions enable social healing. When an individual makes a social error, feelings of guilt or embarrassment changes not just the person’s mood but their body language. In this situation the individual gives out non-verbal signs of submission and this is generally more likely to be greeted with forgiveness. This has been shown in a study where actors knocked over a supermarket shelve (Semin & Manstead, 1982). Those that acted embarrassed were received more favorably than those who reacted in a neutral fashion.
                                        Levels of embarrassment have found to be easier to see in females and African-Americans, than compared to male and Caucasian targets (Keltner, 1995). This is due to social learnings from previous generations.
                                    Behavior regulation
                                        There is a strong link between the ability of an individual to regulate their behaviour in an appropriate manner and problems with their self-conscious emotions. A school was able to list a set of boys who were classified as ‘prone to aggression and delinquent behaviour’. When these boys sat an interactive IQ test, they scored higher on scores of anger compared with the normal boys at the school. They also scored lower in feelings of embarrassment (Keltner, 1995).
                        Development
                            During the second year of life, new emotions begin to emerge when children gain the understanding that they themselves are entities distinct from other people and begin to develop a sense of self.
                                These emotions include:
                                <broken links>
                            Self-conscious emotions are among the latter of emotions to develop.
                                Two reasons are at the cause of this:
                                ^ Body language
                                    Emotions such as joy, fear and sadness can all be gathered reliantly on just a person’s face.
                                    However self-conscious emotions heavily involve the body in addition to the face.
                                        (Darwin, 1965)
                                    This means that when humans are attempting to learn self-conscious emotions, they have more to tend to, making the emotions harder to grasp.
                                ^ Self-awareness
                                    Due to the nature of these emotions, they can only begin to form once an individual has the capacity to self-evaluate their own actions. If the individual decides that they have caused a situation to occur, they then must decide if the situation was a success or a failure based on the social norms they have accrued, then attach the appropriate self-conscious feeling (Weiner, 1986). This is a complex cognitive skill, one that takes time to master.

                        Shame
                        Refine
                            Definition
                                An emotion often associated with negative self-evaluation; motivation to quit; and feelings of pain, exposure, distrust, powerlessness, and worthlessness.
                                [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shame)
                            Importance
                                Impactful
                                    Often drives people to hide or deny their perceived failures or faults.
                            Common causes
                            It can bring about profound feelings of deficiency, defeat, inferiority, unworthiness, or self-loathing. Our attention turns inward; we isolate from our surroundings and withdraw into closed-off self-absorption. Not only do we feel alienated from others but also from the healthy parts of ourselves. This inward focus can intensify self-criticism and contribute to further emotional distress.[3] The alienation from the world leads to painful emotions and self-deprecating thoughts and inner anguish.[4] Empirical research demonstrates that it is dysfunctional for the individual and group level.[5] Shame can also be described as an unpleasant self-conscious emotion that involves negative evaluation of the self.[6] Shame can be a painful emotion that is seen as a "...comparison of the self's action with the self's standards..." but may equally stem from comparison of the self's state of being with the ideal social context's standard. In Western, individualistic contexts, shame is often conceptualized as an internal failure of the self, whereas collectivist cultures may interpret shame as a relational emotion tied to maintaining social harmony or fulfilling obligations to family and community.[7][8] In some East Asian settings, shame can be experienced vicariously, felt on behalf of one’s social group or family.[9] According to Neda Sedighimornani,[10] shame is relevant in several psychological disorders such as depression, phobia of social interactions, and even some eating disorders. Some scales of shame measure it to assess emotional states, whereas other shame scales are used to assess emotional traits or dispositions- shame proneness.[11] "To shame" generally means to actively assign or communicate a state of shame to another person. Behaviors designed to "uncover" or "expose" others are sometimes used to place shame on the other person. Whereas, having shame means to maintain a sense of restraint against offending others (as with modesty, humility, and deference). In contrast to having shame is to have no shame; behaving without restraint, offending others, similar to other emotions like pride or hubris. This distinction between “having shame” and “being shamed” also function both as a social regulator and as a punitive tool within communities.
                            blushing, confusion of mind, downward cast eyes, slack posture, and lowered head
                            Shame is a self-punishing acknowledgment of something gone wrong.[13] It is associated with "mental undoing". Studies of shame showed that when ashamed people feel that their entire self is worthless, powerless, and small, they also feel exposed to an audience—real or imagined—that exists purely for the purpose of confirming that the self is worthless. Shame and the sense of self is stigmatized, or treated unfairly, like being overtly rejected by parents in favor of siblings' needs, and is assigned externally by others regardless of one's own experience or awareness. An individual who is in a state of shame will assign the shame internally from being a victim of the environment, and the same is assigned externally, or assigned by others regardless of one's own experience or awareness.
                            The person experiencing shame might not be able to, or perhaps simply will not, identify their emotional state as shame, and there is an intrinsic connection between shame and the mechanism of denial.[14] " The key emotion in all forms of shame is contempt (Miller, 1984; Tomkins, 1967). Two realms in which shame is expressed are the consciousness of self as bad and self as inadequate.[15] People employ negative coping responses to counter deep rooted, associated sense of "shameworthiness".[16] The shame cognition may occur as a result of the experience of shame affect or, more generally, in any situation of embarrassment, dishonor, disgrace, inadequacy, humiliation, or chagrin.[17]The dynamics of shame and devaluation appear to be consistent across cultures. This has led some researchers to propose the existence of a universal human psychology related to how we assign value and worth. This applies both to us and to others.
                            Behavioral expression
                                Physiological symptoms caused by the autonomic nervous system include blushing, perspiration, dizziness, or nausea. A feeling of paralysis, numbness, or loss of muscle tone might set in making it difficult to think, act, or talk. Children often visibly slump and hang their head. In an effort to hide this reaction, adults are more likely to laugh, stare, avoid eye contact, freeze their face, tighten their jaw, or show a look of contempt. In another's presence, there's a feeling of being strange, naked, transparent, or exposed, as if wanting to disappear or hide.[19][20] The Shame Code was developed to capture behavior as it unfolds in real time during the socially stressful and potentially shaming spontaneous speech task and was coded into the following categories: (1) Body Tension, (2) Facial Tension, (3) Stillness, (4) Fidgeting, (5) Nervous Positive Affect, (6) Hiding and Avoiding, (7) Verbal Flow and Uncertainty, and (8) Silence.[21] Shame tendencies were associated with more fidgeting and less freezing, but both stillness and fidgeting were social cues that convey distress to the observer and may elicit less harsh responses. Thus, both may be an attempt to diminish further shaming experiences. Shame involves global, self-focused negative attributions based on the anticipated, imagined, or real negative evaluations of others and is accompanied by a powerful urge to hide, withdraw, or escape from the source of these evaluations. These negative evaluations arise from transgressions of standards, rules, or goals and cause the individual to feel separate from the group for which these standards, rules, or goals exist, resulting in one of the most powerful, painful, and potentially destructive experiences known to humans.
                            Types of shame
                                Existential
                                    Occurs when we become self-aware of an objective, unpleasant truth about ourselves or our situation.
                                Situational
                                    The feeling we have when violating an ethical principle, interpersonal boundary, or cultural norm.
                                Class
                                    Relates to social power and pertains to skin color, social class, ethnic background, and gender and occurs in societies that have rigid caste stratifications or disparate classes.
                                Narcissistic
                                    Occurs when our self-image and pride are wounded, affecting how we feel and think about ourselves as an individual, in contrast as a member of a group
                                ...
                                Unwanted exposure
                                    Definition
                                        Something personal that we would like to keep private is unexpectedly revealed, or when we make a mistake in [a] public [setting].
                                        Examples
                                            You are called out in front of a whole class for doing something wrong.
                                            Someone sees you doing something you did not want them to see.
                                        This is what you would normally think of when you hear the word "shame".
                                Unrequited love
                                    Unreciprocated love that causes yearning for more complete love.
                                    Common causes
                                        When you love someone but your partner does not reciprocate, or one is rejected by somebody that they like; this can be mortifying and shaming.
                                    An experiment called "The Still Face Experiment" was done where a mother showed her baby love and talked to the baby for a set period of time. She then went a few minutes without talking to the baby. This resulted with the baby making different expressions to get the mother's attention. When the mother stopped giving the baby attention, the baby felt shame. 
                                    According to research on unrequited love, people tend to date others who are similar in attractiveness, leaving those less attractive to feel an initial disappointment that creates a type of unrequited love in the person.
                                Disappointed expectation
                                    The feeling of dissatisfaction that follows the failure of expectations or hopes to manifest.
                                    Examples
                                        Not passing a class
                                        Having a friendship go wrong
                                        Not getting a big promotion in a job that you thought you would get.
                                Exclusion
                                    Being left out of connection or involvement with others or groups that we would like to belong to.
                                    also means being left out. Many people will do anything to just fit in or want to belong in society, e.g., at school, work, friendships, relationships, everywhere.
                                ...
                                Internalized shame
                                    Definition
                                        Internalized shame reflects deep-seated beliefs of inadequacy that feel permanent and irreversible and are accompanied by words, voices, and images.
                                        Distinction
                                            Different from an acute short-lived experience of shame.
                                    Importance
                                        Impactful
                                            Once internalized, the original shaming event(s) and beliefs need not be recalled nor be conscious.
                                            Later experiences of shame are intensified and last longer. 
                                            They do not require an external event or another person to trigger associated feelings and thoughts and can cause depression and feelings of hopelessness and despair.
                                            It also causes "shame anxiety," which makes people apprehensive about experiencing shame.
                                    Causes
                                        Internalized shame stems from chronic or less frequent severe experiences of shame occurring with prior trauma or in childhood. It can take over a child's emotions and identity and continue into adulthood or may gradually increase over time.
                                Vicarious shame
                                    Definition
                                        The experience of shame on behalf of other person(s). 
                                    Individuals vary in their tendency to experience vicarious shame, which is related to neuroticism and to the tendency to experience personal shame.
                            Collectivist shame
                            Link in sociology or something, and maybe move under types of shame.
                                In collectivist cultures, shame is tied with social harmony, family honor, and group reputation. Chinese cultures are influenced by Confucian values and experience shame as a tool to regulate moral conduct and social cohesion.[7] Japanese individuals, similiarily, encounter both personal shame and shame felt for the actions of others within their social groups, highlighting the communal aspect of shame in collectivist contexts.[8] Differences still exist among collectivist cultures. Analyses of language corpora from multiple cultures demonstrate cross-cultural variation in how shame, guilt, and embarrassment are conceptualized and expressed. Even within East Asia, semantic and philosophical interpretations of shame diverge. Korean shame is very closely associated moral failure and the protection of family honor, with an individual's shame reflecting throughout their household.[9] In Japan, shame is tied to maintaining social harmony and fufilling expected social roles, and resulting in feelings of embarrassment or proxy shame when group norms are disrupted.[9] While shame may be a universal self-conscious emotion, it can operate as a social function depending on the cultural norm.
                            Distinguishing shame from similar emotions
                                Importance
                                    Distinguishing between shame, guilt, and embarrassment can be challenging.
                                Distinguishing guilt
                                    According to cultural anthropologist Ruth Benedict, shame arises from a violation of cultural or social values while guilt feelings arise from violations of one's internal values.[24] Thus shame arises when one's 'defects' are exposed to others, and results from the negative evaluation (whether real or imagined) of others; guilt, on the other hand, comes from one's own negative evaluation of oneself, for instance, when one acts contrary to one's values or idea of one's self.[25] Shame is more attributed to internal characteristics and guilt is more attributed to behavioral characteristics.[26] Thus, it might be possible to feel ashamed of thought or behavior that no one actually knows about (because one is afraid of what they find), and conversely, feeling guilty about the act of gaining approval from others.Psychoanalyst Helen B. Lewis argued that, "The experience of shame is directly about the self, which is the focus of evaluation. In guilt, the self is not the central object of negative evaluation, but rather the thing done is the focus."[27] Similarly, Fossum and Mason say in their book Facing Shame that "While guilt is a painful feeling of regret and responsibility for one's actions, shame is a painful feeling about oneself as a person."[28]Following this line of reasoning, Psychiatrist Judith Lewis Herman concludes that "Shame is an acutely self-conscious state in which the self is 'split,' imagining the self in the eyes of the other; by contrast, in guilt the self is unified."[29]Clinical psychologist Gershen Kaufman's view of shame is derived from that of affect theory, namely that shame is one of a set of instinctual, short-duration physiological reactions to stimulation.[30][31] In this view, guilt is seen as a learned behavior consisting primarily of self-directed blame or contempt, and the shame that results from this behavior, making up a part of the overall experience of guilt. Here, self-blame and self-contempt mean the application, towards (a part of) one's self, of exactly the same dynamic that blaming of, and contempt for, others represents when it is applied interpersonally.Kaufman saw that mechanisms such as blame or contempt may be used as a defending strategy against the experience of shame and that someone who has a pattern of applying them to himself may well attempt to defend against a shame experience by applying self-blame or self-contempt. This, however, can lead to an internalized, self-reinforcing sequence of shame events for which Kaufman coined the term "shame spiral".[30] Shame can also be used as a strategy when feeling guilty, especially when the hope is to avoid punishment by inspiring compassion.
                                Distinguishing embarrassment
                                    One view of difference between shame and embarrassment says that shame does not necessarily involve public humiliation while embarrassment does; that is, one can feel shame for an act known only to oneself but to be embarrassed one's actions must be revealed to others. In the field of ethics (moral psychology, in particular), however, there is debate as to whether or not shame is a heteronomous emotion, i.e., whether or not shame does involve recognition on the part of the ashamed that they have been judged negatively by others.Another view of the dividing line between shame and embarrassment holds that the difference is one of intensity.[33] In this view embarrassment is simply a less intense experience of shame. It is adaptive and functional. Extreme or toxic shame is a much more intense experience and one that is not functional. In fact, according to this view, toxic shame can be debilitating. The dividing line then is between functional and dysfunctional shame. This includes the idea that shame has a function or benefit for the organism.[34]Immanuel Kant and his followers held that shame is heteronomous (comes from others); Bernard Williams and others have argued that shame can be autonomous (comes from oneself).[35][36] Shame may carry the connotation of a response to something that is morally wrong whereas embarrassment is the response to something that is morally neutral but socially unacceptable. Another view of shame and guilt is that shame is a focus on self, while guilt is a focus on behavior. Simply put: A person who feels guilt is saying "I did something bad.", while someone who feels shame is saying "I am bad".[37]Embarrassment has occasionally been viewed as a less severe or intense form of shame, which usually varies on different aspects such as intensity, the physical reaction of the person, or the size of the present social audience, but it is distinct from shame in that it involves a focus on the self-presented to an audience rather than the entire self.[26] It is experienced as a sense of fluster and slight mortification resulting from a social awkwardness that leads to a loss of esteem in the eyes of others. Embarrassment has been characterized as a sudden-onset sense of fluster and mortification that results when the self is evaluated negatively because one has committed, or anticipates committing, a gaffe or awkward performance before an audience. So, because shame is focused on the entire self, those who become embarrassed apologize for their mistake, and then begin to repair things and this repair involves redressing harm done to the presented self.[13]One view of difference between shame and embarrassment says that shame does not necessarily involve public humiliation while embarrassment does; that is, one can feel shame for an act known only to oneself but to be embarrassed one's actions must be revealed to others. Therefore shame can only be experienced in private and embarrassment can never be experienced in private.[13] In the field of ethics (moral psychology, in particular), however, there is debate as to whether or not shame is a heteronomous emotion, i.e. whether or not shame does involve recognition on the part of the ashamed that they have been judged negatively by others. This is a mature heteronomous type of shame where the agent does not judge herself negatively, but, due to the negative judgments of others, suspects that she may deserve negative judgment, and feel shame on this basis.[38] Therefore, shame may carry the connotation of a response to something that is morally wrong whereas embarrassment is the response to something that is morally neutral but socially unacceptable.

                                Sociology
                                Probably move most or all of this to sociology and link it here.
                                    According to the anthropologist Ruth Benedict, cultures may be classified by their emphasis on the use of either shame (a [shame society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guilt%E2%80%93shame%E2%80%93fear_spectrum_of_cultures)) or guilt to regulate the social activities of individuals. Shame may be used by those people who commit [relational aggression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relational_aggression) and may occur in the workplace as a form of overt social control or aggression. Shaming is used in some societies as a type of punishment, shunning, or ostracism. In this sense, "the real purpose of shaming is not to punish crimes but to create the kind of people who don't commit them".

                        Embarrassment
                        Related
                            Frontotemporal lobar degeneration
                                Frontotemporal lobar degeneration (FTLD) is a [neurodegenerative disease](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neurodegenerative_disease) that attacks the brain selectively in the frontal lobe, temporal lobe and [amygdala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amygdala). Patients suffering from FTLD offer information on the biological complexity involved in generating self-conscious emotions. With the use of a startle experiment (where patients and control participants are exposed to an unexpected and loud sound) it has been shown that sufferers of FTLD show and experience the basic negative emotions expected to be attached to the startling sounds. However they show significantly less signs of experiencing self-conscious emotions compared to control groups. This is due to an inhibition of [embarrassment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embarrassment) caused by the damaged brain (Sturm & Rosen, 2006).
                                The ability to show basic emotions while lacking the ability to perform the more complex self-conscious emotions demonstrates that self-conscious emotions are biologically harder to perform than average emotions. FTLD patients tend to struggle in social situations (Sturm & Rosen, 2006). This is again linked with their inability to perform self-conscious emotions adequately.
                    Defensiveness
                        Definition
                            Psychological self-protection used in an attempt to ward off perceived threats, criticism or shame. 
                        Importance / Limitations
                            Impactful
                                
                        Causes
                            When one feels unjustly accused, it's common for them to fish for excuses so that the person who they feel unjustly accused by will back off.
                                More accurately, it's likely that one subconsciously follows the "advice" of a previously commonly learned habit which tells them to fish for excuses. And the habit is typically learned because it tends to have helped with getting people to back off in previous cases. 
                            When one feels accused (especially unjustly), it's common to feel misunderstood, attacked, stressed, etc.
                                Which can often cause anger, which can often cause [impulsivity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ghD22JG6DO01dRavfcanfIYs).
                                Which can often cause [social withdrawal](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=eifa30Mr_JzDmKtBSW1swo5B).
                        Recognizing defensive feelings
                            Definition
                                Frequently practicing thinking about how you feel during and after your discussions.
                                I think this is easier in text, because there's less pressure to respond immediately and more natural time for reflection, and you can look back over the messages you sent.
                            Importance / Limitations
                                Impactful
                                    Crucial for [preventing defensiveness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=d5aMFPJNTagqE-3KO89FGDh-).
                                        [Preventing defensive feelings is important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HsdSacyuDhDM9n41_Xn7jK2b).
                                        Relevance
                                    Crucial for [addressing defensive feelings](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=cLEzyxQ79Cg_ihyVlzluxgpi).
                                        [Addressing defensive feelings](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=K3-_A86nX8A3yr4j-YsoLIxv) is important.
                                Difficult
                                    [People don't tend to be very good with self-awareness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ocsBqG0JTPgES1WfMoMGb7Gk).
                                    Ironically, the more emotional we get, the harder it becomes to reflect like this.
                                        This kind of reflection is the kind of auxillary process that our brain is wired to (by default) shut down in favor of more efficiently attacking/defending (whether emotionally or otherwise).
                                        Link this to evolutionary section.
                                        It typically takes a lot of practice to overcome that instinct to be able to recognize it consistently in real-time.
                            Strategies
                                During the discussion ask yourself questions about yourself.
                                    Examples
                                        How tense am I? If I'm tense, why?
                                        How long am I spending thinking about this? How much time should I be spending?
                                After a discussion, look back over it and ask yourself questions about yourself.
                                    Importance
                                        It makes it easier to recognize and deal with your emotions.
                                            This builds up habits for thinking about how you're feeling and gives you a better understanding of how your emotions work, which makes it easier to recognize and deal with them.
                                        It's typically easier to do retroactive self-analysis than real-time self-analysis.
                                            You're usually less defensive in the future looking back.
                                    Examples
                                        How was I feeling when I said that?
                                        If negative, why was I feeling that way?
                            Related
                                [Self-awareness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=02ErcrGfQIBieY8kw6Fke-Nc)
                        Preventing defensive feelings
                            Definition
                                Preventing defensive feelings from arising or sustaining (as strongly) in at least some cases.
                                Distinction
                                    Not the same as [ignoring](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qcuSSeqHqOV-skymlWBLrXhf) or [repressing](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NOPn06eFCiAI1WnxcQwKHLMl) defensive feelings or making yourself suffer.
                                    Not the same as [addressing defensive feelings](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=cLEzyxQ79Cg_ihyVlzluxgpi), which is less about resolving the emotions and more about working around them.
                            Importance / Limitations
                                Impactful
                            Strategy
                                Typically the main strategy is understanding the primary [causes](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qenn0boc4gHKBedikLWTyyaq) to learn to [recognize defensive feelings](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=U22yH5CUuOIMUy0MIBGdwSXj) and [avoid causes](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=w2gZVkev0-poImf01hYsbbkc) where worthwhile.
                        Addressing defensive feelings
                            Definition
                                Distinction
                                    Not the same as [preventing defensive feelings](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=d5aMFPJNTagqE-3KO89FGDh-), which is less about working around the emotions and more about resolving them.
                            Importance / Limitations
                                **Common:**   It's often very important to ensure we can address defensive feelings effectively.
                                **Ignoring emotions:**   If this is done __instead__ of [preventing defensive feelings](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=d5aMFPJNTagqE-3KO89FGDh-) rather than alongside it, then it's typically a form of [ignoring emotions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qcuSSeqHqOV-skymlWBLrXhf).
                                    Ignoring emotions [tends to cause a lot of issues](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=4CGQCYFNDW5VpkRTZAnuo6e0).
                            Strategies
                                The below strategies rely on being able to [recognize when you're feeling defensive](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=U22yH5CUuOIMUy0MIBGdwSXj)
                                Reduce impact on reasoning
                                    Definition
                                        Reduce the impact that defensive feelings have on reasoning.
                                    Practice habits for [self-awareness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=02ErcrGfQIBieY8kw6Fke-Nc) about how defensiveness might be impacting your reasoning.
                                        Whenever you're feeling even a little defensive, practice thinking about:
                                        1. How your points might be influenced by your emotions.
                                        2. How some crucial epistemic steps might have been skipped because your emotions are compelling you to be more reactionary.
                                            **Related:**   [Thoroughness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=lj9jejrk2s5bCXgZLKLHwWkZ)

                    Empathy
                    Expand this a lot
                        **Definition:** The ability or propensity to feel others' emotions.
                        Importance / Limitations
                            Common
                                Almost everyone feels empathy, usually a substantial amount of it.
                            Impactful
                                Tends to be based on a lot of irrational subconscious beliefs/associations.
                                    This makes it very dangerous to rely on.
                                    Relevance
                                        It makes it so our empathy can be leveraged for irrational ends.
                                        Likelihood
                                            It is usually the case that many of our irrational subconscious beliefs/associations can be leveraged for virtually any end. So if our empathy is based on that, our empathy too can likely be leveraged for virtually any end.
                                            Likelihood
                                        If it is very dangerous to rely on, then it is very important to learn how to avoid relying on it in important cases.
                                        Relevance
                                Heavy reliance tends to result in [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37).
                                    Our empathy is largely dependent on our https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=r2u3zb8iZbtZCj5aZBVmU0ON , therefore relying on empathy tends to mean relying on our subconscious/intuition. And [our subconscious/intuition is based on heuristics](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yXhXm2F6GcWOS8hNsMQfe-xS), therefore heavy reliance on empathy tends to result in heavy heuristic usage.
                                    Likelihood
                                        [Most emotions are largely dependent on our vibes/intuition](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yP6X4DaMABkdUQV3tpI7NRpZ) , therefore empathy likely is too.
                                        Likelihood
                                Tends to have a very strong effect on peoples' behavior.
                        Managing <broken link>
                            Reducing in-group empathy
                                Often very helpful for reducing many biases and epistemic issues related to in-groups.
                                Can make it more difficult to intuitively understand others.
                            Increasing out-group empathy
                                Can counteract the bias from in-group empathy.
                                Can help with intuitively understanding others.
                                Can create new biases in favor of out-groups.
                        Sociopathy / psychopathy
                            Definition
                                The state of lacking empathy, either partially or completely.
                            Importance
                                Impactful

                            Evolution
                            Link and expand
                            Experience
                                We tend to empathize more with things that have a high capacity to experience things like emotions (especially pain/suffering).
                            Low agency
                                We tend to empathize more with things that have low agency (especially low ability to prevent themselves from going through pain/suffering, i.e. helplessness).
                            Unpredictable
                                We tend to empathize more with things that we can't predict.
                                Example
                                    If a shape merely moves in a circle, we feel like it's programmed, mechanical, **experienceless**.
                                    If a shape moves in a way that feels very unpredictable, then we tend to feel like it's alive, experiencing and choosing to make these movements.
                                        Especially if even its form(s) of unpredictability feels unpredictable.
                                This could also be due to feeling like if something can't be predicted, it must have an experiential element guiding its processes because systems lacking such an experiential element tend to be more predictable.
                            Clear work -> value trade
                                We tend to empathize more with things which allow us to trade work for value.
                                Maybe because it feels more like a relationship.
                        Anthropomorphism
                            Definition
                            Causes
                                Empathy
                                We tend to anthropomorphize more when we desire positive social connection or control over our environment.
                                    The idea is that one attempts to escape social loneliness by trying to make one's own social connections. As such (the idea goes), we tend to anthropomorphize objects in a positive way.
                                    Anthropomorphization often brings a kind of fictional clarity that sometimes feels comforting to us.
                                This is perhaps a process of inductive inference where we subconciously guess at the experiential nature of an object using the model we're most familiar with, our own.
