        In practice
        Create categories for many of the items here
            Definition
                Reasoning in practice; Practical reasoning.
                This category is full of practical problems, techniques, strategies and misc info about the nature of reasoning in practice.
            Importance
                **Informal:**   Practical reasoning is very useful for informal reasoning.
                Link informal reasoning
                    Importance of systematic reasoning
                    Likelihood | Link
                    Importance of heuristics
                    Likelihood | Link
                **Formal:**   Practical reasoning can help even in very formal contexts.
                    We cannot (at least ordinarily) be literally certain that an argument is valid.
                    Likelihood | Exchange for link
                        We could always be making a mistake while calculating whether it is valid.
                        Likelihood | Move
                        So lots of practical reasoning can substantially increase our chances of being correct.
                        Relevance
                            Eg. <Time usage in reasoning>
                            Likelihood
                            ^ By learning to solve formal problems using an amount of time proportional to the given problem's difficulty and importance, we can substantially increase our consistency on important formal problems.
                            Relevance
                    Practical reasoning plays a big role in translating [thoughts / informal arguments] into formal arguments.
                    Likelihood | Maybe link somewhere in formal reasoning or something
                        Translating [thoughts / informal arguments] into formal arguments is important.
                        Relevance | Get link from somewhere for sure.
                            The process of translation is essentially necessarily an informal one.
                            Likelihood | Move
                            ^ [Practical helps informal reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=MtVsy73dMPwCpEQ2Xc-qZHIz)
                            Relevance
            Reducible
                Standard deduction
                Link
                Probabilistic reasoning
                Link
                All reasoning can be reduced to logic, and logic can fit in these things.
                Likelihood
                    1. All reasoning [can be reduced to logic](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=AbNaVbQdlvY2B3giwwf9Ej7N)
                    2. Logic can entirely fit in standard deduction and probabilistic reasoning.
                        1. Standard deduction allows one to express and perform any expressable/performable logic that isn't mainly probabilistic.
                        2. Probabilistic reasoning covers all logic that's mainly probabilistic. 
                        3. Via the <law of excluded middle> and the first two premises, we can conclude that logic can entirely fit in standard deduction and probabilistic reasoning.
                See specific items under "in practice" for more details on how they can ultimately be reduced to these things. 
                Likelihood
            Systematic reasoning
            Amateur | Move/link lots of importance stuff
                Definition
                    Thorough, precise reasoning, similar to formal reasoning.
                Importance
                    Systematic reasoning (done right) tends to be pretty reliable.
                        It's much more falsifiable.
                        Likelihood
                            Typically helps us more quickly find and solve the mistakes we make.
                            Relevance
                                Often before we even present the argument.
                                Often in a way that we aren't even conscious of.
                            Allows others to much more consistently point out our errors.
                            Relevance
                                Significantly helps with [cooperative reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=tIkQysDqkCY4YvrwV_mIfQ_d)
                                Relevance
                                Substantially helps us [improve](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Mcey5J-t6AUuWU92iqr8Nkqe)
                                Relevance
                    Systematic reasoning (done right) tends to help us improve our reasoning.
                        It helps us realize when we've made reasoning mistakes. 
                        Likelihood
                            [It's more falsifiable](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=gCMDk-EhAY2mxkVNdjEpewnJ)
                            Likelihood
                        Realizing when you've used faulty reasoning is [crucial](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=iZyW5tUw6i05PsjXMDoV4nIw) for improving your reasoning.
                        Relevance
                    Unsystematic reasoning is typically very unreliable.
                        [Heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37) is typically substantially involved in unsystematic reasoning.
                        Likelihood
                            Heavy heuristic usage [is typically very unreliable](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HX0lYNDX77zlbRkuVQVQONHa)
                            Relevance
                        Unsystematic reasoning typically involves a lot of [vague wording](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0gOG4jQ-x49IDTjJn9kHifS6)
                        Likelihood
                            Unsystematic reasoning is typically [imprecise](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=581SfOCyfIekfCaptWzmdIKD) (defined semi-specifically)
                            Likelihood
                                Argument
                                Likelihood
                                    Variables (read first)
                                        AA: Unthorough and imprecise
                                        AB: Unthorough and precise
                                        BA: Thorough and imprecise
                                        BB: Thorough and precise
                                        Defining precision and thoroughness (and their opposites) in relation to a cutoff point between "a little" and "substantial".
                                            Eg. Right when "substantially precise" becomes "a little precise", we call it "imprecise".
                                    1. Unsystematic reasoning by definition cannot be both thorough and precise (BB).
                                    2. Therefore the only way for someone to be precise and unsystematic is by being unthorough (AB). (1)
                                    C. Unsystematic reasoning is typically imprecise. (See descendants)
                                        1. Typically, unsystematic reasoning takes one of the possible forms other than AB (AA or BA).
                                            Given some imprecise reasoning of one form, it's less specific to suppose [AA or BA] than it is to suppose AB.
                                            Likelihood
                                                Therefore [Occam's Razor](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UULVW93uPNRrRBC6RL4mTOjT) supports the former. 
                                                Relevance
                                                ^ Specifically: the former would have 2x the prior probability as the latter.
                                                Relevance
                                                ^ ^ Since the former takes up twice the [possibility space](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CWsm9Z1X5Oi2O7vGouwzf91b)
                                                Likelihood
                                            AA is more common than the other forms.
                                            Likelihood
                                                Imprecision is correlated with unthoroughness.
                                                Likelihood
                                                Usually unsystematic reasoning is caused by significant [reasoning short-cuts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QbiCWrDMPM38d8BpMPryvG49), which typically substantially reduce both thoroughness and precision.
                                                Likelihood
                                                [We're typically imprecise](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=qCyvK43-Yc-Y7ZQo-Hb_mzeL)

                                                [We're typically unthorough](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=E7hTHOqdFuMmgkXsh1PNzKFN)
                                                Likelihood
                                        C. Therefore, typically unsystematic reasoning does not take the AB form, and so typically it isn't precise. (1)
                                High imprecision usually involves vague wording.
                                Relevance
                            Vague definitions are [very bad](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=vZ5DP62BXbbbpK2Q0RvXyKbm) for reasoning.

                Precision
                    Definition
                        Distinction
                            Distinct from specificity.
                                It is possible for something to be broad but precisely defined/understood.
                                It is possible for something to be specific but not precisely defined/understood.

                Thoroughness
                    Definition
                        Consistently accounting for a high ratio of important things.
                    Importance
                        If you are not thorough, you can easily fail to address impactful parts of important contrary positions.
                            Definitions
                                "Address" (distinction)
                                    Here defined to mean addressing all the reasoning down to (or very near to) axioms common between the [relevant party(s)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=YOd4VHcHzO33_ixJ0g_jzns1).
                                    "Relevant party(s)"
                                        Usually including you and one other person you're mainly discussing the matter with.
                                        Can also include the audience.
                                            Eg. In a debate and trying to convince the audience, you may want to account for the axioms that the audience are likely to be using, therefore they may be a relevant party as well.
                                    Distinction (important)
                                        Distinct from addressing every argument other(s) give.
                                            If you've already addressed their reasoning down to common axioms and they fail to 
                                "Important"
                                    Important to your goals specifically.
                                    ^ "Your goals" as in the goals that you hold, not necessarily selfish/self-interested goals.
                                    Maybe create a definition for this, since this is 2nd copy of this?
                            This tends to greatly reduce your ability to [critical piggyback](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=2J5emCtKut8DRA6avr2C4r87) and [learn](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XwNwxOcZRgH_hY46vX9t5UTb) many important things.
                            Relevance
                                It can easily lead you to underestimate counterarguments against your position, because of not fully understanding them due to being insufficiently thorough.
                                Likelihood
                                If you fail to address impactful parts of important contrary positions, then you typically fail to fully understand those positions.
                                Relevance
                                    Usually, if you don't understand important positions, you cannot accurately determine how probable they are.
                                    Relevance
                                        Therefore you cannot change your mind to accept them.
                                        Relevance
                                        ^ Therefore you're often left with inaccurate beliefs about important topics.
                                        Relevance
                                    Because if you do understand them, it's typically easy to address them.
                                    Likelihood
                                        And when it's easy to address them, we typically would address them.
                                        Relevance
                                        ^ instinctually, for satisfaction's sake.
                                        Likelihood
                            There are many exceptions to this.
                            Likelihood | Create a section in "thoroughness" about when __not__ to be thorough, likely linking this to those sections' importance sections.
                                When the reasoning presented is confirmed to consistently ignore relevant axioms, it's unlikely to be accurate.
                                Likelihood & Relevance
                                    **Relevant axioms definition:**   Axioms common between you and the [relevant party(s)](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=YOd4VHcHzO33_ixJ0g_jzns1).
                                    Therefore it would typically be justified to skip addressing the specific arguments/people that consistently ignore the relevant axioms.
                                    Relevance
                                    This typically provides very little evidence against [the position the reasoning is advocating for].
                                    Relevance
                                        People can (and frequently do) provide horrible reasoning for true claims.
                                        Likelihood | Likely link to how bad people are about "soft" reasoning in "frequently do".
                                            [Reversed stupidity is not intelligence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=N_EWHi855Un0BfN7zOBsD5ki)
                                            Likelihood
                                        So this does not justify skipping addressing __any__ reasoning about the position in question.
                                        Relevance
                        Improvable
                            **Currently bad:**   Our reasoning is almost always very unthorough.

                Vocal
                    Definition
                        Reasoning via voice.
                    Importance
                        **Clarifications:**  Typically easier to ask for quick clarifications.
                        **Lower dehumanization:**  Typically harder to dehumanize the other person(s) and be unkind.
                            This [dehumanization typically encourages bad reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=AuuzJ03tHDk4T9CCNgjuoP5o)
                            Relevance
                            **Clearer emotions:**  Typically easier to tonally express emotions clearly, and less opportunity for the emotional tone to be misunderstood.
                            Likelihood
                                [In itself, this is largely unimportant for reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=u9E5oHFZ92jI180s6vkRse7J)
                        **Clearer intuition:**  Voice usually offers a better representation of one's intuition and internal monologue.
                        **Memory:**  Typically harder to know what exactly was previously stated.
                            <broken link>
                            This can easily result in unrecognized contradictions or conditions.
                            Typically harder to correct what you said without causing confusion.
                                Especially when the corrections are about things that were stated a while ago.
                                Relevance
                                    <broken link>
                                [There are transcription apps and stuff that could help](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=kiO5NCzEPhcheUmzQmMBBav_)
                            Typically vocal reasoning can't consistently be reused in future discussions.
                                There are transcription apps and stuff that could help.
                        **Time limit:**  Typically harder to cover much ground.
                            Vocal discussions are usually short and rarely revisited.
                            Likelihood
                                Thus, there is typically far less than the required amount of work done to cover all the relevant and important points, to the final important level of detail.
                                Relevance
                            It's usually hard to convince people to take a lot of time to be more thorough in vocal discussions.
                            Likelihood
                                Social norms encourage fast vocal discussions.
                                Likelihood

                Mostly-normal text
                    Definition
                    How to view it
                    Importance / limitations
                        **Thorough:**  Typically easier to be thorough than in [vocal](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=G1iy3M7QYVTcnJLmWkvOH-7n) discussions.
                        **Systematic:**  Typically easier to be [systematic](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7) then in https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=G1iy3M7QYVTcnJLmWkvOH-7n discussions.
                        **Context:**  Typically easier to know what was previously stated.
                        **Systematic:**  Typically very [unsystematic](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7).
                            Rather than responding point by point to each point that the other person made, people tend to focus more on addressing the "vibes" or apparent "main point", often missing a lot of important details.
                        **Clarification:**  Typically harder to ask for quick clarifications than in [vocal](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=G1iy3M7QYVTcnJLmWkvOH-7n) discussions.
                            People often write long messages when reasoning about important topics via text, and others can't see the messages mid-formulation.
                        **Kindness:**  Typically easier to dehumanize the other person(s) and be unkind than in [vocal discussions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=G1iy3M7QYVTcnJLmWkvOH-7n)
                            Our empathy likely evolved to rely on audible tones a substantial amount.
                            Likelihood
                            Beneficial because dehumanization and unkindness of this kind typically encourages bad reasoning, both directly and indirectly.
                            Relevance
                                Indirectly via encouraging the other person to respond in kind.
                        **Emotion:**  Compared to [vocal discussions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=G1iy3M7QYVTcnJLmWkvOH-7n), it is typically harder to express/read emotions well.
                            There are more opportunities for the emotional tone to be misread.
                            Likelihood
                            In itself, this is largely unimportant for reasoning.
                            Relevance
                                Better understanding emotions to the degree that this typically offers doesn't result in a much better understanding of the relevant reasoning.
                                Likelihood
                            When combined with sufficient knowledge/guidance of [how to avoid arguing while trying to get to know someone's motivations], understanding another's emotions better often makes one less defensive and more open minded towards that person.
                            Relevance | Link and move
                                The success of [the Enemies Project](https://www.enemiesproject.org/) indicates that with sufficient guidance, this is possible.
                                Likelihood
                                    The guidance offers reminders/targeted education, but knowledge and <broken link> could achieve the same effect.
                                    Relevance
                                Open-mindedness [is important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=3JYkrvEjUHYENVJaDqFSq3Cd).
                                Relevance
                        **Deep dive:**  It is hard to [dive deep](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=iFjccGMkfI0qrorobVMX02-i) into a single point without seeming to change the subject in a negative way.

                    Reply chain
                    ~~Amateur
                        Definition
                            Chain of messages replying to other messages.
                        Importance
                        New reply
                            Definition
                                Adding a new reply to a reply chain.
                            Reviewing context
                                Definition
                                    Look at far back in the reply chain as seems necessary to understand the context thoroughly.
                                Importance
                                    This tends to be important to do before adding to a reply chain.
                                        Otherwise, it is quite easy to forget (and then fail to account for) important details in previous replies.
                            Checking for other arguments
                                Definition
                                    Checking that the other person(s) haven't addressed [what you would add to the chain] in other messages/reply chains.
                                Importance
                                    This tends to be important to do before adding to a reply chain.
                                        Otherwise, you might miss that they already addressed your counterargument. 
                                            This might mean wasting time writing your reply.
                                            This might mean not at all realizing that they already addressed your argument until they point it out.
                                                If this repeatedly happens, it wastes a lot of time by forcing them to have to point it out many times for the discussion to constructively progress in those areas.
                                                    This would likely result in the discussion being dropped before it is sufficiently explored for the mistaken person(s) to change their mind(s).
                                                This is typically embarrassing.
                    Check for later context
                        Definition (specific)
                            When it's important, respond after reading through it all to make sure that there isn't important context to what's said earlier that you're missing later on in the text.
                        Importance / Limitations
                            Prevents missing how later context addresses your counters to earlier text.
                                Important to avoid embarrassing yourself.
                                Important to be quicker.
                                One can learn to be decently consistent at recognizing when later context addresses their counters.
                                    https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=KVIlDwqG0oJsf9U7kwCk8GwM
                            Avoids wasting time
                                If you have to wait until later to find out that what you wrote was addressed, you'll likely end up writing counter(s) that are doomed to be useless.
                    Related
                        https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=lj9jejrk2s5bCXgZLKLHwWkZ
                        [Time usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=kcYTc2USwUk8D8GkV6D7xpH2)
                Argument mapping
                    Colors
                        I used colors here in a different way than anywhere else, just to fit the theme of the sites mentioned. Sorry.
                    Definition
                        The systematic mapping of arguments, usually organized into a tree of supporting/contesting arguments.
                    Importance / limitations
                        Encourages much more structured reasoning
                            We tend to have a ton of mental habits for taking unreliable [Reasoning short-cuts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QbiCWrDMPM38d8BpMPryvG49), and argument mapping encourages us to reason more consciously and thoroughly.
                            Link for tendency probably
                                Argument mapping encourages us to reason more consciously and thoroughly.
                                Support
                        Minimal downtime
                            You don't have to wait for other(s) to think (and if text: write out their responses).
                            Likelihood
                                Argument mapping makes it easier to reason asynchronously, which means you don't have to wait for thinking/response times.
                                Likelihood
                            This saves time, [which is important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                            Relevance
                        [Clear organization](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=gPpuYdjQBzNcKQP8OSmbU--A)
                        Less context for the reader
                            This results in a substantially greater likelihood of misunderstandings.
                            Relevance
                                This wastes time, [which is impactful](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                                Relevance
                                The difference in likelihood would not be substantial, as even normally, context misunderstandings about unfamiliar words are very common.
                                Likelihood
                                A policy of using clearer definitions can be employed, to avoid that consequence.
                                Likelihood
                                    The platform can be created around a user-built dictionary.
                                    Likelihood
                                    This would be difficult to get used to.
                                    Likelihood
                                        It would thus be employed inconsistently.
                                        Relevance
                                            There can be groups who are willing to take the time to be thorough and apply it consistently.
                                            Likelihood
                                                Most users would not be willing to do this.
                                                Relevance
                                                    We can slowly teach users such that they feel comfortable doing it, and would be willing to.
                                                    Likelihood
                                                    For many cases, we do not need to appeal to most users.
                                                    Relevance
                                        Users would thus not engage, due to the challenge of using the policy being too great.
                                        Relevance
                                            [There can be groups who are willing to take the time to be thorough and apply it consistently](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NPvSvJHsb1veKdYIhbw0ESq4)
                                    This wastes time, [which is impactful](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                                    Relevance
                                Among groups who are already familiar with one another's definitions, the likelihood is not substantially greater.
                                Likelihood
                                    Limiting the group size so substantially greatly reduces many of the other benefits of argument mapping.
                                    Relevance | link
                        Contains more reasoning that is unimportant to the reader
                            This wastes time, [which is impactful](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                            Relevance
                                The reader would likely be able to identify most of that reasoning as unimportant from the titles of the arguments in/under which the important reasoning resides.
                                Relevance
                                    There wouldn't be many titles that would have to be recognized for this, therefore not much time would be wasted.
                                    Relevance
                        Usually slower back-and-forth
                            This means a longer time before misinterpretations can be resolved.
                            Relevance
                                This wastes time, [which is impactful](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)

                    General considerations
                        Canonicality
                            Definition
                            Probably pull from CDL
                            Importance
                                Impactful
                                    In typical debates and debate societies/platforms, past work is not often systematically saved and reused in future works, leading to lots of pointless repetition. In reasoning mapping tools with canonicality, a lot of work can be reused that wouldn't normally be reusable.
                                    ^ This can (depending on the size of the userbase) greatly increase the amount of knowledge/human computation available.
                                    Relevance
                        Clear organization
                            Definition
                                The intuitive categorization of reasoning, especially propositions and logic (eg. arguments).
                                In this context: Reasoning mapping tools tend to allow for much more clear information organization than typical methods.
                            Importance
                                This allows us to more frequently identify parts that we can likely skip without issue.
                                Relevance
                                    This saves time, [which is substantially important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                                    Relevance
                                This allows us to more often quickly find reasoning that we've seen before.
                                Relevance
                                    Our brain has an easier time remembering what branches things fall under, than remembering where exactly things are in a linear text thread.
                                    Likelihood
                                    This saves time, [which is substantially important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                                    Relevance
                        Scores
                            Definition
                            Importance
                                [The importance of multi-user scores](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=l40lFCwOdbZfToKN724fTGGZ)
                                [The importance of roll-up scores](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=nqRECYXGIaQ-RjuyfzSWKR6T)
                            Roll-up scores
                                Definition
                                Probably pull for CDL
                                Importance
                                    Impactful
                                        In typical debates, unreliable intuitions must be relied on to calculate and combine confidences, giving our cognitive biases a huge opportunity to influence our thinking. But in reasoning mapping tools, there are sometimes roll-up systems that can be used to combine scores in a reliable, mathematical way.
                            Multi-user scores
                                Importance
                                    Prioritization: Multi-user scores can substantially improve prioritization of where to reason.
                                        Multi-user scores make it much easier to know where people disagree, which makes it easier to know where to prioritize reasoning.
                                        Likelihood
                                        This makes it easier to avoid providing reasoning in cases where it isn't needed.
                                        Relevance
                                            This allows you to skip writing this reasoning out.
                                                This saves time, [which is important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                                                Relevance
                                            This usually allows other(s) to skip reading that reasoning.
                                                This saves time, [which is important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                                                Relevance
                                            This usually prevents other(s) from adding to that reasoning.
                                                This saves time, [which is important](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FAE5Snb_O1pGzF4zHU-u5_VU)
                                                Relevance
                        Saving time
                            Importance
                                That time can be spent expanding the reasoning.
                                More time as more users read the reasoning and as the reasoning grows larger.
                        Future potential
                            Importance
                                If a tool will turn out to be incredibly useful for you in the future, it may be best to start learning it now even if it isn't in the ideal state currently.

                            [General software considerations](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mQybGtFSpBoxAfGY9y6Y-dIq)
            Structure of reasoning
                Definition
                    The structure of reasoning.
                Importance
                    [Importance of circularity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=PUNY_2ce8BRrC3FeVbISYGPU)
                Input and output
                    Definitions
                        Input
                        Output
                    Importance
                        Common
                            Virtually all reasoning can be grown down to functions with inputs and outputs.
                Inferences
                    Definition
                        A conclusion reached by way of evidence and reasoning.
                        Examples
                            If you notice someone making a disgusted face after they've taken a bite of their lunch, you can probably infer that they do not like it.
                            If a friend walks by with a graded test in her hand and a smile on her face, you can probably infer that she got a good grade on the test.
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inference)
                    Deductive inference
                    Consider moving to definitions
                        Definition
                            A deductive inference is an inference that is [valid](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UUAfcY2vsb-7FyMTvOmfclqa) from its [premises](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0sMGYY-2pbkR4lPQUYVVwZs9), meaning that it is impossible for the premises to be true and the conclusion to be false.
                Circularity
                    Definition
                        Circular reasoning.
                        Reasoning in which the conclusion is present in one of the (stated or implied) premises.
                        Distinction
                            Distinct from non-loop circles.
                                Definition
                                    Circles of reasoning that don't form a loop, so there's no justification chain that supports itself.
                                Example
                                    A -> B -> D
                                    A -> C -> D
                                    When you graph this out, it forms a kind of circle of connections, but the connections don't run in a "loop" such that the justification chain supports itself.
                            Distinct from relation/category loops (partial or otherwise).
                                Total relation/category loops
                                Partial relation/category loops
                                    Example
                                        A supports B, B is in the A category.
                                        This isn't necessarily problematic because non-B things in the A category can support B.
                                            i.e. One member of a set can support another member of that same set.
                    Importance
                        [Importance of non-axiomatic circularity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=1zF87KJR0xKJlwgZ38Bmt6GV)
                    Recognizing circularity
                    Vicious vs non-vicious circularity
                        Vicious circulatory
                            **Synonym:**   Vicious circle fallacy
                        Non-vicious circulatory
                    Non-axiomatic circularity
                        Definition
                            **Synonym:**   Begging the question
                            Examples
                        Importance
                            Common
                            Unreliable
                                Inductive counterarguments
                                Likelihood
                                    Observed unreliability of circular reasoning
                                    Likelihood
                                        We seem to consistently observe that circular reasoning does no better than chance at deriving truths.
                                        Likelihood
                                            **Truths:**   Defined broadly to include avoided falsehoods.
                                    Energy counterargument to circular reasoning
                                    Likelihood | [Intuition pull](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6Efrp6c3GsagWNAxFomdfD8A)
                                        Justification functions sort of like energy. You can't use energy to increase itself without drawing from other energy.
                                        Likelihood
                                **Contradictory circularity:**   Circular reasoning must be limited in such a manner that it cannot be used for any pair of proposition and its negation.
                                Likelihood
                                    Argument
                                    Likelihood
                                        P1. If circular reasoning can be used to deduce a proposition and its negation, then a contradiction is entailed.
                                        Likelihood
                                        P2. True logical contradictions [are impossible](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=iRkaxeyoE5AgB17FzWOds2C7).
                                        Likelihood
                                        Conclusion derived via P1, P2, [Proof by contradiction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QPYK9FVR9vJVazmsKRYBSnT8)
                                        Likelihood
                                    Unlikely due to lacking good rules
                                    Relevance
                                        P1. Without sufficient rules for determining when one can/can't use circular reasoning, contradictions arise.
                                            [Argument](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=xpIkS0WCH9OWWsPkBIwMNj4h)
                                            Likelihood
                                        P2. Therefore either circular reasoning isn't reliable, or sufficient rules must accurately reflect reality. (P1)
                                        P3. It's unlikely that sufficient rules accurately reflect reality.
                                            It seems unclear what plausible rules would be sufficient well for determining when you can/can't use circular reasoning.
                                            Likelihood | Link to dialetheism section
                                            It seems like sufficient rules would have found widespread adoption if they were justified.
                                            Likelihood
                                                Sufficient rules have not found widespread adoption.
                                                Relevance
                                        P4. Therefore it's likely that circular reasoning isn't reliable. (P2, P3)
                                    Unlikely due to specificity
                                    Relevance
                                        **Elaboration:**   Supposing that circular reasoning with only this detail is accurate to reality requires a more specific hypothesis, and is thus less likely compared to the hypothesis that circular reasoning is simply never reliable.
                                        [Occam's razor](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UULVW93uPNRrRBC6RL4mTOjT)
                                        Likelihood
                    Axiomatic circularity
                        Definition
                        Importance
                            Many people (at least claim to) use circularity at the foundation of their epistemology.
                        [Intuition pull](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6Efrp6c3GsagWNAxFomdfD8A)
                            There's no inherent inconsistency in presupposing the validity of certain limited forms of circular reasoning.
                            It's common for people to unknowingly use axioms to justify circular reasoning to justify axioms.
                    Arguments for/against circulatory
                        Premise-circularity is vicious but rule-circularity is not
                            It is not necessary to know or even justifiably believe that rule R is reliable in order to move to a justified conclusion using the rule.
                                As long as R is in fact reliable, one can form a justified belief in the conclusion of an argument relying on R, as long as one has justified belief in the premises.
                                    If one does not have justified belief in the rule of inference, then it is not possible to have justified belief in a conclusion that requires that rule of inference.
                                This doesn't work without [its support](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=3oTnQJF4XeV7rlmYxWvWn-Cf), which doesn't work due to the support's [counter](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=3HO2eD1swi2P5I4S4c-9zwAE).
                            Rule-circularity is sufficient to demonstrate the reliability very fundamental rules.
                                It is unreasonable to require an uncircular demonstration of a very fundamental rule's reliability. 
                                    There is nowhere more basic to turn for such fundamental rules, so all that we can reasonably demand of a fundamental form of reasoning is that it endorse itself.
                                        Even deductive inference cannot be justified deductively.
                                        There is somewhere more basic we can turn.
                                        We do not a priori owe fundamental forms of reasoning a realistic chance to defend themselves.
                            This is odd (and thus analogically unlikely), because there appears to be an easy interchange between rules and premises.
                                A rule can always be added as a premise to the argument. 
                                    There is a fundamental difference between being prepared to accept a premise stating a rule and being prepared to use that rule.
                            A circular rule argument does not provide a full justification of the rule, so it is pretty much useless.
                                A circular rule argument does not provide a full justification of the rule.
                                    Less sane inference rules such as counterinduction can support themselves in a similar fashion. The counterinductive rule is CI:Most observed As are Bs.Therefore, it is not the case that most As are Bs.Consider then the following argument CI*:Most CI arguments have been unsuccessfulTherefore, it is not the case that most CI arguments are unsuccessful, i.e., many CI arguments are successful.This argument therefore establishes the reliability of CI in a rule-circular fashion.
                                [...] so it is pretty much useless.
                                    It's useful for showing people who accept inductive inference that it is reliable.
                                        There is some added value in showing that inductive inferences are reliable, even when we already accept that there is nothing problematic about them.
                                            If "there is nothing problematic about them" is defined to preclude inconsistency, then this is equivalent to saying "there is some value in showing x even when we already know x", and it's false.
                                        The inductive justification of induction provides a kind of important consistency check on our existing beliefs.
                                            It doesn't provide this consistency check because of [ovserved unreliablility of circular reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=kgcU5zacmTUoYtHGYAVsQ6hW)
                                            It doesn't provide this consistency check because of [energy counterargument to circular reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=iz1E432YHazYDQWjizy3SjDl)
                        Circular reasoning using a sufficiently large circle can provide useful information
                        It is generally unclear (given the limited reliability of circular reasoning) what forms of circular reasoning should be considered ok and what forms should not.
                            eg. Why is circular reasoning not allowed for establishing the existence of unicorns?
                                Premise 1. Unicorns exist (assumption)
                                   Premise 2. If unicorns exist then unicorns exist (law of identity)
                                Premise 3. Unicorns exist (Modus ponens 1, 2)
                Trees of knowledge
                Maybe combine with reasoning pyramids?
                    Examples
                        Axioms and presuppositions are the roots.
                        Other beliefs are the branches and leaves.
                    Representing trees of knowledge
                        Definition
                            Creating/maintaining dependency maps between beliefs.
                        Importance
                            Impactful
                                Very helpful for more [systematically reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7).
                    Pyramid of practical reasoning
                        Definition
                            Quicker but less reliable reasoning methods built on shower and more reliable methods.
                        Importance
                            It allows us to know to (and to some degree how to) default to more reliable methods when the less reliable ones come into sufficient question.
                    Related
                        [Fitting things together](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FORaGWzKq7OJtxVu6LDAqa11)
                Reasoning pyramids
                    Definition
                        An epistemic structure where a proposition is only directly justified via a combination of other propositions, which are in turn likely the same, and likely so on.
                            Technically virtually everything is only justified via a combination of other propositions if we allow for indirect justification.
                                Virtually all proposition justifications at least involve the law of identity and some other proposition.
                        From now on in this section, "pyramid" will be used as a short-hand for this.
                    Importance
                        **Common:**   We use reasoning pyramids to justify a vast amount of our beliefs.
                        Large pyramids are unstable
                            It's difficult to properly learn what justifies what for a sufficiently large (and important) portion of large reasoning pyramids.
                            Likelihood
                                The lack of already-present maps
                                Likelihood
                                    We lack many easily-accessible well-structured maps for even much of the most common reasoning we use.
                                    Likelihood
                                We are typically ignorant of large portions of large reasoning pyramids.
                                Likelihood
                                    Causes
                                    ^ Unstructured reasoning
                                    ???
                                We typically have no idea how to.
                                Likelihood
                                Related
                                    [Argument mapping](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=uSb4_0tF0jO3_fwXZ7rtFxjv)
                            It's difficult to determine when all known crucial things are justified in a large reasoning pyramid
                            Likelihood
                                Examples
                                    NASA failure because of metric vs imperial confusion.
                                        In retrospect this was a very obvious thing to check for and avoid. But when dealing with the massive pyramid of reasoning involved in making sure everything in the rocket will work, there's so many things that could go wrong that it's very hard to thoroughly check everything.
                                Technicality
                                    This is technically slightly misleading since there are situations where there are multiple options for how to justify something and only one is required.
                                        None are individually crucial but having at least one is crucial.
                                [Propogation of uncertainty](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=73NTuS0d863Qd-z8AYKHURPc)
                                Likelihood
                                [Error cascade](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6ZzVQMb1isI3hsKZFaTm88Gu)
                                Likelihood
                            We frequently use large reasoning pyramids for important reasoning.
                            Relevance
                                Examples
                    Propagation of uncertainty
                        Definition
                            In statistics, propagation of uncertainty is the effect of variables' uncertainties on the uncertainty of a function based on them.
                            Examples
                                62 crucial justifying propositions that, if true, are all 98% likely to justify their parent.
                                    Only ~28.6% likely for the main proposition to be justified, despite all its supporting propositions having such a high likelihood.
                    Error cascade
                        Definition
                            A single important error upon which many things are built causing a major collapse.
                            Examples
                        Importance
                            Common
                                [Examples](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=i31tuKPtyg6pvBWtaDQRlNOp)
                                Likelihood
                    Related
                        [Fitting things together](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=FORaGWzKq7OJtxVu6LDAqa11)

                Gödel's incompleteness theorems
                    Definition
                Reductio ad absurdum
                    Definition
                        The form of argument that attempts to establish a claim by showing that following the logic of a proposition or argument would lead to absurdity or [contradiction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CGk4nS-fRre90tF0iZFJB846).
                        Synonyms
                            Argumentum ad absurdum
                            Apagogical argument
                        [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reductio_ad_absurdum)
                    Importance
                        Frequently an effective form of [persuasion](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=S-S1oq4cZbPSPeH4eYJKd9K7)
                    Limitations
                        It's very easy to strawman others using this technique.
                            In order to make a reductio ad absurdum, we often have to perform a few steps of reasoning from the other person's perspective.
                            And if our understanding of the person's perspective is wrong such that any of those steps don't work, we would likely be creating a strawman argument.
                                "Likely" because we could be doing something like using multiple lines of reasoning to justify our ad absurdum, so that if one line doesn't work, the other could still work.
                            Taking care to avoid strawmanning others is important.
                                [People frequently strawman eachother](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=d1K4S_SqoO5J7cMB8zmTFkxz) and [strawmans often cause major and quite impactful misunderstandings when people misunderstand each other](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=9J3EO8ahD-_oTN6g6gKaOnVa).
                    Types
                        [Proof by contradiction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QPYK9FVR9vJVazmsKRYBSnT8)
                            The deductive form.
                        Inductive ad absurdum
                            Definition
                                An argument that following the logic of a proposition or argument leads to an overly unlikely outcome, and thus that the proposition/argument is more likely to be flawed than it other would be.
                                Distinction
                                    [Emotional ad absurdum](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HcBO8q0z-StpkEjtE3kNTzMz)
                            Limitation
                                Often largely based on an [emotional ad absurdum](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HcBO8q0z-StpkEjtE3kNTzMz)
                        Emotional ad absurdum
                            Definition
                                An argument intended to appeal to our subconscious/intuition about how "absurd" something is.
                                Distinction
                                    Different from [inductive ad absurdum](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QwtJDbQokrF5wmiAXN_Pn-RP)
                            Importance
                                Learning to recognize and avoid this is very important.
                            Limitations
                                The unreliability of emotions.
                                Link
                Likelihood and relevance
                Move or link to induction
                    Distinguishing likelihood and relevance
                    Link(s) to likelihood/relevance, link to semantics somewhere probably
                        Importance
                            Impactful
                                Failing to distinguish between likelihood and relevance can easily lead to substantial misunderstandings.
                            Improvable
                                It's common for people to assume that likelihood quantifiers lose almost all weight when combined with a relevance quantifier of sufficiently high or low strength.
                                    [The norm is for relevance quantifiers to supercede likelihood quantifiers](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=8lKqHN0nm2G0QS-nsVBOnoM8)
                        The norm is for relevance quantifiers to supercede likelihood quantifiers
                        Related
                            [Quantification](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=YbVdzkes7vVzGEsCil7Rsrze)
                    Likelihood is often considered more important than value, likely due to our inability to intuit very large or very small things, and the regular upper and lower bounds typically subconsciously placed on likelihood and value.
                    Link the cognitive bias about very large/small values.

            Designing arguments
                Importance
                    **Common:**   Occurs in essentially all reasoning whatsoever. 
                    Crucial to good reasoning.
                Gathering premises
                    Definition (specific) 
                        Thinking of, filtering and remembering premises.
                    Importance
                        We [can significantly improve](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HGJvXMB9FfWcYmbnPbbWDJlg) when it comes to discarding irrelevant premises.
                    Discarding irrelevant premises
                        Definition
                            Avoiding relying on premises that are not relevant to the argument you're trying to make.
                        Importance
                            Saves time
                                By avoiding including irrelevant premises, you save reader(s)' time.
                                Likelihood

                            **More validity:**   Lesser chance of making an invalid argument.

            Decomposition
                Definition (broad)
                    The process of splitting up a bigger concept into smaller ones.
                Reasoning decomposition
                    Definition
                        Decomposition of reasoning.
                    Importance
                        Common
                            Practically all reasoning involves at least some decomposition.
                        Impactful
                            Very important for one's [thoroughness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=lj9jejrk2s5bCXgZLKLHwWkZ).
                        Difficult
                            [Reasoning short-cuts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QbiCWrDMPM38d8BpMPryvG49) (especially [heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)) tends to make it very difficult to decompose in a sufficiently [thorough](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=lj9jejrk2s5bCXgZLKLHwWkZ) manner to avoid missing tons of very important details.
            Accounting for unknowns
                Importance
                    Common
                        We have a tendency to focus on only the things that we have explicitly accounted for, without accounting for much of the uncertainty that we would ideally account for.
                        [Overconfidence often causes us to lean towards much more extreme probabilities than we really ought to](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=X_nTX0Yfk9EuT8wiLvWk7Yck)
                    Impactful
                        [Overconfidence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=6dfZL4s3LjJlqjUv7pk86tV4) often causes us to lean towards much more extreme probabilities than we really ought to.
                Reading between the lines
                Move to "understanding others" probably.
                    Definition
                    Importance
                    Related

                Understand before dismiss
                    Definition
                        Thoroughly understand an idea (usually important) before dismissing it.
                    Importance
                        Without following this, it is virtually guaranteed that out of all the important ideas you will investigate, there will be some that are prematurely rejected.
                Avoiding missing exceptions
                    Importance
                        We often fail to account for certain broad possibilities and as a result spend all our calculation in areas that don't matter.
                            Examples of failing to account for all possibilities
                                Classical theism vs atheism
                                    Definitions
                                        Atheism
                                        Deism
                                        Theism
                                            Classical theism
                                            Non-classical theism
                                    A common false dichotomy is between classical theism and atheism. But there are other possibilities:
                                    Deism
                                    Non-classical theism
                    Deductively categorizing possibilities
                        Definition
                            Starting with all possibilities and creating categories for mutually exclusive groups of possibilities to check if you've missed anything.
                        Importance
                            It's a good way to make sure that we've accounted for all possibilities.
                    Involving person(s) with different plausible opinion(s)
                        Definitions
                            Getting other person(s) with different plausible opinion(s) to look at the topic and see if they can spot any important things that you would miss.
                                Opinion
                            Synonym
                                Getting fresh eyes on the topic.
                        Importance
                            Impactful
                                Particularly useful when you're too exhausted/bored but you want the topic to be considered more.
                            Common
                        Finding people with different plausible opinion(s)
                            Contacting fitting people you know
                            Finding people with high [criticalness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=wpVxdpKSZf5aCjKmiiGsbwWh)
                Other-shoe reviews
                    Definition
                        Checking how your statement looks from the perspective of the primary side(s) your statement contests.
                        [Distinction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=l8W3XME24iqi21mn6CWaeV6F)
                        Synonyms
                            Placing yourself in the other person's shoes.
                                This can have other connotations like emotionally empathizing with others, which can also be useful but isn't necessarily meant by "other-shoe reviews".
                    Importance
                        This is an incredibly useful technique for catching things you missed and properly accounting for obvious counterarguments before they are given.
                    Always assume you're wrong
                        Definition (it's hyperbole)
                            The idea is to temporarily think almost entirely as though you know you are wrong but don't know how.
                        Importance
                            Our brain evolved to argue for one perspective and not adequately account for the other side. This tricks our brain into focusing on treating one side to that luxury, then the other.
                                <broken link>
                                It encourages you to try to look for all the ways in which you could be wrong and give all the promising counterarguments you can find adequate attention.
                        Applied to cooperative reasoning
                            If you disagree
                                Almost always assume that the other(s) are right at first.
                                    Because you should (temporarily) assume that you're wrong about them being wrong.
                            If you agree
                                Almost always assume that the other(s) are wrong at first.
                                    Because you should (temporarily) assume that you're wrong about them being right.
                        Back and forth
                            It's good to switch from acting as though one side is right and then acting like another side is right and so on.
                                But when you do this, you should be careful to account for all possibilities:
                                [Avoid missing exceptions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ki9wj7h_kf_1i1KKOgL9aO_X)
                Preemptive steelman counter check
                    Definition
                        The process of steelmanning your opponent and checking if they've already (implicitly or explicitly) accounted for your counterargument(s), concern(s) or similar.
                        Connotation
                            Typically the order of events is:
                            1. You see a statement/argument.
                            2. You think about it.
                            3. You write counterargument(s).
                            4. You carefully [Steelman](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=LWB8YQUQupE7UQRtFL7J9g6w) the statement/argument and re-read it, checking for implicit or explicit counter(s) to your counterargument(s).
                        Distinction
                            [Other-shoe reviews](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=d7AlWLW-6EAuja2PutdZEj64) are about looking for counterarguments that were neither implicitly nor explicitly given. Whereas PSCCs are about looking for counterarguments that were implicitly or explicitly given. However "implicitly" is defined pretty vaguely, so it's fair to regard these topics as having some overlap.
                Multiple checks
                    Definition
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            Consequences of not finding flaws
                                [Lacking evidence ≠ false](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=PoAcoW2P9Q9L6hiLGzVECWql)
                        Common
                            A lot of people perform multiple checks in a flawed way.
                                A lot of people use an unreliable but consistent method, so the multiple checks give the same wrong answer multiple times.
                                A lot of people overestimate the thoroughness of correctly performed multiple checks.
                                    It is often decently effective, but usually far from definitive.
                    Limitations
                        Potential systematic issue
                            If the potential uncaught error emerged from a systematic error (eg. the relevant intuition(s) failing to account for a very important variable), then it is likely that the systematic error will be repeated if the same person(s) analyze(s) the same thing again.
                            This can be somewhat mitigated by using (at least slightly) different techniques/people for the checks.
                                For example doing an ordinary check, coming up with counterargument(s), then doing a [preemptive steelman counter check](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=dlapD9QkEDz8gtBNqJGSpZ_E)
                    [Cooperative](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=5Qlio6RfNJC5UcTvCa9o-7SX) multiple checks
                        Definition
                        Importance
                        Ordinary peer-review
                            Importance
                        Conceptual replication
                            Definition
                                A research strategy in which the same underlying hypothesis or theoretical concept is tested using different methods, operationalizations, samples, or experimental conditions compared to the original study.
                                Connotation
                                    Has professional and academic connotations, but does not necessarily need to be professional or academic.
                            Importance
                    Related
                        [Preemptive steelman counter check](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=dlapD9QkEDz8gtBNqJGSpZ_E)
                        [Other-shoe reviews](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=d7AlWLW-6EAuja2PutdZEj64)
                Lacking evidence ≠ false
                    Definition
                        Lacking evidence in favor of a proposition is not necessarily substantial evidence of that proposition being false.
                        Synonym
                            Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.
                    Importance
                        Lacking evidence against a hypothesis is often not great evidence for that hypothesis.
                            Even if you've done multiple checks, depending on the subject it could have a feature that makes it very easy for there to be many checks that would simultaneously fail.
                        This is often particularly relevant when applied to topics where people frequently reason badly.
                            The arguments for/against things that you are aware of are likely not the only possible ones.
                            As such, if people are not good at finding good arguments, you addressing all the bad ones likely doesn't tell you much about the reasonable position to take.
                Unexpected obstacles
                    Causes
                        With a sufficiently complex task, it is impossible to account for all the challenges that will be involved.
                    Hofstadter's law
                        Definition
                            "It always takes longer than you expect, even when you take into account Hofstadter's law."
                            A law usually used to describe the widely experienced difficulty of accurately estimating the time it will take to complete tasks of substantial complexity.
                            https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hofstadter's_law
                        We have a tendency to focus only on the things that we can explicitly account for.
                        Link to bias(es)
                    Plan for failure
                        Definition
                            Planning for things to go wrong in unexpected ways.
                                Unexpected: Not specifically predicted.
                                    Example
                                        You might expect that trying to build your own submarine would go poorly, but it might be difficult to predict exactly which part(s) of your design would fail.
                                            After all, it's your design, so you would ostensibly prevent any sufficiently specific failures that you could successfully predict.
                        Importance
                            Depending on the importance, it may be worth coming up with contingencies for if things go wrong in unexpected ways.
                        Systematically planning contingencies
                            https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=e-rx5GVRcMwUlauim9BgIzme
                            Assigning [time usage in reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ix4g1EoGh3HsteZTJwCBMY8E)
                            Find semi-plausible failures
                                **Experiment method:**   Try to break the components in all the semi-realistic ways you can think to.
                            Make contingencies
                                Multiple contingencies
                                    Importance
                                        Oftentimes you'll want multiple contingencies if it's very important not to fail.
                                    Independence
                                        Importance
                                            It's very important to keep the contingencies very independent, so that a common failure of one thing doesn't cause a failure of multiple contingencies.
                                        Design independence
                                            Different designs so that if one design is faulty (or faultily implemented, etc.) then that's ok.
                                        Source independence
                                            Different people, manufacturers, companies, etc.
                                        Implementation independence?
                                        System independence
                                            Contingencies that are meant to be independent should not be reliant on a common system.
                        Commonly useful contingencies
                            Backup time
                            Backup money
                        Commonly useful areas to plan for failure
                            Reasoning
                                Importance
                                Contingencies
                Related
                    [Prior probabilities](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=WtCnPwTlo9i-Whi4OhvH_ZQd)
                        Often one of the most important unknowns to account for. ([source](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=THdiKHErbTGYJvqcfGOasDCk))
                    [Not accounting for unknowns](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HqeODo74AAT86530mLDuvEMf)
