                        ["Winning"](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=yDxHpzr99Ib8E5XfGJAHVTzK)
                Fallacies
                Show how almost all of these are reducible to more fundamental mistakes.
                    Definition
                        Specific instances of flawed (fallacious) reasoning in arguments*.
                            The arguments can be either formal or informal.
                    Limitations
                        Most can be avoided using [systematic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7)
                            Most fallacies are implicitly avoided if you use systematic reasoning, which you should be doing anyway. So most are not very important to learn about specifically.
                        Learning isn't enough
                            Merely learning about the fallacies will not make you much better at accounting for them. For that, you have to [form habits and practice processes that avoid falling into fallacious reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mwz5Ve2BffIKD_YiMRN4SJOU)
                    How to view them
                        Mostly common mistakes that people make. It is much less common for people to intentionally employ fallacies, though it does of course happen.
                        Most of these fallacies are best understood in terms of other, more fundamental reasoning practices, mistakes, etc.
                            They implicitly include most of these fallacies with most of their nuances.
                            We mostly list all these fallacies so that you can easily find a good definition for them if someone mentions them.
                    Formal fallacies
                        Definition
                            Formal fallacies occur when there is a problem with the form, or structure, of the argument, such that the argument is [invalid](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UUAfcY2vsb-7FyMTvOmfclqa).
                            **Etymplogy:**   The word "formal" refers to this link to the __form__ of the argument.
                        Fallacies of presumption
                        Maybe should be in informal fallacies?
                            Definition
                                Fallacies that make presumptions, often in a hidden way. That is, they rely on hidden and unstated assumptions written, as it were, between the lines.
                        Fallacies of ambiguity
                            Definition
                                Fallacies that involve one switching between meanings without acknowledging that they are doing so.
                    Informal fallacies
                        Definition
                            An informal fallacy is an error in reasoning that occurs due to a problem with the __content__, rather than the form or structure, of the argument.
                        Irrelevance
                            Definition
                                Fallacies that employ irrelevant claims that typically feel relevant.
                                Synonyms
                                    Fallacies of irrelevance
                                    Fallacies of relevance
                                    Relevance fallacies
                            General countermeasures
                                Causes
                                    These fallacies depend on the use of information that may seem relevant to establishing the conclusion but isn’t really relevant after all.
                            The genetic fallacy
                                Definition
                                    The fallacy in which arguments or information are dismissed or validated based solely on someone's or something's history, origin, or source rather than its current meaning or context.
                                    Example
                                        Wedding ring
                                            You're not going to wear a wedding ring, are you? Don't you know that the wedding ring originally symbolized ankle chains worn by women to prevent them from running away from their husbands? I would not have thought you would be a party to such a sexist practice.
                                Nuances
                                    Common misconception
                                        We should focus on what was said rather than the who said it.
                                            Definition
                                                The idea that it's always more reasonable to figure out if something is true on its own merit rather than to assume one way or the other based on where the information comes from.
                                            Importance
                                                We can't learn much on our own.
                                                    It's unlikely that we can learn whether even a small fraction of the most important things are true based only on their own merit.
                                                    Likelihood
                                                        We would have to learn enough to have the equivalent of several phds in fields such as logic, mathematics, philosophy, psychology, neurology, etc.
                                                        Likelihood
                                                Focusing on who said what is often reliable.
                                                    If it's a relevant expert (or collection of experts, or even technology) who has a near-perfect track record about hundreds of similar claims, it seems likely that it will be true. And this often is the case.
                                                    Likelihood
                                            How to view it
                                                An oversimplification that shouldn't be heavily relied on, but not a horrible rule of thumb if you have barely any time to think about something (which is almost never the case).
                                Reducible
                                    1. We subconsciously associate things with their histories/origins to enough of an extent that during recollection, we have a very strong intuition that it is best to emphasize feature(s) of the associated history/origin in a way that is irrelevant to the current context. 
                                    2. We decide to follow and defend that intuition without sufficiently questioning it.
                                    [Heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                                    <broken link>
                                Appeal to authority
                                    Definition (specific)
                                        An appeal to an inappropriate or clearly inadequate authority.
                                            Inadequate after taking into account the other evidence presented.
                                        The issue that the "appeal to authority" fallacy is meant to point out is the overconfidence people often have for certain authorities.
                                        Example
                                            My friend who is a scientist insists that global warming is not cause for alarm, and for me that is a good enough reason to accept her conclusion.
                                        Synonym
                                            Appeal to inappropriate authority.
                                        Distinctions (important)
                                            "An appeal to an authority."
                                                It is not *intrinsically* unreasonable to "appeal to an authority" as defined here, because the authority in question may have the relevant expertise to be much more likely to be correct than an ordinary person, all else being equal. Therefore the authority's opinion may actually be very relevant and substantial evidence.
                                                ^ Especially if the "authority" in question is the complete collection of relevantly qualified experts, as opposed to a specific expert.
                                                Relevance
                                                    Oftentimes, there are so many "experts" (and the process of picking who gets to become an "expert" is so problematic) that there are bound to be some specific so-called "experts" who have absurd views in their relevant field.
                                                    Likelihood
                                                    Example: Polls of the opinions of relevant scientists seem to show a consensus that global warming is a significant cause of alarm.
                                            [We should focus on what was said rather than the who said it](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OX68H_rbv6ALl6A6E_X2E0Pc)
                                                [We should focus on both](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Tcx7-9H6qS3CAP63e4LNp8Lw)
                                [Ad hominem](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=PIhQJrDSFo0Z48InInK-u0Wh)
                                [Naturalistic fallacy](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=l772s-XNogR_GYsI8PZBdDZH)
                            Ad hominem
                                Definition
                                    An argumentative strategy that attacks the character, motive etc. of the person(s) associated with the argument, in a way that provides no sufficiently substantive evidence to be plausibly indicative of a relevant and important truth.
                                    Synonyms
                                        Personal attack
                                        Abusive fallacy
                                Importance
                                    Common
                                        This fallacy is very common.
                                    Impactful
                                Nuances
                                    An argument against one's character can be given for a constructive purpose outside of furthering the primary topic. 
                                        Examples
                                            Constructive criticism given mid-discussion.
                                    An argument against one's character can be genuinely relevant and impactful to the discussion.
                                    Maybe move this limitations into above?
                                        Explanation
                                            While we should not care intrinsically who gives an argument and should typically focus on the argument's validity and soundness, oftentimes who gave the argument will be extrinsically substantially relevant to the trustworthiness of the argument.
                                        Limitations
                                            Mid-discussion character criticism often causes people to get more defensive.
                                            Mid-discussion character criticism is often seen as a bad faith technique.
                                        Example
                                            If one person states that they witnessed God, and the other person argues that the supposed witness often takes drugs that cause hallucinations, this attacks the reliability of the witness (at least insofar as the particular claim they made goes) in a relevant and impactful way.
                                    [We should focus on what was said rather than the who said it](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OX68H_rbv6ALl6A6E_X2E0Pc)
                                <broken link>
                            Red herring
                                Definition
                                    The red herring is a seemingly plausible, though ultimately irrelevant, diversionary tactic.
                                    Examples
                                    Distinction
                                        This is distinct from red herrings as literary devices for eg. mystery novels.
                                Limitations
                                    Easily misattributable to genuinely important and relevant [deep dives](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=iFjccGMkfI0qrorobVMX02-i) into important and relevant details primarily involving other topics.
                                Nuances
                                    A red herring may be intentional or unintentional; it is not necessarily a conscious intent to mislead.
                            Appeal to popularity
                                Definition
                                    Synonym
                                        Bandwagon fallacy
                                Nuances
                                    [We should focus on what was said rather than the who said it](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OX68H_rbv6ALl6A6E_X2E0Pc)
                            Appeal to consequences
                                Definition
                                    Rejecting some particular viewpoint, theory or idea based on the consequences to which it leads, typically because of a negative emotional reaction to those consequences.
                                Importance
                                    Impactful
                                        It is usually irrelevant whether or not we like or want to believe something, truth is indifferent to feelings.
                                    Common
                            Appeal to emotion
                                Definition
                                    The fallacy that intends to manipulate the recipient's emotions in order to win an argument.
                                        **Source:**   https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appeal_to_emotion
                                    **Often:**   In the absence of factual evidence.
                                    Examples
                                        Charity advertisement
                                            A charity advertisement showing a single suffering child, eyes wide, belly descended. Statistics about millions in need remain abstract, but this one face breaks through our defenses. Our wallets open not because we've evaluated the charity's effectiveness or administrative overhead, but because we cannot bear the weight of those eyes. The emotion becomes the argument.
                                        Crime statistics
                                            Crime statistics might show historic lows, but one brutal incident (properly packaged) overrides decades of data. The politician who practices safety from the manufactured threat wins over the one citing improvement trends. Parents clutch their children tighter, vote for harsher measures, support policies they would reject in calmer moments. Fear doesn't require facts.
                                        Product commercials
                                            A lot of advertising is created and followed not because of a fallacy, but because of social expression. i.e. Socially expressing certain characteristics about one's self by buying products with advertisements associated with those characteristics.
                                                [Source](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GFYasDoJ-98)
                                            The car commercial doesn't list specifications or safety ratings, instead, it shows a father driving his daughter to her wedding, both of them crying.
                                            The watch advertisement ignores precision engineering, focusing on a grandfather passing his timepiece to his grandson. We buy the emotion, not the product.
                                        Climate change
                                            Climate change discussions devolve into pictures of polar bears on shrinking ice vs images of unemployed coal miners.
                                        Healthcare reform
                                            Healthcare reform gets buried under individual tragedies from both sides. The emotional ammunition flies, while substantive policy discussion dies in the crossfire.
                                        [Source](https://youtu.be/GpFS9pDrNhM)
                                            Taken virtually verbatim from the linked video.
                                            Seems AI genned, but I think the examples still largely work.
                                    Distinction
                                        It is only fallacious when the emotions that are elicited are irrelevant to evaluating the truth of the conclusion and serve to distract from rational consideration of relevant premises or information.
                                        Merely including emotions in some premises in an argument is not sufficient to count as a form of appealing to emotions.
                                            Eg. If I feel frustrated, then I will likely yell. I feel frustrated, therefore I will likely yell.
                                            This is not a fallacious argument, unless perhaps it is implied that the listener(s) ought to do something as a result of this argument.
                                Types
                                    [Appeal to fear](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appeal_to_fear)
                                    [Appeal to flattery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appeal_to_flattery)
                                    [Appeal to pity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appeal_to_pity)
                                    [Appeal to ridicule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appeal_to_ridicule)
                                    [Appeal to spite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appeal_to_spite)
                                Related
                                    [Appeal to consequences](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=fYoI4jUDOc5HAoMP_GDmv2PD)
                                        Often has overlap with this.
                                    [Wishful thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=GSuqwuWjSYY3yCdYSuAEztWz)
                                        A common bias preyed on by this fallacy.
                            Misc
                                Unimportance
                                Naturalistic fallacy
                                    Definition
                                        Assuming that something is better because it is natural, without anywhere near sufficient justification for the assumption that natural things are more likely to be better.
                                    Nuance
                                        Some people hold beliefs that (at least in their opinion) justify the conclusion that natural things are typically better. This is not an example of the naturalistic fallacy since it is not assuming that natural things are better without any reason.
                                Appeal to force
                                    Definition
                                Weak analogy
                                    Definition
                                        An insufficient analogy.
                                        Example
                                            Galileo and freemasons
                                                Galileo was ridiculed because of his views, and these views later proved to be correct.
                                                I too am ridiculed for believing that the Pope is a reptilian alien in league with the Freemasons.
                                                Thus I too will have my day and my views will be accepted.
                                                The issue
                                                    The main issue is the "thus", since that implies that the preceding statements should substantially support the ending.
                                    Limitations
                                        It's easy to mistakenly attribute the fallacy to sufficiently strong analogies.
                                    [Analogical reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=y83CLm4PtT9N4Nk3aCZBPJq5)
                        Ambiguity
                            Definition
                                Fallacies involving switching between meanings without acknowledging that one is doing so.
                            Strawman
                                Definition
                                    A statement that misrepresents (intentionally or unintentionally) other statement(s).
                                    Memorable
                                        Prop up a weak argument that's more easy to knock down, like propping up a person made of straw.
                                Importance
                                    Common
                                        People frequently strawman each other
                                    Impactful
                                        Strawmans often cause major and quite impactful misunderstandings when people misunderstand each other.
                                Related
                                    [Steelmanning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=LWB8YQUQupE7UQRtFL7J9g6w)
                            Equivocation
                                Definition
                                    Equivocation is using multiple meanings of a word as if they were the same.
                                        Specifically multiple **non-synonymous** meanings.
                                    Example
                                        Prisoners are free
                                            Prisoners are really free. This is because if you can think whatever you want, then you are free and prisoners can certainly think whatever they want.
                                Reversed stupidity is not intelligence
                                    Definition
                                        "Reversed stupidity" here means the "opposite" of "stupidity".
                                            ["Opposite" being defined vaguely in one of several ways](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=AJBb8wh2Ihx1GcBVC84TTA8O)
                                    Very broken ≠ capable at "opposite"
                                        Definitions
                                            That something is very bad at doing one thing doesn't mean it's very good at doing the "opposite".
                                            "Opposite" being defined vaguely in one of several ways.
                                                It might mean the [inverse](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=j3H0k5NrgqqJ2__9SgtP8ngy) / [converse](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=hn5cIVc2DKo-2jmUg5S_fwSh) of the belief(s) that were stupidly derived.
                                                It might mean the logical negation of the belief(s) that were stupidly derived.
                                            Example
                                                A car with a broken engine cannot drive backward at 200 mph, even if the engine is really really broken.
                                        Related
                                            [Superintelligent stupidity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0TqLxMx-Szy_DkKna7mzLrt_)
                                    Superintelligent stupidity
                                        If you knew someone who was wrong 99.99% of the time on yes-or-no questions, you could obtain 99.99% accuracy just by reversing their answers. They would need to do all the work of obtaining good evidence entangled with reality, and processing that evidence coherently, just to __anticorrelate__ that reliably. They would have to be superintelligent to be that stupid.
                                        Related
                                            [Very broken ≠ capable at "opposite"](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=4PpU1Sktv3zM589PwF4pUwjv)
                                    Example
                                        “The world’s greatest fool may say the Sun is shining, but that doesn’t make it dark out.”
                                Associating with believers/disbelievers
                                    Negative association
                                        Definition
                                            Examples
                                                Some people may be reluctant to believe that God doesn't exist, not because there is evidence that God does exist, but rather because they are reluctant to affiliate with Richard Dawkins or those darned “strident” atheists who go around publicly saying “God does not exist.”
                                    Positive association
                                Related
                                    [Conflations](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=rkFEurnAxeGlEmMJ8RQUkGO2)
                            Cherry-picking
                            Link in induction
                                Definition
                                    Selectively reading, noting or citing the evidence in favor of your own hypothesis, or “cherry picking” the data to get the parts that support your hypothesis.
                                    **Typically:**   often ignoring things that contradict it.
                                Related
                                    [Confirmatory thought](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OrIjqF9wWhAuAxUvnk078FOy)
                                        Essentially cherry-picking what you think about, rather than what you read/note/cite.
                            Texas sharp-shooter
                            Link in induction
                                Definition
                            Composition
                                Definition
                                    The inference that something is true of the whole merely because it is true of some part of the whole.
                                    Examples
                                        Tire, vehicle, rubber
                                            This tire is made of rubber; therefore, the vehicle of which it is a part is also made of rubber.
                                                This is fallacious, because vehicles are made with a variety of parts, most of which are not made of rubber.
                                        Atoms, sets of atoms, life
                                            No atoms are alive. Therefore, nothing made of atoms is alive.
                                                This is a statement most people would consider incorrect, due to emergence, where the whole possesses properties not present in any of the parts.
                                    The [converse](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=hFk3VZ444mAV4Q2_NN6-xWr5) of [division](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Clm7YytA7OR8d3E5z9U0OuGf)
                                Nuance
                                    The fallacy of composition can apply even when a fact is true of every [proper part](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=dhDE7pPnq-_CrD9g7LqP-Wcy) of a greater entity.
                                        Example
                                            [Atoms, sets of atoms, life](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=IR9c-gXVAZT1UJQUZ14Bs4_d)
                            Division
                        Presumption
                            Definition
                                Errors in reasoning where the premises rely on [unstated and insufficiently justified] assumptions.
                            Mere assertion
                                Definition
                                    An assertion made without any evidence.
                                    Example
                                        Abortion is just wrong, and that is all there is to it.
                                Importance
                                    Impactful
                                        Mere assertions are often very confidently asserted, which can make them feel like much stronger evidence than they really are.
                                Reducible
                                    Partially reducible to faulty [heavy heuristic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                                    Partially reducible to a lack of [systematic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=XUvhNLQybh6UlJJgJCSnbDU7)
                                        If systematic reasoning was employed, it would likely be quite clear that the statement is unsupported.
                                    Partially reducible to a lack of [other-shoe reviews](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=d7AlWLW-6EAuja2PutdZEj64)
                                Slippery slope
                                    Definition
                            Appeal to ignorance
                                Definition
                                    A direct appeal to the lack of evidence in favor of something, with the intent of definitively proving that the something is false.
                                Nuance
                                    It's very easy to mistake this with the appeal to the lack of evidence __without__ the intent to definitively disprove it, particularly when they are implicitly relying on [occam's razor](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=UULVW93uPNRrRBC6RL4mTOjT) or something.
                                reducible
                                    Partially reducible to faulty [probabilistic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=i_iQ7vvMYVnf1xdY5Ymr8--m)
                            False dilemma
                                Definition
                                    A presented dilemma between an incomplete set of options, without the remaining members being sufficiently implied.
                                    Synonyms
                                        False dichotomy/trichotomy/etc.
                                        Black or white fallacy
                                Importance
                                    Impactful
                                        We are typically biased to fall for false dilemmas.
                                            [<broken link>](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=AD9hC2YbbBV8QT-gHvIFussM)
                                Reducible
                                    Partially reducible to the failure of sufficiently [accounting for unknowns](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=cXfd8G1zK1UZ_HGp2aSVkGmK)
                                <broken link>
                            Hasty generalization
                                Definition
                                    Generalizations made with pretty obviously too little justification.
                                    Synonym
                                        Law of small numbers
                                    Example
                                        All three of my ex-wives always told me what to do. This clearly shows that all women want to control us men all the time.
                                    [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faulty_generalization#Hasty_generalization)
                                    Pull a bunch from this
                                Reducible
                                    Partially reducible to faulty [probabilistic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=i_iQ7vvMYVnf1xdY5Ymr8--m)
                            False cause
                                Definition
                                    Assuming that because two events are correlated and/or related and/or one precedes the other by a relevant amount of time, that means one causes the other.
                                    Example
                                        My alarm goes off every morning before sunrise, therefore my alarm causes the sun to rise.
                                Importance
                                    Impactful
                                    Common
                                Reducible
                                    Partially reducible to faulty [heavy heuristic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                                Correlation ≠ causation
                            Secundum quid
                                Definition
                                    The fallacy of failing to recognize the difference between rules that are generally true (but still leave room for exceptions) and rules that are always true.
                                    Synonyms
                                        Since it ignores the limits/qualifications of general rules, this fallacy is also named:
                                        **Ignoring qualifications**
                                        **Sweeping generalizations**
                                        The expression **misuse of a principle** can be used as well.
                                    Examples
                                        [Accident fallacy example](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=LCZswhmPXsGOVDlE72Cn8kUC)
                                        [Converse accident fallacy example](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=_VgiDs-odwhsR9N4DYr1CXGL)
                                Importance
                                    Impactful
                                    Common
                                        Despite being lesser known than many other fallacies, this is still a very common fallacy.
                                        [Examples](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=dupzgbswSnbg0GWH1PkzG-nO)
                                Reducible
                                    Substantially reducible to faulty [heavy heuristic reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                                Accident fallacy
                                    Definition
                                        The fallacy of applying a general rule to an exceptional case (one in which some special circumstance (“accident”) makes the rule inapplicable).
                                        Synonyms
                                            **Destroying the exception**
                                            **A dicto simpliciter ad dictum secundum quid**
                                        Example
                                            Blind people
                                                The fact that “people are capable of seeing” is insufficient for the conclusion that “blind people are capable of seeing.”
                                                    In this context, "people are capable of seeing" is defined as something like "in general, people are capable of seeing", which is insufficient (in light of our evidence that blind people exist) to establish that there are no blind people.
                                    Importance
                                        Common
                                    Source
                                        https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accident_(fallacy)
                                Converse accident fallacy
                                    Definition
                                        The fallacy of applying a rule (that only applies to some exceptional cases) to all cases in general.
                                        Example
                                            1. The [Tunguska explosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tunguska_event) happened in Siberia.
                                            2. [Magnitogorsk Iron and Steel Works](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnitogorsk_Iron_and_Steel_Works) is in Siberia.
                                            3. This is a bad company to invest in because it's just going to be destroyed by a meteorite.
                                            
                                            Here, a general rule is mistakenly made (that places in Siberia are likely to be destroyed by meteors in the foreseeable future) based on one exceptional case.
                                    Importance
                                    Source
                                        https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Converse_accident
                                    Related
                                        [Slippery slope](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=q8f2vFegczgxQcWuoRciRil1)
                            [Circularity](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ikMYp91GwlvLX0gZwMfCovX_)
                            Anecdotal fallacy
                                Definition
                                    The fallacy of placing undue weight on experiences of close peers.
                                    ^ Often atypical experiences.
                                    Synonyms
                                        Argument from anecdote
                                        "person who" fallacy
                                Importance
                                    Common
                                        [Anecdotal evidence is common](https://dynalist.io/d/axyUc8EpOb4NEwUA5hpTKBU1#z=dguLUeGiyxzDZakD4UifdIAN)
                                        Likelihood
                                        [Anecdotal evidence is often unreliable](https://dynalist.io/d/axyUc8EpOb4NEwUA5hpTKBU1#z=STq7W1pdf1cJ1xEjskutHmGg)
                                        Likelihood
                                        Thus it's important to learn how to spot and avoid it.
                                        Relevance
                                Reducible
                                    In cases involving our own anecdotal evidence
                                        [Specific/"grounded" bias](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mks1gpdiHB01sMtJ2DSU-1Xn)
                                            We typically feel like experiences that have happened to us are more real than those that have happened to others, and are thus stronger evidence, even in cases where they aren't.
                                            Likelihood
                                                Eg. We sometimes don't look at it from the perspective of the person we're sharing the anecdote with, who hasn't had the experience we have and thus should weight it according to how much they trust our reliability.
                                                Likelihood
                                                Eg. We sometimes make generalizations based on our personal experiences even when our personal experiences are pretty limited and reliable statistics indicate otherwise.
                                                Likelihood
                                Common forms
                                    "I know a person who..."
                                    "I know of a case where..."
                                Related
                                    [Anecdotal evidence](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=rP6G0D6KrdxKx1tz39CYdv-T)
                                    [faulty generalization](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ecu_HuUAoK7nIZBDmynRINzl)
                                    Probably rename "hasty generalization" section (linked here) to "faulty generalization". Or else probably change this text.
                                        If an anecdote is used to justify a desired conclusion without the anecdote providing nearly as much support as intended, it is considered a faulty generalization.

                Presumptuous reasoning
                    Definition
                    Recognizing presumptuous reasoning
                        Common forms
                            Definition
                                Common forms of presumptuous reasoning.
                            [Presumption](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=L9ETyQmgiS8qHo_zlxHdFzdm)
                            [Confirmatory thought](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OrIjqF9wWhAuAxUvnk078FOy)
                        Commonly confused non-forms
                            Definition
                                Forms/types/cases of reasoning that are frequently mistakenly considered to be presumptuous.
                            Quick conclusions
                                Definition
                                    The idea that when someone comes to a quick conclusion, it is due to inadequate thinking about the subject.
                                Explanations
                                    People often have sufficient [previous thoughts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=10Zg_q4wbC_KG0o0ze6nfb2a) that they used to reach their conclusion.
                                    Some seemingly complex matters are much less complex than they seem.
                                        So it is possible to arrive at a conclusion much quicker than might otherwise be expected.
                    Preventing presumptuous reasoning
                    Addressing presumptuous reasoning
                    Related
                        [Fallacies of presumption](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=L9ETyQmgiS8qHo_zlxHdFzdm)
                Related
                    [Reasoning short-cuts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QbiCWrDMPM38d8BpMPryvG49)
            Reasoning self-improvement
                Definition
                    Literal self-improvement of reasoning. Without some of the common connotations of "self-improvement".
                Recognizing mistakes
                    Definition
                        Realizing when you're wrong due to faulty reasoning.
                    Importance
                        Common
                        Realizing when you've relied on faulty reasoning is crucial for improvement.
                        Typically increases general open mindedness
                        PC fix
                            By more often recognizing mistakes, one typically forms more/stronger associations about their own reasoning being imperfect.
                            Likelihood
                                This generally reduces epistemic self-confidence.
                                Relevance | Link epistemic self-confidence
                                    This generally increases open-mindedness.
                                    Relevance | Link item about epistemic self-confidence typically being correlated with lesser open-mindedness.
                                This generally increases self-awareness.
                                Relevance | Link self-awareness
                                    By forming habits about the imperfections of one's own reasoning, one will more often recall it when relevant and then look at their own reasoning more critically in that moment.
                                    Likelihood
                            Open mind importance
                            Relevance | Link
                            Typically increases self-awareness.
                            Relevance | Move
                                Being more open minded to counterarguments typically involves being more open-minded to the possibility of being wrong, which typically involves being more open-minded to the possibility that one's reasoning is flawed, which typically involves more self-awareness.
                                Likelihood
                                Self-awareness helps one recognize some of their other mistakes in the future, by helping them recognize how their own reasoning flaws work and impact their own reasoning.
                                Relevance
                                    Self-awareness is awareness of the self, and [how one's reasoning flaws work and impact one's own reasoning] is typically a big part of their self.
                                    Likelihood
                                Self-awareness helps increase self-awareness.
                                Relevance
                                Self-awareness helps increase general open mindedness.
                                Relevance
                            Typically increases general open mindedness.
                            Relevance | Move
                        Typically increases open mindedness in the moment
                        Fill out
                            Typically helps with immediate self-awareness.
                            Likelihood
                                
                                Relevance
                Acknowledging mistakes
                    Definition
                        Admitting that you made mistake(s).
                    Importance
                        **Recognizing degree of mistakes:**   Without acknowledgement, we are much more likely to greatly underestimate our mistakes.
                            Sometimes even to the degree of not recognizing them as mistakes at all.
                            Relevance
                        **Remembering:**   Without acknowledgement, we are much more likely to forget about our mistakes.
                            We often only have the faintest notion that they might be mistakes, before immediately forgetting via habitual thought suppression.
                            Likelihood & Relevance | Move to rationalization (or similar) and link here
                                We typically have habits to rationalize our position (especially to defend it to others), and this thought suppression is an extremely useful habit for effective rationalization.
                                Likelihood
                        **Compelling improvement:**   Without acknowledgement of mistakes, we typically feel much less compelled to improve on our biggest weaknesses.
                            We tend not to naturally feel very compelled to improve on our biggest reasoning weaknesses.
                            Likelihood
                                We typically don't recognize our biggest weaknesses as such.
                                Likelihood
                                ^ If we did naturally recognize them as such, we would have likely already improved them.
                                Likelihood
                                Therefore getting into the habit to [do things (such as acknowledge mistakes) in order to further compel ourselves] is very important for making big improvements.
                                Relevance
                            Usually, some of our biggest weaknesses are among the most important areas to improve on.
                            Relevance
                    Internal and external acknowledgement
                        Internal acknowledgement
                            Definition
                                Acknowledging one's mistake(s) to themselves.
                            Importance
                                Consciously improving from mistakes requires acknowledging them at least internally.
                                    Oftentimes there's a little feeling/intuition that something might be a mistake, but we haven't really consciously acknowledged it as such.
                                    If we don't consciously acknowledge it, the feeling/intuition will typically fade away and leave us with no way to improve.
                                [Long-term open-mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=F2BJsS6OGWLKLX02yY7kMCeM)
                                [Immediate open-mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=N2Ryo-Ll-udSEK1t6QjA_iNX)
                            Limitations
                                This typically doesn't make the mistake(s) feel as real/significant as [external acknowledgement](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=AcL_5ai3F6ZI2uJnP1Qp3lGG)
                        External acknowledgement
                            Definition
                                Acknowledging one's mistake(s) to other(s).
                            Importance
                                This makes it much harder to delude yourself about how many mistakes you actually make.
                                    This typically makes it much harder to forget about mistakes.
                                [Long-term open-mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=F2BJsS6OGWLKLX02yY7kMCeM)
                                [Immediate open-mindedness](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=N2Ryo-Ll-udSEK1t6QjA_iNX)
                    Consistency
                    Organize
                        It's often easy to acknowledge small mistakes, or even major ones (in terms of how bad the reasoning was) if the impact was relatively insignificant.
                        But it's often significantly harder for us to acknowledge (or even recognize) bigger mistakes, so learning to acknowledge those is really the main importance of acknowledging mistakes in general. Though getting into the habit of acknowledging small mistakes probably helps with acknowledging bigger ones.
                    Related
                        [(Un)conservative claims](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=clC-svjqyNajpM81R8mrQFEl)
                        [Inflammatory statements](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=cdf2M2ciEV3h0SaelXqPiXwU)
                Analyzing your own discussions
                    Definition
                    Importance
                        There ordinarily isn't a clear feedback mechanism that works well for reasoning, so analyzing your own discussions can be a very helpful process.
                    Limitations
                    Analyzing a past discussion
                        Importance
                            We typically miss a lot of our own mistakes in the midst of our discussions, looking back at them (especially with the context of the things that are said later on) can sometimes help us see where our reasoning was flawed, which is useful to learn from our mistakes and change our minds.
                            It can often be relatively stress-free, since it's after the discussion is already over. And you can do it alone so that there's no one to judge you except yourself.
                    Analyzing a current discussion
                        Limitations
                            This often doesn't give you the full picture of a lot of things that become clearer after a discussion has finished.
                            You're mind is typically pretty occupied with the discussion you're having. Trying to analyze your discussion at the same time is likely going to involve making significant mistakes in one or both.
                                An exception possibly being when responses are on a very large delay or something.
                        How to view it
                            Typically a much worse way to do it.

                    Limitations
                    Talking to yourself
                        Plausibly has benefits with remembering.
                        Plausibly has benefits with getting into character for multiple sides.
                Questioning old beliefs
                ~~Advanced, link elsewhere in practical techniques probably.
                    Definition
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            Even if you're perfect at reasoning right now, you've surely formed many fundamental beliefs while you were previously not great at reasoning. So revisiting and updating your confidence for those beliefs is very important.
                    What beliefs to look for
                        Old
                            Especially from when you were not very good at reasoning.
                        Impactful
                            Justifying some very impactful belief(s) of yours.
                    How to question them
                        Look for hidden premises
                            Systematically map out your reasoning for the belief, ideally formally, and don't settle until the conclusion deductively follows from the premises (though including induction in premise(s) and the conclusion is fine).
                        Look for unsupported premises
                            Look for weaknesses in the premises.
                            Link some stuff here.

                        Finding your own weaknesses.
                    Importance
                        Often we are not aware of many of our weaknesses, because the weaknesses we are not aware of are much more likely to remain, whereas we usually solve many that we are aware of.

                Reasoning short-cuts
                    Definition
                        Practices that are intended to skip large portions of reasoning to make the process faster.
                    Importance
                        Using the wrong short-cuts can often be disastrous for good reasoning on the topic
                        It's common to use the wrong short-cuts
                        Using the right short-cuts consistently can save a ton of time
                    Heuristics
                        Definition
                        Shorten
                            Heuristics is the process by which humans use mental shortcuts to arrive at decisions. Heuristics are simple strategies that humans, animals, organizations, and even machines use to quickly form judgments, make decisions, and find solutions to complex problems.
                            Often this involves focusing on the most relevant aspects of a problem or situation to formulate a solution.
                        Importance
                            Impactful
                                Uncertain judgments and decisions based on heuristics are often good enough to satisfy a pressing need in situations of uncertainty, where information is incomplete.
                            Common
                                Everybody relies on heuristics practically all the time.
                        Limitations
                            It's very easy to rely too heavily on or overly trust heuristics.
                            Heuristics can differ from answers given by logic and probability.
                                While heuristic processes are used to find the answers and solutions that are most likely to work or be correct, they are not always right or the most accurate.
                                    Heuristics rely on simplificatied rules that try to cut likely unimpactful nuances to more quickly come up with an approach to a problem.
                                    Sometimes impactful nuances are accidentally cut.
                        Evolutionary cause
                            To solve problems, our brains have evolved to employ cognitive heuristics— rough shortcuts that get the right answer often, but not all the time.
                        Representativeness heuristic
                            Definition
                                Our tendency to assess phenomena by how representative they seem of various categories.
                                [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representativeness_heuristic)
                                Pull more from this.
                            UNORGANIZED
                                The representativeness heuristic is used when making judgments about the probability of an event being representational in character and essence of a known prototypical event.[1] It is one of a group of heuristics (simple rules governing judgment or decision-making) proposed by psychologists Amos Tversky and Daniel Kahneman in the early 1970s as "the degree to which [an event] (i) is similar in essential characteristics to its parent population, and (ii) reflects the salient features of the process by which it is generated".[1] The representativeness heuristic works by comparing an event to a prototype or stereotype that we already have in mind. For example, if we see a person who is dressed in eccentric clothes and reading a poetry book, we might be more likely to think that they are a poet than an accountant. This is because the person's appearance and behavior are more representative of the stereotype of a poet than an accountant.
                                The representativeness heuristic can be a useful shortcut in some cases, but it can also lead to errors in judgment. For example, if we only see a small sample of people from a particular group, we might overestimate the degree to which they are representative of the entire group. Heuristics are described as "judgmental shortcuts that generally get us where we need to go – and quickly – but at the cost of occasionally sending us off course."[2] Heuristics are useful because they use effort-reduction and simplification in decision-making.[3]When people rely on representativeness to make judgments, they are likely to judge wrongly because the fact that something is more representative does not actually make it more likely.[4] The representativeness heuristic is simply described as assessing similarity of objects and organizing them based around the category prototype (e.g., like goes with like, and causes and effects should resemble each other). This heuristic is used because it is an easy computation. The problem is that people overestimate its ability to accurately predict the likelihood of an event. Thus, it can result in [neglect of relevant base rates](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=PvpKwaS4egxN1pbrRVVNRd3Z) and other cognitive biases.
                                Link to neglect of relevant base rates
                            Common resulting biases
                                Conjunction fallacy
                                Create section for it and link.
                                    **Example:**   [Tversky and Kahneman](http://faculty.econ.ucdavis.edu/faculty/nehring/teaching/econ106/readings/Extensional%20Versus%20Intuitive.pdf) found that experimental subjects considered it less likely that a strong tennis player would “lose the first set” than that he would “lose the first set but win the match.”
                                    ^  A comeback seems more __typical__ of a strong player, so we overestimate the probability of this complicated-but-sensible-sounding narrative compared to the probability of a superset (and thus more/equally likely) scenario. 
                                [Base rate fallacy](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=AqpSpZ3KQtnFLltBRtAslxPF)
                            Source
                                Much of this was pulled from https://www.readthesequences.com/Biases-An-Introduction
                        Related
                            [Heavy heuristic usage](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=waIk3BhYJ4eBaM4bzQtwem37)
                            Formal deduction rules
                            Link
                                Heuristics are essentially the informal and less reliable version of formal deductive rules.
                                ^ They are both reasoning short-cuts to derive some proposition(s) from other(s).

                How our emotions often affect our time usage
                    Social pressure
                        We tend to feel pressured to finish our reasoning in a very short amount of time in live discussions, especially when face-to-face with people. One of the biggest advantages of text conversations is that it's much more socially acceptable to take much longer to think things through properly.
                    Impatience
                        Types of impatience
                            Fatigue
                            Overwhelm
                            Frustration
                        We often start using a lot of dubious [reasoning short-cuts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QbiCWrDMPM38d8BpMPryvG49)
                        We don't perform a lot of the checks or double-checks that we would normally perform when we're at our peak. Time usage can be a decent way to measure this, as typically when we're thinking things through properly on a remotely complex topic, it takes a while for us to properly consider all the important relevant points and overall reason well*__. When we get into an emotional state where we by default stop performing a lot of the checks that are necessary for good reasoning, it will take much shorter amounts of time for us to come up with a response that we feel substantially compelled to send instead of thinking further upon.__
                            * Assuming we aren't pulling from our memories of our conclusions from when we previously did the same process on the topic or something.
                Proper time usage
                    Strategies for going slower in general
                        Reasoning alone
                            Importance
                            ^ Impactful
                            ^ It often makes it easier to take more time with one's reasoning.
                                ^ It removes social pressure to reason quickly.
                                Perhaps link sections about how unconfidence lowers social value in many cases. Probably do the same in other places in time usage as well.
                    Pausing briefly to override [system 1 thinking](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ya-MUAgoHP7_PEgC9ypSAe9I)
                         Importance:   Impactful
                        ^ Taking more time often results in our initial intuitive inclination fading enough for our more reliable reasoning processes to have a fair chance to overrule it.
                            There is a tendency for us to trust our initial intuitive inclination far too much, so this is quite important.
                            Relevance
                        As a result of how our emotions often affect our time usage, it's important to make yourself take the necessary time and think things through more than you're tempted to in cases where you know it's likely necessary, even if you're very emotionally compelled to take various [short-cuts](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QbiCWrDMPM38d8BpMPryvG49) instead.
                        Organize this a bit better.
                    Ideal time usage [in text](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=YqxhIh8cQC3UlXflwRLyqM1B)
                        Rereading
                        ^ It's often ideal to reread some key sections a few times in a text conversation.
                            There's often going to be a lot of useful relevant information that you'll otherwise forget or fail to notice/deeply understand.
                            Link to consequences of misunderstandings.

                Breaks
                    Definition (broad)
                        The temporary ceasing of progress towards some objective.
                    Importance
                        Impactful
                            Breaks help with [reducing emotions](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=j3lG3AEozd127qU6GtNPal82)
                            Breaks substantially help with thinking of new ideas/solutions.
                                Getting a fresh perspective on things often really helps with thinking of solutions that felt impossible to find before.

                        Improvable
                            It's very feasible to form better habits about taking breaks.
                    Break techniques
                        For reducing emotions
                        Often called "resetting" emotions.
                            Importance / Limitations

                                It's easy for breaks to turn into [abandonment](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=_2Sxqw7l0w28Kd3hd18oTft-).
                                    It's common for breaks to turn into abandonment.
                                    Likelihood
                                    It's easy to forget to come back.
                                    Likelihood
                                    It's easy for it to start feeling more overwhelming the more you think about how you're not making enough progress, caused by taking more breaks.
                                    Likelihood

            Theoretical reasoning
            ~~Amateur
                Definition
                Importance
                    Common
                        Thought experiments
                        Likelihood
                        Hypotheticals
                        Likelihood
                    Often more useful than it seems.
                    Important, should maybe be linked elsewhere. And/or theoretical reasoning should be linked in more places.
                        Because people often have a mistaken notion that theoretical reasoning is internal and (in itself) unverifiable, in contrast to empirical evidence.
                        Likelihood
                            Supposing that we accept axioms like those recommended in [axioms](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=HTSBFQMP49xI2F7tHDD5P1pd), we can [decompose](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=e-rx5GVRcMwUlauim9BgIzme) the theoretical reasoning and verify it through fundamental operations. This is clear via the chain if reducibility you'll find throughout the fundamental sections like [standard deduction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ENg9uidRSS0t3eQBEFX4pGmm) and [induction](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OJSQEWGwiRo2syWQyU9FbjoE)
                            Likelihood (about the "mistaken")
                            Empirical evidence is evidence only because we trust things like induction, the semi-reliability of our mental faculties, etc., and reasoning for the trusthworthiness of empirical evidence __from__ those things.
                            Likelihood (about the "mistaken")
                                Thus, the interpretation of empirical evidence is also internal and (without accounting for specifics) just as (un)verifiable.
                                Relevance
                                ^ [Our theoretical reasoning is typically unsystematic](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=xME6gm1S14EoUhLjaab5Fly0)
                                Relevance
                        Because of the [bias towards the "grounded"](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=mks1gpdiHB01sMtJ2DSU-1Xn)
                        Likelihood
                    Often the best/only method for finding a good answer
                        Many important hypotheses cannot be empirically tested but can be rationally investigated to a sufficient degree to come to a reasonable conclusion.
                        Likelihood
                    Inventions / Innovations
                        Virtually all inventions/innovations were based on a good amount of theoretical reasoning, especially the more significant ones.
                        Relevance
                            Eg. Computers
                            Likelihood
                    Easy to overlook practical obstacles
                        We often fail to properly account for unknown practical challenges.
                            <broken link>
                            Likelihood
                            [Hofstadter's law](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=zRXP26n914iccTGdJXRxB69C)
                            Likelihood
                        [It is one of the easiest areas for confirmatory reasoning to creep in [...]](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=sslHc9-gXfi27WFQHol-J5hU)
                        We are often biased to assume that the practical obstacles cannot be overcame.
                            We [are often biased](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Ddzvc06L23MljpQS9N8Fm3rS) to reject some ideas which we cannot understand the full reasoning for.
                        Related
                    Our theoretical reasoning is typically unsystematic
                        There is a general trend for applications of theoretical reasoning to be less systematic and thus less reliable.
                        Likelihood + relevance | Probably split
                        This is mainly due to our cultural propensity to perform theoretical reasoning in a more vague and unsystematic manner than how we typically deal with empirical evidence.
                        Relevance
                            Thus one should not typically heavily rely on heuristics about the reliability of theoretical reasoning vs empirical reasoning, it is better to consider the reliability of theoretical/empirical reasoning on a case-by-case basis.
                            Relevance
                    Difficult
                        It is one of the easiest areas for [confirmatory reasoning](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OrIjqF9wWhAuAxUvnk078FOy) to creep in (both for and against some theoretical reasoning), because it implicitly involves starting with a conclusion.
                            Against because: If one sees theoretical reasoning for a claim they disagree with, they will (typically subconsciously) begin with the conclusion that it is wrong.
                            ^ [confirmatory thought](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OrIjqF9wWhAuAxUvnk078FOy)
                How to view it
                    A very important but limited kind of reasoning that must be dealt with carefully, both when using and contesting it.
                    [It is one of the easiest areas for confirmatory thought to creep in [...]](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=sslHc9-gXfi27WFQHol-J5hU)

                    Cooperation
